THE RED PAGE.
KYRIELLE OF OPEN SPEECH.
Good friend of mine, you feel with me—
Your blood grows hot by sympathy
With something that I say or do ;
Then speak—l want a word from you.
Let not the silence wrap you round
While you are living over-ground.
They say that earthly years are few:
Then speak—l want a word from you.
Perhaps I pass you in the street,
And, when our eyes a moment meet,
I wonder are you wishing too:
Then speak—l want a word from you.
Are you, too, longing for a sign,
Yet fear to stretch a hand.for mine?
What other am I writing to?
Then speak—l want a word from you.
Some way our thoughts together run,
Since both lift brow toward the sun
Beneath the self-same vault of blue ;
Then speak—l want a word from you.
J. La GAT BREETON.
" Harold Grey."
“Creeve Roe 1 ’ writes regarding' that brilliant
Sydney journalist of the seventies and eighties,
“ Harold Grey ”
One day, about the end of ’Bl, I was stand-
ing in the bar of the Bulletin Hotel, Sydney,
then kept by the late Richmond Thatcher.
A tallish, slenderly-built man with close-cut
fair hair, short moustache, thin Roman nose,
and hawk-like look came in, and, nodding
lightly to Thatcher, said, “ Absinthe, Dick ! ”
Then he turned and stared at me with
large, light-blue, prominent eyes. During this
inspection he held his head slightly on one side,
bird-fashion. He had, in fact, much the air of a
large, inquisitive bird—one with, say, the body of
a crane and the head of a bald eagle. Thatcher
introduced us. “ The Pilgrim a title by which
Grey was best known in those days—gave me a
few long, cold fingers to shake, and asked me if I
would have some absinthe. It flashed on me at
once that Miirger and all the brilliant Bohemians
I had read about used to drink absinthe, and I
replied that I would have some. I had some.
That casual drink was, though I knew it not at
the time, my Bohemian baptism. After making
a few jesting remarks, which I thought very
bright and cynical, Grey left us ; his greyhound,
“Rose,” following c. c his heels.
• • • *
At that time he was employed on the staff of
The Bulletin. But before that journal started
he had made himself, fighting single-handed, a
power in Sydney. It was in the wicked old days
when the Cabbage-Tree Mob did what seemed best
in their own eyes. And what seemed best to them
then would seem pretty bad to the boldest rufflers
in these feeble times. It was then that the all-
night flourished, and painted, patchoulied
Sin sat in the private bars singing on the knees of
drunken, leering Lust, and Priapus was a god in
the land. What a harvest for the sickle of the
satirist! The satirist arrived. “The Devil in
Sydney ” was the first intimation of his presence.
This was a weekly pamphlet, written chiefly in
verse in the manner of Churchill, in which the
iniquities of the city were exposed in a fashion
that delighted all readers but those who were
chiefly concerned. It had an immediate success,
and, when it had run its course, was followed by
“ The Pilgrim,” a publication which was more in
the form of a journal. This had an even greater
sale than its predecessor, and the name of
“Harold Grey’’became a term of loathing and
terror to the cafe keepers and all who went in
unto them. For the former saw clearly that their
reign was coming to an end, and the latter also
saw it arid were maddened by the knowledge that
their tnerry nights with Silenus and Venus Pan-
demos would soon be things of the past. And it
fell outevenasthey foresaw. The cates were closed.
The authorities shut them up with a violent, vir-
tuous bang, and poor Venus Pandemos had to
gather up her liquor-stained skirts, and hide her
marigold head behind curtained windows in
private streets where her old-time patrons could
scarcely revel above a whisper lest the neighbours
should fetch the police. This was chiefly Grev’s
work, and goes to his credit in the reckoning of
his life-account. And, as said before, lie did it
single-handed. No other writer of that tune
could have helped him, even if there liad
been—which there was not- any other writer
with the courage to try. “The Pilgrim ” played
a lone hand. But he was in Maitland Gaol when
what lie had wrought for came to pass. Those
who had smarted moßt keenly under the.lush of
his satire laid a trap for him—and he was caught
in it. It is an ugly story long since forgotten.
Let it rest.
* » * *
A word or so as regards Grey’s literary style. In
his case the style was the man, beyond doubt.
His manner of writing was bis manner of think-
ing. Its lightness, brightness, delicature, accuracy
of epithet, and delightful devilry in general were
Gallic plus Grey. lie was by blood half-French,
half-Jewish. His father was an Old Bailey lawyer;
his mother a Parisian actress. The union of such
a couple might well have resulted in a Voltaire.
Theodore Emile Argles was Grey’s real name.
This gets rid of Ins genealogy. To return to bis
style. It was clear-cut, aiid yet light as frost-
work on a window-pane. And what a master of
literary-fence he was ! His rapier-thrusts were
swift and sure, and left no ragged wounds behind.
In a word, his style was infused with his spirit,
and gave forth an aroma as of absinthe. M. de
Jouy, an eccentric French literary-man of the
pre-Romantic era, filled his library with glass
flagons in the shape of books, containing exquisite
wines and liqueurs labelled “ Spirit of Montes-
quieu,” “ Spirit of Rousseau,’’&c. I have now-
and-then thought of copying his example, and
buying a book of absinthe, which I would label
“Spirit of Harold Grey;” but the fear that at
times when inspiration flowed feebly I might
empty the bottle has hitherto restrained me.
* * * *
Old Bulletin readers will not need to be told
what a sparkling and piquant sketch-writer
Harold Grey was. Those of a newer generation
may get an approximate idea of his style by glanc-
ing through Grenville Murray’s social sketches,
which should be procurable in any decent library.
When Archibald Forbes read “Hash ” he strongly
urged Grey to go to London. “ Hash ’’ was
written in an upper room of a Sydney boarding-
house on a summer morning. Grey sat at the
table with a sheet around him. I sat, similarly
attired, at the other side of the same table writ-
ing “ Sundry Show ” paragraphs. There was an
empty bottle between us, and, for the reason that
writing all night is dry work, we were both pray-
ing for the time to arrive when the hotels would
open. This may not be a respectable reminis-
cence, but it is part of the picture. Grey has been
twelve years in his grave, and yet I could, by
shutting my eyes, almost delude myself into the
belief that he is at this moment sitting opposite
me, holding his head bird-fashion, and searching
the ceiling for the inevitable word. And then,
having found the word—as he always did—l can
hear him chuckling. He was one of those rare
writers who can chuckle over their own screed,
and know that it is good. A precious gift.
• * • «
On such occasions, after having finished our
copy,” we would go out and stroll through
Macleay-street, and, as we glared through the
iron gates that barred the entrance to some of the
great houses there, Grey would speculate upon
the different ways in which their owners must
have sold themselves to the devil in order to
acquire such lordly habitations. It was great
comfort to us to reflect that, bad as we might be,
we were not criminals of this colossal kind. I
remember there was one especially fine mansion,
the proprietor of which, Grey said, had committed
the Unspeakable Sin. Neither of us knew what
the Unspeakable Sin was, any more than did
Nathaniel Hawthorne, but Grey said the man
had committed it, and that was an end of the
matter.
• • * •
Seriousness was not in Grey. The riddle of the
painful earth had no meaning for him—exoept in
so far as it might deal with the getting of coin for
the spending necessities of the moment. Yet
with all his levity there was one subject—religion
—about which he never jested, though he had no
more idea of the real meaning of Religion than a
bush-missionarv. I tried more than once to get
him to say what he truly thought about the
problem of Existence and the possible Hereafter.
But he always eluded me. He seemed to have a
sort of superstition that it was unlucky to speak
lightly of such things. And who could expect a
man who didn’t trouble himself about the day
after to-morrow to trouble about the Day after
Death? Grey’s philosophy might be summed up
(as it often was by himself) in the sentence—
“ Let’s get some coin and go fishing.”
* * * *
Fishing—or rather boating, for he was too reck-
less to make a good angler—was his favorite
amusement. He could sail a boat well, but took
too many chances. Twice we were wrecked on
the shores of the harbour, and once cast away on
Shark Island. For the country he cared little or
nothing, though he was always talking about
getting away from the city to some place where he
could “ see something green.” When he did get
away he was miserable, and couldn’t even write.
The Bush, with its weirdness and mystery, was to
him simply a dismal wilderness peopled by melan-
choly, unshaven semi-savages, who ate like ogres
and talked like crows. He was, hi fact, a born
boulevard ier.
* * * *
Though Grey made many enemies with his pen
there was no real malice in his disposition. Impish
maliciousness he had in plenty, however. But he
was serenely unconscious of giving serious olfence.
Even when he riddled with ridicule the character
of a public man, he could not understand why the
man should scowl upon him afterwards. Had the
fellows no sense of humor? Benvenuto Cellini
in his autobiography describes with much gusto
how, having met an enemy in the street, “ I
plunged my dagger in him, with great address.”
This was Grey’s mental attitude. He was an
artist, even when something of an assassin, and
was amused and delighted by the dexterity of his
own strokes. Outside of print he overflowed with
boyish spirits. He used to place bottles, labelled
with the names of his enemies—the chief of whom
on those occasions was the cashier of The Bulle-
tin, whose name appeared on a large-sized square
gin bottle which could be easily hit—on posts, and
pelt stones at them until they were smashed.
Then he would laugh—or rather chuckle, for he
had not the lungs of a laugher—till the tears
ran down his hawk-like beak. A free-handed
Islunaelite he was, too, and as ready to spend, or
lend, as to borrow. He borrowed with a
consummate ease and grace probably modelled to
some extent upon the charming style of that
Prince of Borrowers, the late Marcus Clarke, who
was, by the way, Grey’s literary godfather.
* * * •
This slight and ineffective sketch of the bright
personality known as “Harold Grey” must end
here. He said to me one day when we were talk-
ing about Kendall, who was lately dead: —“I
don’t care what they say about me when I’m gone,
as long as they don’t say ‘He was no man’s
enemy but his own.’’’ And that was precisely
the first thing they did say after his death. But,
what matter ? His spirit has long since met the
spirit of Voltaire in that part of the Elysian fields
which is railed off for the accommodation of Wits
and Humorists. Hamlet’s remark concerning
Yorick would answer well as an epitaph on Harold
Grey : —“ He was a fellow of infinite jest.”
*.
Lond. publisher Fisher Unwin (impudently
enough) advertises in Literature (4pril 2) regarding a
local rhyme-book previously noticed : “An Australian
Poetess. ‘ A Twilight Teaching, and Other Poems,’ by
Lala Fisher. Crown Bvo., cloth, 6s. net. This is the first
book of poems published by a Queensland woman, and,
although to some extent the poems are coloured with a
local colouring, there are many which treat of other
lands and other things.” Lala Fisher isn't a poetess;
and her book isn’t the first book of poems published by
a Queensland woman. Mary Hannay Foott issued in
Brisbane a-dozen-or-so-years-ago a book (“ Morna Lee
and Other Poems ”) which did not merely suggest to this
scribe the undisciplined emotion of a poodle in moonlight.
W. S. Gilbert, interviewed lately, depreciated
Sydney Grundy as “only a translator.” Lond. Era
commented, speaking of G's “overweening conceit,” and
G. sued for libel. Some sparks flew from plaintiff under
cross-examination. Q. Did you say you were going to
write no more plays?— A. I said it was no longer neces-
sary for me to be a cockshy for the herd of low-class
critics. “Brantingham Hall ” was a failure, no doubt
owing to the critics. Then counsel m vde “a palpable
* Bee y° u re ? er to criticism in one of your plays,
Rosencrantz and Guildenstern.” King Claudius says—
The acts were five. Though by five acts too long,
I wrote an Act by way of epilogue ;
An Act by which the penalty of death
Was meted out to all who sneered at it.
The play was not good, but the punishment
Of those that laughed at it was capital.
A. Those were th- words of Claudius of Denmark, and
not of myself. Q. Who composed them ? A. I did, but
I do not hold myself responsible for what King Claudius
says. And then Gilbert wriggled to admit that he had a
quarrel with D’Oyly Carje, with Horace Ledger, and with
John Hare ; and emitted a dubious ‘Well, no ; I did not
have a quarrel with Sir Arthur Sullivan.' Henry Irving
testified that Gilbert’s pride had stooped to ask for the
production of a play at the Lyceum ; and altogether Gil-
bert had a highly uncomfortable time—fruitlessly, for the
jury disagreed.

H. L.M. : Apropos the Local Dogmatist and
Essex Evans, take this from negro Paul Laurence Dun-
bar—
They please me not —these solemn songs
„ That hint of sermons covered up.
'Tis time the world should heed its wrongs ;
But in a poem let me sup,
Hot simples brewed to cure or ease
Humanity's confessed disease,
But the spirit-wine of a singing line,
Or a dew-drop in a honey cup!
Yarns from Mountstuart Duff’s recent “Notes
from a Diary.” There was a ruinor that Garibaldi was to
marry the old Duchess of Sutherland. “ Impossible 1 ”
says some ope, “he has a wife already.” “ Oh,” replies
some one else, “we will put up Gladstone to explain her
away.” An M.P. yawned during his own speech. Some
one observed, “This man is not without taste, but he
usurps our province.”
Le Gallienne’s “Omar ” has educed this anony-
mous complaint—
Boy, 1 dislike a paraphrase of Omar
Done into English second-hand from Persian ;
Roses distilled with patchouli’s aroma
Are my aversion.
Give me instead the feast once faithful drew to,
Trumpeted forth by neither Star nor herald;
That loaf of bread, that jug of wine, and you, too,
Rare old Fitz Gerald.
Eng. critic Sidney Lee, whom research for his
“ National Biography ” article has steeped to the ears in
Shakespeare-lore, definitely plumps for Lord Southamp-
ton as the “ Mr, W.H.” who begot the sonnets. Comes
to this: there’s no decisive evidence any way, but what
there is points to Southampton. Lee stresses shat not
a'l sonnets have “ genuine autobiographio significance.”
Reasonable. His mistress’ eyebrow may suggest a woe-
ful ballad ; hut the poet needn’t (often doesn't) confine
the ballad to that particular eyebrow. So Southampton
was text, not. sermon.

relied—were deliberate garblers and suppresses. Con-
sequently everybody’s estimate of Nelson, right down to
Mahan’s, must, be readjusted.

Ignotus : In “ Pantologia,” a 12-vol. cyclopaedia
by J. M. Good, F.R.S., Gregory, LL.D., Newton Bos-
worth, of Cambridge, and others (Loud., 1813), I find re
New Holland that many people doubted whether it would
be possible to subsist in such a place ; all depended on the
senior chaplain of the colony (of N.S.W.), one Sam. Mars-
den ; “Scarcely anything is too much to expect . . ,
from this excellent man ; and we doubt not that unborn
ages will revere his name." This was the Parramatta-
buried flogging parson.
Omega : Has the fact been noticed that in
Australian aboriginal dialects he is an equivalent for
she ? In referring to lubra or ohildren, the Combo says—
“ That feller he,” etc. Never she or her. Why!
Omega : There are eight or ten verses in the
“Whaler’s Rhyme ” sent by 6xB. In the eighties, on
Lachlan river side, it was very popular, and was rendered
thus—
Hurrah for the Bogan !
That's the place to shear;
That’s the place to go
To make a cheque, boys, every year.
With my —
Little white flour-bag
Looking nice and plump,
Little tea and sugar-bag,
Sitting on a stump.
Three little johnny-cakes
All nicely cooked,
Nice little cod-fish
Just freshly hooked.
“ TFhen the shearing’s over," etc.
I’ve forgotten most of the rest. Will some Lachlan
shearer supply ? >-

Jeffreys? H. B. Irving, writer of a just-published

ceeded in showing . . . that he was a sound lawyer, a
just if merciless Judge, a politician not more unscrupu-
lous or self-seeking than his competitors and colleagues
of that evil day ; and that, as regard* the particular judi-
cial acts which have blackened his reputation, they were,
apart from his manner of doing them, not marked by any
more cruel severity than the upholders of authority . . .
conceived themselves . . . bound to use against the
enemies of the established order in Church and State."
If Jeffreys, in fact, had held his tongue, his name would
have borne no particular historic odium. But he had
»uch a ferocious knack of “language” that his Mount
Rennie trials were burnt into public memory. Macaulay’s
Jeffreys, by the way, is said to be a Whig picture gained
by garbling or worse.
Eable of the capable plagiarist (in Bond. Aca-
demy) :—The stars accused the moon of plagiarism. And
they sent word to the nightingale not to commend her, say-
ing, “She deceiveth thee, and borroweth this beauty.”
“Even so,” answered the nightingale. “ Yet which ot
you will tell me that she borroweth not to advantage?”
W. E. Barnard on “ Art ”
The tree of life had grown through time untold,
And branch and leaf had each fulfilled its part ;
There came a perfect spring with sun of gold—
The tree burst forth in bloom; and that was Art.

Bards of the Backblocks.
The Violets and the Years.
[Foe The Bulletin.]
I was trudging to the schoolhouse when she gaily
ran to meet me,
All her glossy curls a-tumbling o’er the wee, ex-
cited face ;
One brown arm half-hid behind her, and the other
raised to greet me,
And I smiled and marvelled greatly at our
School-Queen’s fawnish grace.
Then she wheeled beside me, breathless, and the
big brown eyes grew grand :
“ I-have-something-nice Just-guess-it ! ” —but
my guess was most remote,
And the little maid, triumphant, showed the
hidden small brown hand,
“Just-this-little-bunch-of- violets ! May-I-pin-
them-in-your-coat ? ”
Every morn whilst flowers were blooming came
the little brown-haired maid,
Now as quiet as spring dawnings—now a scrub-
land fairy wild;
And she told me of her bright days, and the days
of dismal shade,
Till I grew to love the lassie—as a boy might
love a child.
I was eighteen then ; entrusted with the guidance
and the care
Of some thirtv other youngsters, groping on the
road of life;
And I blazed their tracks at random as I con-
jured visions fair
Of a queenly girl from Somewhere, waiting then
to be my wife.
Then I left the country school-house for a billet in
the town,
And the jealous god, Ambition, led me over
lonely ways
Till he left me near the death-gate, just a powdered
padded clown,
Stuffed with heaps of useless knowledge from
the withered heathen (Jays.
All the life seemed gone from living, and my
path stretched bleak and grey,
Measured out in annual programmes with a
dreary twelve-inch rule,
Till a bunch of fragrant violets greeted me one
sunny day,
Gift from May, my brightest scholar —and I
dreamed that day in school.
And the holidaying found me walking down the
old school-track,
Walking ’mid the scent of violets, dreaming of
the years long flown,
When a full-toned girl-voice called me from the
dead years swiftly back,
And I turned to find a brown face smiling
sweetly at my own.
But the gladness in the bright eyes faded ’neath
my wondering stare,
And I saw a half-sob struggling ’neath the
dainty shapely throat;
Then a brown hand sought her waistband, and I
heard the old voice “ There ! ”
“ There’s a little bunch of violets ! May—l—

L.M.D.
The Old School.
(On the “ back” Port-road.)
There’s a little clap-board schoolhouse on the
back Port-road :
’Tis a lonesome little spot;
In the summer, melting hot;
In the winter, freezing cold ;
Very frail, and very old.
But the gill-bird and the green-finch find a fair
and free abode
In the rnallee round the school-house on the back
Port-road!
Many merry moments fleeted on the back Port-
road,
In that little wooden school,
Where we used to play the fool—
Midnight melting into morn
When the juice of Barleycorn
Used to keep us on the warble till the early rooster
crowed,
Waking echoes in the school-house on the back
Port-road !
And the timbers of the school beside the back
Port-road
Are the same as yester-year.
But the welcome sounds of cheer
And the hand-clasp and the smile
That we knew upon a while,
They have vanished like the colors that the
Autumn sunset showed
Of an evening by the schoolhouse on the back
Port-road !
S.A.
NIL.
Only a Girl.
Poets may rave of the charms that enrapture us,
Grace of a fawn with the form of a fay,
Glances that thrill and enthrall us and capture us,
Juno-like orbs that are bright as the day ;
Truce to their trills and their vaporings amorous,
Peace to the pipes and their classical skirl,
’Way with their witches so wily and glamorous—
Give us the goddess who’s only a Girl!
Angels for Angels and Gods are for Goddesses;
Heav’n is the land for the ladies with wings ;
Earth is the home of the darlings in bodices
Picking our pockets and fooling our kings.
Beauty’s a dower that’s lavished diurnally
Down on this earth where the roses uncurl;
Virginal voices can warble supernally
Here —where the Goddess is only a Girl !
He who hath dreams of the dainty Romanciful,
Ladies bedight with their gems and their gold,
Let him fare on at his feast of the Fanciful,
Spread in the halls of the Wizards of Old ;
Lightly we’ll pardon the sluggard for slighting us,
Let him read on till his brain’s in a whirl;
Red are the lips that are sweetly inviting us—
Lips of the Goddess who’s only a Girl!
'Way with your Queens in their peerless apparel-
ling!
Room for the Lass in her simple array !
Hark to the sound of her maidenly carolling !
Mistress of hearts —she shall queen it to-day.
Give us her laugh in its musical sanity ;
Give us her smile with its ruby and pearl;
Give us her whims and her innocent vanity—
Give us the Goddess who’s only a Girl !
Brisbane.
P. LUFTIC.

SATURDAY, MAY 28, 1898.
The Axes and the Grindstones.
One of the weary Commonplaces of the
Federal campaign has been the remark that
coarse personalities and the impuiation of
personal motives should he strictly avoided.
The campaign, it was urged over and over
again, should he kept apart from the miry
tracks of ordinary politics, and conducted in
a high, pure atmosphere of its own. There
was to he a mutual agreement that all the
parties concerned were absolutely free from
all ideas of self-interest, and that though
they might sin, and scramble for billets and
perquisites, and have all the ordinary wicked-
nesses in every other capacity, yet in their
Federal or anti Federal capacity they were
pure as the new-laid snowball. This appeal
for a mutual agreement that nobody could
possibly have any axe to grind has come
most frequently from the anti-Federal camp
—in New South Wales especially—and it
was mixed up with loud shrieks that all the
other provinces were actuated by base motives
of robbery and plunder. The Bulletin
coldly and brutally infers that the anti-
Federal push made this appeal because it
was grinding the bigg s personal axe, and
had most reason to desire that the painful
subject of axes and grindstones should be
dropped altogether.
* * * •
The Bulletin has no desire to be any
more offensive than usual on the question of
the axe and the grindstone, but it. regards
this proposal that all parties should begin by
assuming the spotless purity of everybody
as the saddest kind of drivel. In every
other business, the great mass of mankind is
rushing after the loaves and fishes, after the
loot and the boodle, and the plunder ; and it
is absurd to suppose that on this one subject
it has suddenly risen to the level of the
angels. Simply because there is a lot of
human nature in man, this paper assumes
that almost every person who has an axe to
grind, and who sees that the grinding thereof
would be accelerated or retarded by Federa-
tion, is more or less influenced, consciously
or unconsciously, thereby. In Australia,
almost every importer is a Foreign-trader, and
almost every manufacturer is a Protectionist,
and almost every Land-taxer doesn’t own
land, and almost every anti-land-taxer does,
and almost every man who believes that
long pastoral leases will be the salvation of
the country is a pastoralist, and so on. It
is too absurd to suppose that this is all mere
coincidence. And when these men go into
Federalpolitics, The IT lletiN assumes that
they are much the same there as they are in
any other politics, and that their statements
should be viewed with the same kind of
suspicion accordingly.
* * * *
Taking New South Wales as an example,
that province is run by an Assembly of 125
members, and a Council of 62—-therefore it
finds more or less dignified and profitable
billets for 187 politicians. The Common-
wealth Parliament proposes to run the prin-
cipal concerns of five provinces with 94
politicians in all. If it succeeds in doing
this, the utter absurdity of maintaining the
present huge local Legislature to deal with
the small parochial concerns that are left
behind must occur to the public, and the
most probable result will be a reduction of
the membership to about one-half. That
will mean that 93 politicians will be thrown
out of their billets, and as only 31 of them
can find occupation in the Federal Legis-
lature, a good many men are likely to be
among the Men who are Left. This reduc-
tion is not a certainty, but it is such an ex-
ceedingly strong probability that it is one
excellent reason why the bulk of local poli-
ticians should be found in the anti-Federal
interest. The small dimensions of the Com-
monwealth Parliament are a bad disappoint-
ment ; it will actually only supply half the
number of billets furnished by the New
South Wales Legislature alone. Also, the
union of the five provinces means that there
will be only one Agent-General instead of
five, and one Postmaster-General instead of
five—therefore eight good billets vanish out
of sight in one act.
* * * *
In New South Wales especially Federa-
tion will turn local politics upside-down and
blot out all the old landmarks. For a good
many years New South Wales politics have
consisted of the fiscal question above all
things. Federation will shift the fiscal con-
troversy to the Commonwealth Legislature,
and then a great fall must needs ensue. All
the politicians who are in Parliament simply
because they are Protecf ionists or because
they are Foreign-traders will be no more
wanted, and the useful tariff controvei'sy has
raged so long and so desperately that these
now probably constitute three-fourths of the
Assembly. Lyne, whose one political
merit is that he is a consistent and hard-
fighting Protectionist, will be dead and
utterly superfluous, because it won’t matter
one straw in the New South Wales Provin-
cial Legislature whether a man is Protec-
tionist or not. No one will want Lyne any
more, for the one subject he knows any-
thing about will have dropped. Nor will
any one want Wilks or Neild or
the rest of the multitude of small
men who got in at last election
because they were strong on the shoddy
ticket. About half or three-fourths of
the present Assembly will have to learn
new politics, and find new reasons for
being in politics at all, for the only political
knowledge that a large section of the House
possesses will become useless when the fiscal
row is removed to new quarters. And the
four Sydney daily papers will drop the men
they have boosted up by reason of their
fiscal creeds for so many years, because
these men will be of no more use to any fiscal
creed. The New South Wales Parlia-
ment is the only one that has lived for years
on the tariff question, and the loss of its one
great bone of contention will be a heavy
blow.
* * * *
Then the New South Wales Provincial
Legislature will have to readjust i self into
Democrats and Conservatives and as it will
be no longer possible to crowd out awkward
social problems by raising the old tariff row
a lot of new questions will have to be faced.
It will be a woeful time for the old school of
politicians. Many of them don’t want to
call themselves Conservatives because it
doesn’t pay with the electors, and don’t want
to be Democrats because it is foreign to their
nature and to their private interests, but
when there is no fiscal issue to live on they
will have to be one or the other. Then the
Daily Telegraph will be able to cordially
kick Reid if he remains in provincial politics,
and the Star will be in a position to bounce
the large brick of objurgation oft'the head of
Lyne if he remains there also, because these
two men will be.of no value to their respec-
tive organs any more. They have been kept
by their respective papers for fiscal reasons,
and it will simply be a case of tiring out the
old dog who is no longer wanted. And the
Herald, which has reluctantly supported
Reid and his landaninkuintax for the sake
of Foreign-trade, will then be in a position
to joyfully cast its fat Jonah overboard, and
there will be chaos for a while, till things
get into their new shape. By that time a
good many politicians will have been sub-
merged, and quite a lot of things will have
happened.
These are a few of the unpleasant possi-
bilities of Federation, but there are others
as well. Turner’s amendment on the Con-
stitution Bill makes it certain that''neither
Sydney, Melbourne, nor Adelaide can be the
Federal capital, and the increase of popula-
tion, and the consequent increase of land-
values and rents wjhich w.ould go with the
extra population, are lost to the,landlords of
all these cities. As these cities represent
one-half the landlord interest of the three
provinces, there is naturally a very large
class which must be against any Federation
Bill till this clause is taken out of it.
Again, Turner’s amendment makes it quite
possible that the capital may be a new city,
built on an area purchased by the Common-
wealth, and where the Commonwealth will
be the sole ground-landlord, in which case the
unearned increment will be wholly lost to
the class of private land-grabbers in general.
This also means that a very large grindstone,
with a very large axe grinding upon it, has
a tendency to turn in the anti-Federal
interest.
* * * *
When the Postal Department is taken over
by the Commonwealth, one necessary result
will be the establishment of a uniform postal
rate throughout Australia. This almost cer-
tainly means the abolition of the New South
Wales free newspaper postage system, and as
under this system many scores of private
advertising sheets are carried throughout the
province solely at the expense of the tax-
payers, there are quite a number of in-
fluential traders who' have reasons, amount-
ing to several hundred pounds per annum,
for being of the Provincial persuasion. Also,
the abolition of free newspaper postage
means a serious loss even to the legitimate
newspaper press of New South Wales, which
is now largely subsidised by the State, and
protected to the extent of 50 per cent,
against the competition of outside organs of
public opinion. The fact that a large section
of the press is in favor of Federation shows
that it, at all events, has risen superior to
this monetary consideration; but another
considerable section of the press is against
the national cause, and whether this section
is in any way influenced by corrupt motives
or not, the fact remains that it is mostly
arguing on the side where its personal in-
terests lie. Another consideration is that,
whereas the Age now bosses the Vic-
torian Government and the Telegraph
largely bosses that of New South Wales, a
Federal Government, located neither in
Melbourne nor Sydney, can’t be bossed to
any great extent by any one provincial
organ. Therefore both these papers are
hollering against the cause which means
their political abdication.
* * * *
But, so far as concerns New South Wales,
the most violent opposition to the Federal
movement is located in Parliament, and it
is in Parliament that the largest personal
provincial interest lies. For the New South
Wales Assembly is far the largest one in the
Seven Provinces, therefore it is the one
which is most certain to suffer a heavy reduc-
tion in membership when its importance is
diminished by Federation. The hostility of
its members to Federation may, or may not,
be influenced by this circumstance. Also,
it is the only Parliament in the Seven
Provinces where both leaders owe their
political existence to the fiscal contro-
versy ; Reid is Premier because he is the
boss Foreign-trader, and Lyne is prospec-
tive Premier because he is the boss Protec-
tionist, and when the fiscal question is
entirely removed out of local politics the
principal reason for the existence of both
men will vanish. The fact that both of
them are hostile to the Convention Bill may
be mere accident—or again it may not. The
New South Wales Parliament is the only
one whose members also mostly owe their
political existence to the fiscal question, and
have most reason to object to its removal
out of local politics, and the fact that it is
the most provincial in spirit of all the Legis-
latures may be owing to this—or again it
may not. Also, the fact that the New South
Wales anti-Federal Party—the party with
the biggest private axe to grind—is the one
which has most strongly deprecated any
suggestion of the possibility of interested
motives in the advocates of either policy,
may be mere accident—or again it may not.
But the axe is there, anyhow, and the grind-
stone also.
“Indissoluble.”
On paper, at all events, the Federal Constitu-
tion is indissoluble. In that respect it is like
the Constitution of all the live States, and all
the dead or dissolved ones of which we have
any record ; they never contained any pro-
vision for their own dissolution, but they all
dissolved just the same, when their time came,
or will dissolve in due season. The fact that
the Federal Constitution is nominally indis-
soluble places it on exactly the same level as
the Constitutions of New South Wales, Vic-
toria, and the other provinces ; also on the same
level as our connection with Britain, and with
the other parts of the British empire, and the
connection of every part of Britain with every
other part, and every other political connection
under the sun. The exact degree of indis-
solubleness in the Federal Constitution is a
matter of some doubt, but at the worst the wild
alarm and surprise with which Want and the
other Cabbage - tree prophets of desolation
view the alleged permanence of the proposed
union is wholly uncalled-for. The very worst
that any of them can say about it is that it is
a cast-iron agreement, and if any province
enters it there is no exit formally provided on
paper. That is exactly the condition under
which every part of every Australasian
province lives nov , and always has been
living, and every other State is in the
same awful condition, and has been there since
it first existed, and never expects to be any-
where else. Yftt no one notices the horror of
it, and Heydon and Want and Haynes don’t
sympathise woi I h a cent. Neither does Higgins
or Hancock i. : Ebenezek, Wakd, or anybody
else. ’ -tr '
♦ io *
If these p: ople are actually grieved at the
allegedly . ca!>i-iron of the Federal
Constitution,‘.and View indi: solu'deness as an
evil—though their conduct n akes it difficult to
believe •anything of the kind —T ie Bulletin
asks their, to give up liyster cs a id be logical.
The pretended grievance is, that if one section
of the Commonwealth (say N;S. Wales) wanted
to leave "the Commonwealth, it couldn’t do so
without permission of the other partners and
of the British Government. Yet if Riverina
wanted to leave... N.S. W. and join Victoria, or
if the regions beyond the Darling wanted to
secede from N.S. W. and attach themselves to
S.A., they couldn’t do so without the permis-
sion of the rest of the province, and of the
British Government. The same people
who cry aloud that the indissolubleness of the
Commonwealth is a brutal tyranny and an out-
rage on human freedom defend this, system;
and any hint at an alteration is described as a
conspiracy to “ rob N.S. W. of its territory.”
* * * *
Under the provincial system, N.S.W. has
held a great part of Riverina and a great part
of the West merely by the strong hand. So
long as there are interprovincial tariffs and
purely local railway management, it would pay
these districts far better to attach themselves
to Melbourne and Adelaide respectively. Then
they would have no tariffs between them and
their nearest ports. The Riverina would get
direct railways to the market which is near at
hand, whereas the greater part of it is only
allowed to have railway communication with
the one which is a long way off. The West
would get railways to Adelaide, whereas it has
now to do without railways at all because
N.S. W. can’t afford to build the long lines to
Sydney, and it will see the West “ further” be-
fore it will build lines to connect with Adelaide
or let the S.A. Government do so. Under the
indissoluble N.S. W. Constitution, these dis-
tricts have no right to secede so long as it suits
the Sydney majority to deny that right. They
have no right to trade where they please or
belong to whatever State they please ; they
have to put up with all manner of losses and
disadvantages so that the Sydney majority
may boast of the size of N.S.W. territory and
the volume of N.S.W. trade. If Want, Hey-
don and co. really believe in the right of seces-
sion, then here is a fine chance for them to show
their sincerity.
* * * *
The right of the people to secede from their
political bargain if they don’t like it is not
what the anti-Billious crowd really aims at,
though that is what it talks about. What it
desires is—(1) that the people of N.S.W. shall
have no right to secede from the British Empire,
however much they may want to do so—the
anti-Billious crowd being, in the main, aggres-
sively “loyal”; (2) that the people of any
part or district of N.S.W. shall have no right
to secede from Sydney, however much they may
want to do so ; (3) that the Sydney majority
shall not only have the right to secede from the
Commonwealth if it feels so inclined, but also
to make all the rest of N.S.W. secede also,
however much it may object to do so. If the
word “ indissoluble ” is taken out of the
Federal Constitution, as the anti-Bill crowd
demands, then the N.S.W. majority (which is
Sydney and its surroundings) will have the
right to secede when it feels so inclined. But if
all the residents in the Riverina and the south-
western country wanted to remain in the
Commonwealth for the sake of having access
to their natural ports they would have no say
in the matter at all. The rights of man
wouldn’t count for anything whatever in such
a case, and the right of secession would be nil,
and though the indissolubleness of the Federal
Constitution is said to be a curse, the indis-
solubleness of the N.S.W. Constitution would
be held to be perfectly fair ami proper. r ihe
right of secession which the anti-Bill section
demands is not the right of the people in
general to secede, but the right of the people
of Sydney to prevent other people seceding or
to force other people to secede —as the separate
interests of Sydney may dictate.
• * • •
Wherefore The Bulletin gets by a long and
circuitous road to its text, which is this :
PERMANENT FEDERATION IS THE
ONE THING. WHICH WILL PREVENT
THE ULTIMATE DISMEMBERMENT OF
NEW SOUTH WALES ON A LARGE
SCALE. This may seem a tolerably reckless
prophecy, but there is exceedingly strong evi-
dence behind it, N.S. Wales is in the peculiar
position that nearly half its territory is nearer
to the great ports and the political centres of
other States than to its own port and political
centre. It is under the further disadvantage
that a stupendous mountain range hinders
access from almost the whole of its territory to
that port, and makes that access difficult and
expensive. Provincialism means that a great
part of N.S. Wales territory is cut off from its
natural outlets by customs barriers, and is
likely to continue that way indefinitely. It
also means that the same districts are further
cut off from their natural outlets by the refusal
of the N.S. Wales Government to provide
railway communication with „any place ex-
cept Sydney. And the inevitable end of
provincialism will be that a great part
of the Riverina will some day want to cast in
its lot with Victoria and a great part of the
West with S.A.—so as to get on the right side
of the customs barrier instead of the wrong one,
and so as to get direct communication with
the city that is near and convenient, instead of
only with the one which is distant and incon-
venient. How many years of provincialism it
would take to start the movement, and how
much longer it would take to make it success-
ful, and how the success would be achieved,
and the indissoluble N.S.W. Constitution be
broken up, are beyond present conjecture But


the wreckage of hundreds of indissoluble Con-
stitutions shows that some ink on a piece of
paper is no defence against the inexorable logic
of circumstances. And the circumstances
which can’t be shifted out of the road are a
mountain range over 3600 feet high, and the
fact that Menindie, on the Darling, is 220 miles
nearer to a S.A. port than to one in its own
province. That mountain range and these
extra 220 miles are past explaining away.
• * * *
The difference which Federation will make
is that the customs barrier disappears, there-
fore these outlying parts of N.S. W. will no
longer have to secede from their own province
so as to get trade-access to their natural out-
lets. The other difference is that the railways
will almost certainly come under Federal con-
trol in course of time, and it will be the in-
terest of the Federal Government to build its
lines so as to give the shortest road to the
nearest port, and then the other great
grievance—the refusal of all facilities to trade
except with one enormously remote city—will
also vanish. When these are removed there
will be nothing to secede for, or want to secede
for. No district would care much which capi-
tal the magistrate who tried its local drunk was
accredited from, or which Government col-
lected its income-tax and hanged its local
assassin. But if Provincialism continues the
ultimate dismemberment of N.S. W. is as in-
evitable as the ultimate dissection of Queens-
land, which is suffering from a somewhat simi-
lar disease. In S.A., Westralia, Tasmania and
Victoria (except as regards a piece of western
territory which is more convenient to Adelaide
than to Melbourne) every habitable portion of the
province is nearer to its own provincial capital
than to the capital of any other State, there-
fore these provinces (Victoria to some degree
■excepted) hang together by a natural process of
cohesion. N.S. W., how'ever, consists largely
■of territory which naturally centres round other
■State capitals, and holds that territory by main
force per medium of its “indissoluble” con-
stitution. In this respect, therefore, it has
more to gain by Federation than any of its
neighbors (though Victoria comes a good second),
and the alleged “ indissolubleness ” of the Com-
monwealth is in its favor. For, if it doesn’t
federate, the loss of the Riverina and the south-
western country seems inevitable, some day, by
mere force of geographical circumstances. And
if it does federate, and the Sydney majority
afterwards provokes secession, the same loss
seems equally inevitable.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Upside Down.
Just now, when the reliability of N.S.W.
Statistician Coghlan, regarded as a Federal
authority, is so much in dispute, the following
figures are interesting. They represent the
population of Sydney and the country districts
of N.S.W. for the last eight years, as given in
the N.S.W. Statistical Register for 1896 and
previous issues, and a perfectly new' set of
figures which appear in the Statistical Regis-
ter for 1897 :
Sydney. Country.
Old New Old New
Figures. Figures. Figures. Figures.
1890 380,040 380,040 741,820 741,820
1891 399,270 387,640 766,030 777,660
1892 411,710 391,520 785,940 806,130
1893 421,030 394,480 802,340 828,890
1894 423,600 397,530 827,850 853,920
1895 408,500 403,470 869,370 874,400
1896 410,000 410,790 887,640 886,850
1897 417,250 906,210
The first lot of figures, which were consistently
adhered to till a fortnight ago, showed that
Sydney grew rapidly during the Protectionist
period and that a great and violent downfall
took place when the new Government came in
and the new fiscal policy came in sight. The
second lot not only abolishes all the old story
about that famous decrease of population, but
it turns things right about, and represents Syd-
ney as growing slowly in the Protectionist era,
and growing rapidly again in the Foreign-trade
period that followed. To do this a lot of city
population is shifted into the country, and then
some of it is shifted back into the city. As an
incidental result of this change, the Reid
‘Government’s boast that it has done more to
.settle a large population in the country than
any previous Ministry goes broke. The new
figures show that the country population in-
creased by 29,000 in ’92, 22,000 in ’93, and
25,000 in ’94 ; then under the Reid regime it
grew by 21,000 in ’95, 12,000 in ’96, and 20,000
in ’97. Consequently the Reid Government,
which was, till a fortnight ago, the one whose
policy wrecked the city but made the country
flourish exceedingly, is now suddenly trans-
formed into a Ministry whose policy has done
more than the average to bloat the big metro-
polis ; and has retarded the growth of country
settlement. But when official statistics which
have been adhered to for years are suddenly
thrown overboard in this fashion it is very bad
for the public faith in statistics —also in statis-
ticians. And when Cog hlan shows the awful
results of Federation to N.S.W. The Bulletin
will always remember that these figures are
also liable to be turned upside-down by that
versatile authority a little later on, and will
smile the horrid smile of unbelief.
An Unwise Long Man.
The most beautifully modest proposal which
the N.S.W. anti-Billiouscrowd has yet put for-
ward was announced in Sydney Telegraph on
May 17. That organ rises to propose that after
the people have accepted the Federal Constitu-
tion—
It will be quite within the province of Parliament by a
meeting of the two Houses or by the two Houses sitting
separately, as was the case before the Convention of 1891,
to appoint delegates to a Parliamentary Convention to
reconsider and revise the Bill. If it should be necessary,
an amendment of the Federal Enabling Act might be
made for the purpose. The Bill, as redrafted, could then
be either submitted to another and final popular referen-
dum or left for the acceptance of Parliament in the form
of adopting the address to the Queen. Mr. Lyne is one of
those who think the proposal an eminently safe one. As
the Bill has been prepared, and as there is so much of it
th;it can be adopted with scarcely any alteration, it is
unnecessary, he thinks, to have a second elected Con-
vention.
Let’s see ! The people elected the Convention;
"The people will also, to an absolute certainty,
accept the Bill drafted by their chosen repre-
sentatives. Parliament has already (with the
cordial assent of the Telegraph, which howls
for majority rule) partly disfranchised the
people in the interests of Provincialism by
exacting that if there are 79,999 votes for
Federation and none against it, the 79,999
voters will lose and the nobody will win.
It is now proposed (by the daily paper
which howls for the Referendum) that
Parliament should take hold of the Bill
which the people have approved and ac-
cepted, and alter it however it darned .well
pleases, and disfranchise the whole community
in one act by not giving it a chance to pass an
opinion on the new measure, which may be
“ left for the acceptance of Parliament.” Of
course, if one province alters the Bill there is
no possibility of Federation unless the other
provinces accept its alterations, and, to get
their acceptance, the only way is to hold
another Convention. And Lyne, also appar-
ently the Telegraph, reckon that it would
be quite superfluous to give the people a voice
in the election of the new Convention. So,
presumably, it is proposed that the said Con-
vention should consist of nominees only,
and that the nominees should set aside the
people’s democratic Bill, and fix up a
little thing of their own, and proceed to
federate the provinces in their own fashion
without consulting the nation at all. Lyne, an
allegedly rational human being on two legs, is
actually quoted as approving of this gorgeous
scheme for disfranchising everybody, and he
shows no signs of repudiating the slander.
Seemingly that long wooden political dupe has
no idea of the uses to which he is being put.
When the Telegraph has an utterly outrageous
scheme to propose it says that Lyne thinks so,
or that Lyne cordially approves, wherefore
Lyne stands the obloquy. Also, he will have
to stand the inevitable chuck-out—the Tele-
graph, having no seat to lose, can’t be chucked
out. When the Telegraph is quite done with .
Lyne on the Federal question, and has effectu-
ally undermined him and blasted him with its
approval, it will doubtless boom a Foreign-
trade candidate against him at the Hume on
the old fiscal issue, and then a long sad man
will suddenly find out how many uses he has
been put to, and what a very unwise long man
he has been.

The Cost of Federation.
The committee appointed to consider how the
finances of N.S. W. will be wrecked by the
adoption of the Convention Bill has sent in its
report, and the anti-Biliious crowd bangs its
drum in a minor key, and says that its view of
the case has been “ fully endorsed.” The in-
dependent member of the committee, J. R.
French, manager of the Bank of N.S. W. (who
was appointed as chairman and referee and
bottle-holder, and as the strong man who was
to haul the Bill member off the anti-Bill mem-
ber in case they began to fight) and Dr.
MacLaurin, the nominee of the anti-Federal
party, both agree that the extra cost of Federa-
tion will be about £380,000 a year, and allowing
£45,000 for contingencies and £75,000 for extra
defence expenditure, they bring the total to
£500,000 a year. The Federal member, Bruce
Smith, reckons the estimate excessive. In any
case, both parties acknowledge that it makes no
allowance for the savings to be effected by
having only one Agent-General and Postmaster-
General and military boss instead of five of
each, and by reducing the size of the local par-
liaments, and consolidating the public debt, and
in various other ways. The very largest amount
which the anti-Federal party’s own nominee
can show as the cost of Federation is £500.000
a year —in fact, all the extra cost he shows is
£380,000 a year, the rest being the margin for
contingencies and the extra sum which the
Federal Government may, or may not, spend on
defence, and which would be quite as essential
if the provinces didn’t federate. Considering
that almost all the anti-Federal crowd has
stumped the country telling the people that
the cost of Federation would be £1,750,000 a
year, while Lyne and a few others put it as
high as £2,250,000, this is a very rude smash in
the eye for the firm of Want, Norton and co.
N.S. W.’s share of the expense is put down by
the committee at £207,000, whereas the anti-
Billious crowd has put it at from £BOO,OOO to
£1,200,000. The rest of the report embodies
the fact, which everybody knows already, that,
if the uniform customs tariff yields enough to put
the other provinces in the same position as at
present, N.S.W., when the surplus is divided,
will have a good deal more revenue than at
present. Which will enable N.S.W. to pay out
of revenue that £500,000 to £BOO,OOO a year of
ordinary expenses which have now to be put
into loan expenditure to keep the accounts
straight. The only important feature in the
report is the utter refutation of the very primi-
tive and stupid lie about Federation costing
from £1,750,000 to £2,250,000 a year, and that
has disappeared like the mosquito which is
stepped upon by the fresh elephant in the
spring. When the man whom the anti-Federa-
lists nominated to represent their view of
things on the committee can’t make the visible
cost more than £380,000 a year, and even after
adding on a big margin for accidents can’t make
it over £500,000, that useful tale must needs
go and perish quietly on a back seat.
The “Telegraph’s” Patent Convulsion.
The proposal that the alleged surplus of a
million or 1£ million per annum which N.S.W.
is expected to have under Federation should be
spent in paying for new railways and other
works, instead of the whole cost of these works
being paid out of borrowed money, is the
latest cause of provincialist cursing in the Syd-
ney Telegraph. The cursing culminated in
last Monday’s leading article, when the Tele-
graph had the following very bad fit on the
floor: —
Take the railway suggestion. Its essence is simply
this: The masses of the people would, in the form of
Customs duties on the necessaries of life, subscribe about
a million and a-quarter a year towards providing railway
accommodation for those in need of it. That is the annual
premium which the people would put upon the political
services of the wild-cat line promoter. With this much
money to supplement the earnings of the railways,
there would be no reason why the Government
should refuse auy district its line, and during
first five years in which the Customs surplus had to
be restored to the province a return of the sort of boom
days, from which we are still feeling the effects of the
disastrous reaction, might be looked for. . . . The
million and a quarter a year subsidy to New South Wales
railway construction would, therefore, come to a sudden
end without the people being relieved of one penny of
the taxes out of which it was paid. They are already
taxed to some extent for the maintenance of unprofitable
lines, but this would be quite a new situation, and one
for which all the records of political recklessness are
without a parallel. Yet we have representative men
gravely telling the taxpayer that he need not worry
about his immense Customs overcharge, as it can be put
to good use as a subsidy to the roads and railways. And
this at a time when the province has just been chastened
by the reaction from a policy of unreproductive expen-
diture upon public works.
This wonderful convulsion, therefore, boils
down into these five propositions : (1) To build
railways with borrowed money, and heap up a
gigantic debt, and be always dependent on
foreign capital, and have our country and our
soul in pawn to 8011, is all right; to build the
same lines, with our own money, and be inde-
pendent of Bull, and not to be in pawn, is a
situation “ for which all the records of political
recklessness are without a parallel.” (2) To
build our own lines with our own money, and
let the profits go into the Treasury, is to put a
large premium upon the services of the
“wild cat promoter.” (3) Because N.S.W.
has just been “ chastened by the reac-
tion from a policy of unreproductive expendi-
ture (of borrowed money) on public works ”
it should stick to the borrowed money system
which caused the reaction. (4) The expendi-
ture of 1| millions a year of the country’s own
money is to make such a huge orgy of public
works that every district is to have its railway ;
yet the expenditure, as at present, of over
millions a year of borrowed money isn’t an orgy
but a sober, prudent policy of development.
(5) Because N.S.W. had a disastrous boom
through over-borrowing therefore if the pro-
vince stopped borrowing and went in for a
policy of self-reliance there would be a return
of the boom days and of the disaster. The
paper which denounces Federation as a thrift-
less device for helping N.S. W. to pay its way
instead of adhering to the old, sound, eco-
nomical principle of owing the money and being
in debt, has invented quite a new kind of
patent fit.

L. F. Heydon, M.L.C., shouting promiscuously
in the anti-Federal interest at Glen Innes :
After five years New South Wales would get back
£600,000, instead of £1,500,000, because the power of dis-
tribution would be in the hands of the necessitous Slates.
The real object of Braddon’s blot was that Tasmania,
which was dead-broke, would get back part of the three-
quarters which should be returned to New South Wales.
With regard to the rivers question, he decried it as pre-
posterous, and added that the water that falls from
heaven on New 7 South Wales the moment it entered the
rivers no longer belongs to this colony. Referring to
railways, he said the other colonies were anxious to get
the control of them, but we as a State would still have to
pay heavy interest on their construction. If this Bill
were rejected we would have another, under which the
colony would collect its own customs, and pay a fair share
to the Federal Parliament, and keep control of the rivers
and railways. A vote of thanks to the speaker was car-
ried with enthusiasm, but the feeling of the meeting was
not tested by a vote.
Heydon’s exact knowledge of what will happen
five years hence convinces this wicked paper
that the Almighty ought to- take him into
partnership. And yet his knowledge of what
is happening now is stupidly defective. For
water does not fall from Heaven, and N.S.W.
does keep control of its own railways, and
of its own rivers, barring the Murray, which is
mostly fed from Victorian streams. And the
allegedly bankrupt isle of Tasmania has had a
handsome surplus for some years past against
N.S. W.’s large deficit, and, in propor-
tion to population it has a vastly
smaller expenditure, and is borrowing much
less, and is now spending a smaller amount
annually on unproductive works, and at the
end of ’97 it had, in proportion to population,
the less debt of the two. And, seeing that
Heydon was against all Federation that he
was against it before the Convention met at all
and was opposed to the Bill before there was
any Bill—his promise that N.S. VV., if it will
only throw away this chance, will soon get
another, sounds rather deceptive. On Hey-
don’s past record most people would think
that he is against this Bill simply because he is
against Federation altogether, and that N.S.W.
will never get any other chance if Heydon,
M. can prevent it. If that eminent Tory
is really a friend to Federation it must be the
alleged utter badness of the present scheme
that has made him so. Before this scheme was
drafted he was the bitterest kind of enemy.
4.
Another of the Sydney Telegraph’s reasons
why the Federal Bill should be rejected is this:
If one thing is more certain than another, the Federal
capital of Australia will be a show place, and neither the
legislators nor the Ministry will be housed in buildings of.
an inferior stamp to the State Legislatures and Ministers.
Neither will these buildings be the end of the expendi-
ture. There must be barracks, a Government House, a
park, fine thoroughfares, a drainage system, railway and
telegraph extensions, and other constructions, involving
millions in the aggregate, and it must all be on Federal
territory.
A great part of this burst of grief is, of course,
an argument, not against the present Federa-
tion Bill, but against any kind of Federation.
If Braddon’s alleged “Blot” was taken out
of the scheme, and the representation in the
Senate went according to population, and the
division of the Federal surplus was fixed for all
time, and the debts and railways were taken
over, and Foreign-trade made the permanent
policy of the Commonwealth, and everything
was as the Telegraph wants it to be—the ten-
dency to spend money on ornamenting the
Federal capital would be just the same. Appa-
rently the real trouble in this case is that the
capital is to be on Federal territory. Then, if
the Federal Government keeps possession of
the soil, as it will do if the Australian
democracy doesn’t waste its splendid oppor-
tunity, all these improvements will presently
make the site of the Federal city worth
many millions, and the rental will make it a
handsome profit to the Commonwealth, and
a magnificent public asset. But if it wasn’t
on Federal territory the local Fat Man, who is
strong on the Telegraph directorate, would
get the benefit of these improvements, made
with the public money, in the increased value
of his allotments. Years ago some of the Tele-
graph crowd were deeply involved in a private
enterprise which wanted to buy up the Sydney
tramways, and apparently the same crowd feels
bad at the idea of that great unbuilt show city
being the property of the Commonwealth, in-
stead of belonging to the private capitalist, as
all good things should do.

About the largest illuminated hollow turnip of
theN S. W. anli-Billious party is the «£1,000,000
of extra revenue which the province is to have,
and which, for want of any legitimate outlet,
is to lead N.S.YV. into drink, so that it will
ultimately get the horrors and hang itself at
midnight on a gum-tree. The Bulletin sug-
gested that it should be used, partly to slop the
chronic deficit, and partly to pay for the
<£600,000 or ,£BOO,OOO worth of repairs and
roads and bridges and other non-productive
works, which now go into the loan swindle, and
the balance could go in railway-building.
Minister Carruthers offers a very similar sug-
gestion in the S.M. Herald :
There are many parts of the province which have no rail-
ways at all, and so would not benefit by any reduction of
rates. But such neglected portionsof the province would
be all the sooner likely to get their railway communica-
tion if, instead of borrowing the money for railway con-
struction, we applied portion of this great surplus to
railway extension. For instance, a railway could be con-
structed to the North Coast district, and also the railway
to Broken Hill, and if constructed in this way out of
revenue there would be no heavy burden of interest to
be met. There will be no need to waste the million
surplus, which will be returned to the people in some
beneficent form or other. To use the old protectionist
gag, the money will not go out of the country. At the
same time I reiterate the opinion I have already held that
the railways and the public debts should have been taken
over so as to do away with the necessity for raising this
large surplus through the Customs. But as power to
take over the railways and the public debts exists in
the Commonwealth Bill, they are certain to be so taken
over as soon as the public mind is educated up to the
standard of national life.
There are nine highly-paid politicians in the
Reid Ministry, and Carruthers is actually the
only one capable of realising that N.S.YV.
needn’t wreck its constitution by drinking the
surplus, after all. The.national instinct to go
and knock down the cheque at the pub. was
too strong for the other eight, and the only
thing they could think of was an annual burst
on the strong, brown tanglefoot.
»
Apropos of the great Papuan land-grab, it now
transpires that an Australian syndicate ti ied
hard for several years to buy up 64,000 acres
of land in Papua, but was always blocked by
Governor M'Gregor’s flat refusal to deal.
The local steal only wanted one-fourth the area
which the British steal has got, and it asked
for none of the extra privileges. It was willing
to pay a better price ; and it proposed selecting
its land all in one lot, whereas the comprehen-
sive British fraud and snatch is allowed to
pick the best patches all over the country,
and for many years to come no one can And
a good mine or a good patch of soil without
the probability that the great Papaun grabbers
will take it and fire him out. Moreover, the
Australian looters didn’t ask for indefinite
additional purchasing rights as well as the
original grant. But old Bill M'Gregor in-
formed the Australians (who pay his salary and
expenses) that any large land-sale was contrary
to the policy of the Government; so the local
steal never came off. Apparently something
very large in the shape of argument or in-
fluence has happened since then to alter
that policy—or else the policy never meant
anything except that Australians (who pay
M'Gregor’s salary and expenses) needn’t
apply.
Old .Tack : In Manila practically the whole of
the retail trade is in the hands of the Chows,
who are also the money-lenders and trick-
financiers generally. And this despite the fact
that upon three several occasions within the
past 120 years the Chinese in Manila have been
massacred to a man. Apropos of Ah Cat in
general, every boat arriving at Melb. from
Tasmania now brings more or less Chow-. He
is, in eight cases out of ten, the same Chow-who
has been sent back a few weeks before as an
undesirable importation from Chowland direct.
The Vic. law provides that only One new Chink
to every 500 tons of vessel may be admitted to
the province. But Tasmanian law permits the
introduction of one pig-tail to every 100 tons.
So the rejected sons of Cathay simply go on to
Hobart and then slip over to Melbourne, to the
discomfiture of the Customs, as tonnage be-
comes available. The game of landing
Chows in Melbourne on, the naturalisation
papers of deceased Ail Cows and Ah Cats is
about played out now, though it used to
boom greatly. The issue of these papers
stopped about 12 years ago, and only very
elderly Chows can get through on them. Very
often a squad of Mongols (not covered by the
tonnage regulations), aged about 23 on an
average, turn up in Melbourne with papers
showing that they were naturalised in Victoria
15 years ago, and were then aged 46. And as
the law scornfully rejects them they have to go
to Hobart, and enrich the revenue of the
Raspberry Land with a £lO poll-tax apiece, and
come back in other vessels at the rate of one to
every 500 tons register. Therefore Van Dob-
son’s Isle profits, and the Chow gets the benefit
of the fresh sea air, and he reaches Lilly Buck-
stleet just the same in the end.

N.S.YV. Speaker Abbott, who was elected to
the Federal Convention without making a
single speech to further his candidature, pleads
ill-health as his reason for not making a speech
in support of the Bill now. But he writes a
half-column letter which, though not argumen-
tative, is pretty telling. His expression,
“ Electors of Australia,” is suggestive and
rousing ; and a six-line quotation from Frank-
lin on the acceptance of the U.S. Constitution
is strikingly appropriate : “ I doubt whether
any other Convention we can obtain may be
able to make a better Constitution ; for when
you assemble a number of men to have advan-
tage of their joint wisdom you inevitably
assemble with those men all their prejudices,
their passions, their errors of opinion, their
local interests, and their selfish views. ... It
astonishes me to find this system approaching
so near perfection as it does.”

The ancient restfulness of the “Sign of
the Cross ” comes back to Sydney like a pleasant
dream. Julius Knight and co. are an oasis after
the rather robust and meaty Roman of Wilson
Barrett; also Julius K. wears the brief white towel
of the old Latin over a more graceful pair of legs
than the other Roman, and the towel itself isn’t
quite so disgracefully abbreviated. The old story
of the “ Cross” may not be logical, but, anyhow,
it is pretty in sentiment and gorgeous in coloring,
and very agreeable to look upon. The long,
snowy garment of Mercia never gets soiled even
in prison, and tiie early Fathers and Daughters of
the Church don’t seem to hurry as people do now,
because, if they did, they would assuredly trip
over the tail of the toga and fall into the Tiber.
The miracle by which Mercia is saved from the
affectionate pagan who is pursuing her round a
small room with a view to matrimony is still in good
working order, and The Bulletin has a vague idea
that more rich red perspective has been put into
Nero's palace than there used to be, and that the
jewelled Caesar has fits under a more gaudy dome
that formerly. But perhaps this is a mere un-
founded impression. The only thing that is still
wanted to complete the realism of the drama is a
view of the coarse red beast with the powerful
voice which ultimately eats up Mercia, and if it
could be arranged for that animal to stalk across
the arena making a dust with his tail the effect
would be perfect. But otherwise there is nothing
to complain of ; right down to the creaking of the
rack on which the very small Christian is being
dislocated iii the next room, all the effects are
carefully studied. One thing which this paper
would like to know, however, is whether the
gorgeous Marcus isn’t dashed sorry for it all
at the last moment. He is a magnificent Roman
with unlimited wealth and jewellery and palaces,
and with all the patrician drunks of Rome to cali
and see him, and with money enough to purchase
all the Flossie in Nero’s empire. And he gives
it up to go and be eaten by the lions along
with his pale Christian girl, for the sake of
a faith that he doesn’t comprehend afier all.
When the lion (which is a very unwholesome
beast) got him down, aud left a dirty footmark on
his toga, and breathed a preliminary breath in his
eye, this paper is half inclined to'think Marcus
suddenly decided that he had been a very great
Latin fool of the early days.
• * " * *
One very clever and stately Christian is
gone out of the co. through the departure of Mario
Majeroni, and a new down trodden slave does
Berenis’s hair in place of Mrs. Morris, and a few
other changes have taken place, but as a whole the
co. is as good as ever. Caleb Porter’s Nero is the
same fascinating study of a bad Ftnperor in the
horrors as he used to be. The attractive Miss
Elliott Page and Julius Knight still call for the
cordial support of this powerful organ ; and Miss
Ferrar, as the white-robed saint of the story,
and Gaston Mervale’s Chancellor, show no sign of
deterioration. There is an added carbuncle and
an extra blush on the nose of the red and fruity
Glabrio, and that pretty and clever actress, Miss
Linda Raymond, has put some variations and im-
provements into the part of Dacia, the most
noticeable of them being the extra-warm hug she
now gives to her port-winey patrician. Also, the
little Christian, about whom this paper spoke dis-
respectfully a good while ago, still arouses the
same old ill-feeling. This paper fears that it will
never feel really good to the smallest believer, and
is really pleased when he is dislocated in the next
room.
** * *
“ The Orphan Heiress,” which is now
receiving moderate encouragement at Sydney
Lyceum, is a highly undeserving horror of the
worst description. Even the red literary horror
commands respect when it is well put together •
but when the incidents have obviously been com-
posed with a fork it doesn't. The “Heiress ” was
written by somebody who is apparently lying
low till it blows over, for his name doesn’t appear
on the programme. It is a tale of an irredeem-
ably wicked brother and sister whose methods of
trying to get the old blind relative’s fortune are
so unsatisfactory that their failure calls for
general approbation. Barring their clothes these
two persons haven’t a redeeming trait between
them. They first of all sool the blind capitalist’s
wife away by sending her a curt letter in the old
man’s handwriting telling her to get out, and
when she goes they tell the old man that she has
fled and become a notorious Mrs. Ebbsmith. Then
they take his infant daughter away at night and
ask a curate in the street to hold her for a minute,
and go away. They intercept the old gentleman’s
advertisements in which he implores his wife to
return, and spend the (id. which should have paid
for them in the worst form of dissipation. They
also hand him a loaded gun, and while lie
is breathing down the muzzle and thinking of his
lost wife and child, it naturally goes off and blows
one character out of the story. When the lost
wife turns up the brother fatally jumps his horse
on her, and, after that, he steals the lost daughter
—whom the curate has been holding for 17 years ;
and her lover is charged with burglary, and her
only girl friend is arrested through having a neck-
lace planted among her clothes, and the orphan
heiress herself is beguiled into the wild beast cage
in a circus. And, just here, this paper got such
an awful pain in the ear that it made for home,
and the end of the horror is a mystery.
* * ♦ *
Taking them all round, the co. is much
less of a shock to the nervous system than the
drama. Miss Inez Bensusan is particularly bright
and effective as the heiress who is dragged through
all these bogs of woe, and Misses Watts-Phillips,
Therese Lindahl, and Fanny Liddiard leave a
pleasant recollection behind them. Even
in the worst parts of her career of crime,
Miss Phillips can never be otherwise than
a finished and painstaking actress, and Fanny
Liddiard, in conjunction with Harry Shine,
brightens up the tragedy at times in a brisk, off-
hand fashion, with something concerning a pig’s
trotter. Diver and J. H. Nunn also do more for
the piece than it deserves, and Miss Helen Fergus
doesn’t fail any worse than the drama, in any
case. The aroma of irrelevant drink which Harry
Shine sheds on the play, and the pork trotter
afore-mentioned, were the only things, up to the
time this paper left, which were not unmitigated
ghoul and gloom, and the writer has sent out his
harrowed constitution to be soled and heeled.
** * *
The last night of the Cartwright season at
Sydney Criterion is fixed for >rd June. According
to present arrangements, to-night (Thursday) and
h riday will be devoted to a brief revival of the
“ Marriage of Convenience.” A three nights’
season of “ A Squire of Dames ” starts on Satur-
day, and then after three nights more of the
“Idler” the co. will celebrate its ill-luck by
closing on a Friday, and will bestow its well-
deserved curse on an unappreciative city, and
depart. Barring the regrettable “ Moths,” every
piece which the Cartwright co. has produced was
well-deserving of support, and that solitary excep-
tion was only one insect anyhow, and not a very
large one at that. The absence of Miss Edith
Morley certainly weakened the co., but even
before that charming actress met with her acci-
dent, and either did or did not knock out one of
her pearly teeth on the public road (reports on
this point are varied), things were not brilliant.
Probably her return to the stage this week will find
them still in that condition. Cartwright as Cyrus,
the potter, in “The Middleman,” was great and
magnificent without any noticeable effect on the
business. And, considering that the other mem-
bers of the company didn’t call for immediate
decapitation or any other sign of serious disap-
proval, the ill-luck of the Criterion season is
hereby set down to the unaccountable absence of
the public, and the fact that the Firm is now the
only institution in Sydney which retains the pre-
scriptive right to charge ss. for admission.
** * *
At Sydney Royal, the “ Life of Pleasure ”
is going as strongly as it did at the beginning.
Bland Holt can certainly keep a melodrama
running to profitable business longer than any
other manager in Australia, but all the same he
has resolved to change the bill on Saturday.
“ Straight from the Heart ” is to be the next
production. Last week the horse, alluded to as a
cause of alarm in a previous issue, fell on Bland
in Burmah—it somehow dropped off the perpen-
dicular mountain right into the middle of the cam-
paign—but the cheerful star got it and himself up
again, and the two departed in search of help as
vigorously as ever.
*# * *
The new stars whose coming was lately fore-
shadowed have now arrived at Sydney Tivoli,
where much young bright female limb and drapery
are offered to the public, as usual, at a moderate
tariff. A number of small temporary shows also
announced themselves for the royal birthday, but
as they will mostly be dead and gone before this
paper appears they call for no special notice.
** * *
The general grovelsomeness of the Sydney
daily press in its dealings with mummers must be
held accountable for Wilson Barrett and co.’s
attack upon Nugent Robertson. They were given
to understand that Sydney editors would not
permit their “Hamlet” show to be “ insulted,”
and this promise kept them quiet when one Touch-
stone wrote as follows to Melb. Age, two months
previous to the A.N.R. episode : “ All intelligent
lovers of Shakespeare should feel grateful to you
for the intelligent and straightforward criticism
of Hamlet—according to Wilson Barrett-which
appears in your issue of this morning. Dramatic
art is in a bad way, and dramatic taste is at a low
ebb, when such a fustian performance of the noblest
play ever written is tolerated, applauded, and in
some quarters praisefully written about.”
** * *
Wilson Barrett and co. returned to Melb.
Princess’s on Saturday, and started a quick-change
season with “ The Manxman,” the reception of the
spicy melodrama, likewise public faith in methodi-
cal, unthrilling W. 8., being less apparently warm
than of yore. All the same, the house was nearly
full everywhere, and will probably get more or
less crowded throughout the second chapter of
Barrett. Wherefore The Bulletin exhorts that
tumultuous tragedian to refrain from writhing
under the whips and scorns of criticism, and rest
content with the boodle which droppeth into the
till like dew from Heaven : for the Lord loveth
the cheerful guyver, and venomous epistles to
editors are but the sign of the cross. On the
other hand, The Bulletin advises Barrett to
yell forth “ How dare you, you blackguard ? ” if he
has learned from experience that such indignation
serves a business purpose at the tail end of the
season. The great professional object of acting
is Cash, even as the trade of “right-thinking ’
criticism is Truth, and as long as a full chested
Hamlet can get more money for acts than the
other fellow can tret for facts he is undoubtedly
doing well according to his lights. May Mr.
Barrett never weary of well-doing is all the harm
the present scurrilous writer wishes him. To-night
(Thursday) they play “ Ben-my-Chree ” at the
Princess’s, on Friday “Virginias,” with disput-
able “Gimlet” and “Silver King” to follow.
Next week the approaching restoration of Miss
Pattie Browne to her native stage will be fully and
feelingly mentioned. Miss Pattie follows the
“Man of Wrath.”
