“ Dr. Bill, at the Bijou, on Saturday,
produced further proof that the cosy little house
is a good enough pitch for any 3, 2 and 1 show of
the popular order. A full-sized multitude—
whereof the d.c. portion was requested to remove
its female hats, because Madden, Administrator of
the Gov., had consented to bring himself and
family on the nod—came to sample “ llr. Bill.”
For a time the first-night performance looked pro-
mising. Miss Myra Kemble put in some
bright, artistic work, also George Melville and
one or two other members of a mixed
co. made vigorous efforts to keep the weaker
vessels in front of the wind. But in Act 3
the leading lady’s pace slackened down to a stand-
still, and the joke went all to pieces, and the large
congregation tiled gloomily down the steps as
though it had been consulting “Dr. Bill w re
pains inside, and was going home for a poultice.
A farcical comedy which fails utterly in the third
act is too sad a circumstance for detailed reflec-
tions on the chief offenders.
** * *
The Poluski Bros., who make a first
appearance here on Saturday, give a distinctly
new turn to variety proceedings at Melb. Opera
House. Two live Poluskis, supported by three
dummy figures, perpetrate a shriekful military
sketch in which honors are divided. One Poluski
impersonates “an officer and a gentleman ” much
more convincingly than the average young mum-
mer can play the same character in Bland-Holtian
drama, whilst the other Poluski fools around.
Individually, the brethren are acrobats with india-
rubber spines, and jointly they amount to ah
eccentric comedy, or an original humorous effect,
or something that hasn’t found a name for itself.
Anyhow, the new turn is a large addition to the
bdl, and with the Kellino Family still going
strong, things at the Opera House are all right,
and a little bit over.
** * *
The criminal law which shows such mar-
vellous energy in flaying lunatics and in gaoling
men who wash themselves or play cricket on Sun-
day, has never found time to frame a punishment
for the yahoo who yells “Fire!” in a crowded
theatre and incites a thousand or two people to
stampede and kill the weaker of their number.
Last Saturday night an alarm of this sort was
given at Melb. Opera House by a detected pick-
pocket who desired to distract attention and
cover his escape. The ruse partially succeeded,
but when a young man was subsequently caught
on suspicion of being the offender, the police could
find nothing more serious on the Statute-book to
enter against his name than “ insulting behavior,”
for which the maximum punishment is .£5 fine.
Massacre at reduced prices with a vengeance.
Even the big slaughters of the King of Dahomey
cost more than this in wear-and-tear on the
knives.
** * *
“ Two Little Vagabonds ” prospers at
Melb. Royal, but the run is limited to a fortnight,
and the korf-stricken Wally, who wanted to be a
blacks mi if, will expire for the last time on Friday.
Wally’s decease, by the way, is not fully ex-
plained. Everybody knows that the pallid youth
must pass away before the curtain falls, for
no drama has been known to miss a fair chance
of turning the corpse-light noon an interesting
consumptive, and yet the passing of Wally leaves
the audience doubtful as to whether he died of the
assassin’s knife or jemmy, or fell a victim to mere
shock on the lungs. Did one of the comic villains
administer a fatal wound, and, if so, why does the
trouble end without any reference to a hanging in
store for that humorous miscreant? Perhaps the
least suggestion of Oily Deering and his game leg
falling through the scaffold would demoralise
Wally’s death scene. The pathos wouldn’t be
strong euough to stand it.
** * *
George S. Titheradge—more power to
him !—has undertaken to postpone his last appear-
ance in Melbourne for another month, and when
Chas. Holloway’s co. resumes operations on Satur-
day, at the Royal, it will have Tith. in a lead-
ing part. The drama prepared for the occasion is
“ Shall We Forgive Her?” If the heroine there-
of is anyway accountable for Titheradge’s latest
engagement we do forgive her, and hope she will
do it again.

i Next Saturday’s “ Pop ” concert at the

thusfar. Philip Newbury will give them “ Cujus

Walter Barker now harps back to his brilliant
form of long ago. Madame Spada, too, is singing
well again, and the latest Biographe view trans-
ports the spirit of every good Cockney back to
Hyde Park Corner.

How 100 Halfpennies Make 16s. 8d.
About the boss anti-Billious yarn and misrepre-
sentation which Sydney Daily Telegraph has
perpetrated up to date was its tale about the
alleged Victorian coal bounties in last Tuesday’s
leader column. The. arithmetic is especially
beautiful, and was evidently done by the office-
boy’s younger brother: —
The Secretary to the Victorian Premier says: “The
payment by the Treasury of pi. per ton per mile on all
Victorian coal carried for the public is the only bounty
granted by the Government, so far as this Government is
concerned.’’ That alone would be a very' considerable
bounty'. But the Railway Commissioners also make a
reduction of id. per ton per mile for all such coal. Thus,
Victorian coal is carried on Victorian railways, and will
continue to be so carried if the Convention Bill is
accepted, at £d. per ton per mile less than that sent from
this province, which is a queer conception of the fair-
dealing between State and State, and the complete
removal of trade barriers, which the Federal contract pre-
supposes and professes to assure.
Tiie coal-mining district of Gippsland is, roughly speak-
ing, 75 miles from Melbourne. On thisdistance thecolliery
proprietors receive, in reduction of freight paid for at
public cost, a bonus of at least Us. per ton for ail the coal
sent. Taking Ballarat and Bendigo, each great centres
of Victorian coal consumption, as about lot) miles from
Melbourne, the suppliers from the Gippsland coal pits
receive a bonus of £1 per ton. . . . It is the merest bum-
bug to say, as has been said here by some advocates of
Federation at any price, that there is no bounty on Vic-
torian coal ; that under present conditions that product
is not protected against that of our coal-fields. . . . The
coal-mine owners of Newcastle or Bulli or I.ithgow cbuld
not be more effectively' legislated against if the Victorian
Government imposed a protective duty against their coal
equalling the difference in freight charged on tlje Vic-
torian lines. It is only by means of coddling in this
fashion that the inferior coal of Gippsland has becoiiie a
competitor with the high-class product of our own urines
in the Victorian market. It is only at the expense of the
Victorian public that our output has been diminished so
seriously. . . . The N.S. Wales coal producer must go
on paying 16s. Sd. per ton more to have coal carried from
shipboard at Melbourne to Ballarat or Bendigo the
Victorian.
In the first place, the awful fool who wrote this,
and the wfid-eyed person who inserted it, say that
per mile on 75 miles from Gippsland to Mel-
bourne is 35.; then they say that |d.—the same
id. —per mile for the 100 miles from Melbourne to
Ballarat is 16s. Bd., whereas. 100 half pennies are
really only 4s. 2d. Later on, they again Repeat
the 10s. Bd. fiction. As a matter of fact, there is
no id. in the question. When the Vic. railways
carry coal at |d. per ton per mile below what the
management reckons a fair thing, and the Treasury
pays the same £d. instead of the consumer, then
the concession is only —The Telegraph’s
theory is that if Jones hasn’t got |d. about him,
and Smith pays it for him, the which Smith
pays and the one Jones doesn’t pay make id. —
which is something quite new. A hundred far-
things are only 2s. Id., instead of 16s. Bd. Next
point is that N.S.W. railways carry coal at the
same low rate as the Victorian lines, only the loss,
it any, is charged to the railways instead of the
Treasury, so the coddling is the same in each pro-
vince. Further, the concession of |d. per ton—-not
the Telegraph’s alleged £d., 100 of which make
16s. Bd. will not be continued if the Convention Bill
is carried, unless with the permission of the Com-
monwealth Parliament, for it is only a temporary
allowance made for two years. Also, the N.S.W.
coal output hasn’t diminished seriously—in fact,
it shows a steady increase instead of a diminution,
and the 2,696,623 tons exported in 1897 was the
very largest export in the N.S.W. record, an in-
crease of 222.000 tons on the quantity for 1896,
and over 500,000 tons more than was exported in

J udge Adams, of Tasmania, the smallest
Judge in Australasia, measured in more ways than
one, shortly retires on a il6oo per year pension.
Arrived in V.D.L. in 1856, was Solicitor-General
from ’67 to ’B7, and Judge since that year. Is now
67 years of age, and looks about 48. Is chiefly
remarkable for the persistency with which? lie
“concurs” in the judgtnent of his colleagues in
Full Bench cases. Is locally known as “ Cocky ”
Adams, probably from the natty little belltop-
pered perkiness with which he toddles about the
streets. Has a brother a. parson, also in Tasmania.
This latter gentleman married a granddaughter of
Robert Southey, the poet, and begat some literary
talent in the person of a daughter or two. Though
thus related to a poet, Judge Adams’ >vorst friends
cannot accuse him of ever having presumed bn the
connection by perpetrating verse, though rumor
has it that he oiice made a harmless' little pun, at
which, the Court, as in duty bound, laughed
moderately. The pun is forgotten now, and, 1 but
for his pension. Judge Adams would soon be'for-
gotten, too. Mr. Adams will be succeeded by a
very different kind of man, A. Ihglis-Glark,
ex-Attorney-General, a brainy lawyer, good advo-
cate, and enthusiastic Federationist, who was a
notable figure in former Conventions, but, having
unfortunately been compelled to take a health-
trip to America, was unable to stand for election
a,S a delegate. A humane and clear-sighted Radi-
cal like Mr. Inglis-Olai k will be a valuable acces-
sion to the Australasian Bench. •*

The total amount advanced on mortgage by the
Maoriland State Land Bank up to 31st March, 1898, was
£1,112,775. And the oft-prophesied breakdown of the
system still shows no sign of coming along.
V V * *
The Bank of New South Wales, in its balance-
sheet for the half-year ended 31st March, 1898, shows a
net profit of £88,128, which is equal to £5 11s. lOd. per
cent, per annum on the shareholders’ funds. A 9 per
cent, dividend absorbs £87,750, and the remaining £378
is carried forward. The profits of the B.N.S.W. keep
remarkably steady, some recent results being as follows :
March, 1894 .. £78,451 | Sept., 1896 .. £87,601
Sept., 1894 .. 78,614 March, 1897 .. 87,898
March, 1895 .. 81,714 I Sept., 1897 .. 89,462
Sept., 1895 .. 84,737 j March, 1898 .. 88,128
March, 1896 .. 86,460 |
In the great boom days of 1888, when most of the banks
showed about the 1 biggest earnings on record, • the
B.N.S.W. had a year’s profit of £240,816. It now averages
about £176,000, so it has stood the commercial downfall
better than the great majority of its contemporaries.
The Bank of New South Wales has now £1,950,000
paid-up capital, £l,2oo’ v ooo of reserve, the largest deposit
business in the Seven Provinces, £5,288,840 of cash on
hand, and £6,627,771 reckoning cash and liquid securities
—which is the biggest reserve in Australasia. Said re-
serve equals over 30 per cent, of the entire liabilities to
the public/. Altogether the B.N.S.W.’s report is a
splendid one, and if there are any weak points in it they
are not visible to the naked eye.

The Bank of North Queensland notifies that
another writing-down of the assets will probably be
necessary. There seems to be no end to the weary
writing-down process which started in ’93 and is still in
good health and condition in ’93. When the great breakage
took place the B.N.Q. wrote off £50,000 of capital. In ’95
the reserve-fund (13000) and £7OOO of accumulated profit
were w-ritten off. It is now proposed to write off another
£IOO,OOO of capital, which wilj leave only £IOO,OOO of the
original £250,000. The Bank of North Queensland trouble
is the same one which the Commercial of Australia, Royal
of Melbourne, Colonial, A.J.S., Queensland National, and
E.S. and A. had to face; they all thought the drop in
values had ended with the 1893 slump, and that, when
they provided for losses then, the trouble was over. But
the slump kept on continuously, and though things now
look, in some quarters, like being on the turn, it isn’t cer-
tain even yet that it is all over.
Even with its capital down to illoo,ooo the
North Queensland should be a safe enough institution for
depositors. Its total liabilities to the public at 31st
December, 1897, were £300,090, so there would have to he
an enormous further downfall in values before there was
any chance of loss to outside, creditors. Even the Bank
of New South Wales, whidh has 8) millions of share-
holders’ funds against 22 millions of liabilities to the pub-
lic, hasn’t so large a margin of safety. One principal
cause of the smallness of profits hitherto has been that
the business is hardly large enough to be worth running.
Last half-year the B.N.Q. only realised at the rate of
£1 11s. 2d. per cent, per annum on capital. When capital
is written down as proposed from £200,000 to £IOO,OOO
the earnings, even if they don’t improve, will equal
£3 2s. 4d. per cent, per annum, and as'there are seven
Australasian banks earning even less than that the figure
is perhaps a sign that the B.N.Q. has now got
fairly abreast of the depreciation. But when
the proposed writing-off is effected the North
Queensland had better embody in its constitution
a solemn league and covenant that one-fourth of its pro-
fits are to go to reserve-fund till the said fund equal's
£150,000, and that at least half, if not all of, the reserve
he invested in State securities, so that the hank will know,
where to find it when wanted. Even in these times, when
a lot of Australian banks are getting down to very small
dimensions, a concern with £IOO,OOO capital and'no re-
serves is somewhat ridiculous. In the circular issued by
the board it is stated that the B.N.Q. should he able to
pay moderate dividends (it hasn’t paid any dividend,
moderate or-otherwise, since the half-year ended 31st
December, 1892) on its reduced capital. If, by moderate
dividends, the board means 2J per cent., it may be justifi-
able, provided things get no worse; judging from last
year’s figures, anything over that would lie utterly reck-
less and up justifiable. And last six months’ figures, taken
separately, would suggest 2 per cent, as the outside limit.
The four banks which showed the smallest earn-
ings last half-year in proportion to the shareholders’ funds
were the A.. 1.5. (ss. Sd. per cent, per annum), Colonial of
Melbourne (17s. Id. percent.), E.S. and A; (tl 2s. 4d. per
cent.), National of Melbourne (tl 10s. 6(1. per cent.) and
Bank of North Queensland (£1 Us. 2d. per cent.) Of
these the A..J.S. is a deep and awful mystery. But the
Colonial, E.S. and A., and North Queensland all propose
to write down capital heavily so as to make their earnings
hear a more reasonable proportion to the alleged funds.
And last half-year's figures of the National, which showed
a very serious fall in the profits as compared with the
returns at. 30th September, 1897, seem to suggest that it
may possibly have a similar difficulty to face, though no
hint of such a thing has yet reached the public.
* * * *
The Bank of Adelaide shows a profit of £33,401
for the year ended 28t,h March, 1898. The last seven years
profits are as follows :
1892.. ..£31,116 1896 .. ..£31,225
1893 .. .. 30,976 1897 .. .. 31,017
1894 .. .. 30,703 1893 .. .. 33,401
1895 .. .. 31,019
The profits are equal to t 6 Os. 4d. per cent, per annum on
the shareholders’ funds (capital and reserve), therefore the
one surviving S.A. bank shows much better returns than
the average Australian bank of these times. It contrived
to keep so far outside the whirlpool of the boom that since
the ’93 disaster it has actually been a better paying con-
cern than it was in the grand old days of ’B9. The usual
7 per cent, dividend absorbs £28,000; £SOOO is added to
reserve, making it £160,000; and the other £4Ol, with
previous balance, leaves £15,962 at credit of profit and
loss. The Bank of Adelaide has a sound little business,
and its reserve of £806,000 in cash and State securities
equals nearly 36 per cent, of all liabilities to the public.
It has also the distinction of being the only bank in the
Seven Provinces which pays a larger dividend now than it
did in ’B9. Two others, the National of Maoriland and
the West Australian Bank, pay at the same rate as they
did then. The rest have dropped—some of them with a
small drop, the majority with a very large one.
* * * ' •
The Victorian Insurance Institute met last
week, and the President took occasion to point out that
“ the agitation in favor of State fire insurance ” was a
fool of a thing, anyhow. He also remarked that “ the
Flinders-street conflagration was a lesson to all, old or
young, who were students of sound principles of fire in-
surance, as well as to the advocates of what he might,
without offence, call the chaotic schemes which had been
hitherto presented to them from time to time under the
name of State insurance. With respect to Government
management, it might or might not be efficient, but all
experienee pointed to its expensiveness. They had seen
over and over again that a business which was successful
in the hands of a private firm came to ruin when con-
trolled by a board of directors. The reason was not far
to seek, and as between a joint stock company and the
State, the probabilities of economical management
could hardly be said to be on the side of the
latter. The enormous interests at stake, the
amount of capital required to conserve those interests.”
&c., &c. Yet, when one considers that the only State
Life Insurance Office in these provinces is chiefly notable
for the fact that its expenses are about one-fourth less
than those of private offices of its own size ; and that the
State-managed Bank of New Zealand shows a bigger re-
turn on capital than 14 of the privately-managed banks,
despite the fact that there is an appalling burden
of liabilities left behind by the old private manage-
ment to work off; and that the Q.N.B. first showed
signs of improvement when the State began to par-
tially superintend the dreadful affair, the above
President seems to be a man of considerable powers of
assertion. Also, when allowance is made for the fact that
the countries where railways are most profitable are those
where they are under State control, and the economy and
safety of the State Savings Banks are compared with the
results of the private savings institutions which went
down smash by the dozen a few years ago, the President
seems even more remarkable than he first appeared. Any-
how, the prompt disappearance of four Australian fire
insurance co.’s immediately after (and mostly as the result
of) the same Melbourne fire, which, it is alleged, showed
the great superiority of the hard-up private enterprise
insurance system over one with the State’s resources
behind it, is a rather awkward episode.
* * * •
The first 50 tons of Victorian beet-sugar from
Maffra were sold at a good price in Melbourne last week.
The quality is described by experts as superior to the
Queensland and Mauritius cane product, so if this stan-
dard can be kept up the new industry should take root.
A great deal of the row about bounties has centred round
the Vic. sugar industry, but as the Government merely
lends money to the sugar co’s at a profitable rate of in-
terest, and takes a mortgage over everything as security,
the aid given hardly comes under the bounty provisions.
Or, if it does, then the Vic. State Land Bank and the
N.S.W. State-guaranteed, or partially-guaranteed. Savings
Bank, which make loans to agriculturists in the same
fashion, also come under the bounty clause of the Con-
vention Bill.
* * * *
Melbourne wholesale dairy-produce dealers are
now at their wits’-end how to fill orders. In butter most
of their suburban travellers have been knocked off and
are resting—no stock procurable. For the Westralian
trade N.S.W. butter bought in Adelaide has to meet the
demand as far as it can. As to the South African trade,
orders are cabled to London from Melbourne. And pre-
sent prospects indicate an even worse state of affairs.
• • * »
Melbourne sewerage scheme is wiping out
suburban rookeries wholesale. In South Melbourne alone
over 400 shanties (boom-time jerryites) have recently
been removed —the owners unwilling to pay sewerage-
rates.
* .. *
Vic.: “It transpires that some house-agents
‘ring the changes ’ by keeping their tenants on hand, like
the subjects of an imitation mesmerist, or the bidders at
a mock auction-room. Commission is whacked, the agent
always receiving the first week’s rent. Then the bogus
tenant, shifting to another of the agent’s houses, lets in all
the tradesmen at the last place. I have just seen a sheaf
of such bills.”
* * • *
When the war began, Victorian importers of
kerosene promptly combined to put up the price 30 to 50
per cent. There was no reason to suppose that the
Spaniards were strong enough to blockade American
ports and stop the supply of the illuminant, and the in-
creased marine-insurance rate only amounted to balf-a-
fraction, so the grab had no justification whatever.
Kerosene is free of duty in Victoria, and the incident
shows casually that the Freetrade importer is quite as
good at working rings and combinations as the protected
manufacturer can possibly be.
* * * *
Apropos alleged conspiracy. It looks very much
as though the prosecution of a big manufacturer will fall
through for want of actual proof of conspiracy as legally
defined.
* * » *
The success which undoubtedly attends that
bogus-cheque firm is largely due to the fact that the police
are dubious about the result of prosecuting either the
makers or endorsers (the latter always pass the cheques),
as it would be necessary to prove conspiracy between the
drawers, passers, and endorsers before a conviction could
be got. Conspiracy is always hard to prove.
* * » «
The attachment of the resumed-area halves of
Western (N.S.W.) squattages to the leaseholds under the
’95 Act is going to give all sorts of trouble. The latest
point to arise is whether a selector who puts up a fence
can claim half-value, seeing that the land may be resumed
by the Minister on six months’ notice. The squatters
submit that no claim will lie, because they have not the
five years title which a holder must have before be is
liable to pay for improvements on his boundary. If this
is so, then in many cases attachment will impede instead
of expedite the improvement of the country.
* * * »
When a bank insisted on retaining all the bond
certificates of a certain firm “ the trade ” generally sympa-
thised with the firm and decried the bank’s shortsighted-
ness in incurring heavy storage charges, which could only
increase the firm's liability to the bank’s detriment. It
now turns out that the enormous rise in the price of the
stored commodities has not only enabled the firm to
command the market, but reduce the bank overdraft by
between £15,000 and £IB,OOO.

What last season’s South Australian wheat
harvest failure was like may be judged by the fact tliat,
whereas in 1896 Port Pirie exported 1,509,053 bushels of
wheat and 10,585 tons flour, in ’97 no wheat was sent out
and only 868 tons flour.

Tarrawingee co’s line, being useless now that no
more flux is required at Broken Hill, all the smelting
being done at the seaboard, was about to be pulled up
when the pastoralists induced the Govt, to consider an
offer. The cost was over £90,000 ; price asked, £15,000
and the co. would come down more than a thousand.
But the traffic would be very small: about all that can be
said in favor of taking the line over is that the existence
of 50 miles of line through only partly-prospected mineral
country may help on development.
* * * *
B.H. Prop, has now 11 smelting furnaces run-
ning at Pt. Pirie, which becomes the second town of the
S. A. province. The co. is advertising—in Europe only—
for an assistant manager to succeed W. J. Kohler, who
leaves within a month to superintend the Westralia
Smelting co’s works at Fremantle.
* * * *
Block 10, like almost every other Broken Hill
mine, is finding an unexpected difficulty in the increasing
hardness of its ore. During the co.’s last half-year, this
not only disappointingly reduced the quantity' of ore
treated at the mill, but it increased costs everywhere ;
and, instead of the promised shilling per month div., barely
enough was made to pay 4s. during the half-year ; allow-
ing for depreciation, in fact, only 3/6 could have been paid.
But the directors drew on the co’s resources and paid 55.;
and, as a sale of 100,000 tons of “ zinc tailings ” has been
made for £25,000, to be paid inside 10 months, it’s probably
all right.
* • * *
B.H. Block 14 has fallen on hard times, too—
harder times, at any rate. The profit for the half-year
has reached £14,150, according to the balance-sheet ;
but as £3381 of this has been paid for mine develop-
ment and £9813 for machinery additions necessary to
keep operations going profitably, it is very doubtful
whether the £14,000 odd ought to be regarded as profit.
The hard solid facts that can’t be dodged are that only
1 5000 has been paid in divs., and that liquid assets have
decreased by £3909. Block 14 apparently “ finances ”
considerably. This half-year it owes the Bank of
Adelaide £8352, and other creditors £25,793 ; previous
half, £5878 and £17,970 ; and for the half preceding that,
£lß7l and £19,779. It may pay to buy stores (timber,
&c.) in very large quantities (this line in the statement of
assets has gone from £5860 to £17,499 in the six months);
but overdrafts are generally expensive luxuries.
It is figured, on the basis of last half-year’s
reports, that Block 14 can pay its way on a market of
Is. sd. per oz. for silver, and £l2 17s. per ton for lead.
* * * *
Didn’t the question of Chillagoe railway con-
cessions touch up some of the Northern Labor M.L.A.’s?
Men who had posed as thorough-going State socialists
suddenly found out that there yet was room in the world
for land-grant individualism even though land-grant in-
dividualism was actually unwilling to fix a decent day-
wage in return for its privileges. (Present wages paid,
6s. a day). And Mareeba-Chlllagoe successes are to be
followed by other requests for similar concessions, in next
session of Bananaland Parliament.
• * » *
.Judging by the rapid progress of the Emu Bay
Railway Co.’s works, the Chillagoe line should be finished
well within schedule time—which has about 18 months to
run. There will be none of the engineering difficulties
the Emu Bay line has experienced—with bridges every
mile and cuiverts every two chains, and long, spongy
button-grass plains and peaty slopes to get over. Also
the Chillagoe line will probably be constructed by day
labor—so well has the system proved itself on the Emu
Bay works.
• * * *
According to the Warden, the Charters Towers
output for the first quarter was highly satisfactory. The
Day Dawn Block and Wyndham, Brilliant, Brilliant and St.
George, Victory, Victoria and Queen, Band of Hope all
appear on the dividend-list with their wonted consis-
tency; while recent excellent returns from stone in
Kelly’s Queen Block point to speedy inclusion of this mine
in the same enviable catalogue. On the other hand,
Mills’s United makes a 6d. call. Cyanidation returns
would have been much larger but for want of both lime
and cyanide. Search for good limestone is proceeding
round the Towers just now, but, so far, with little suc-
cess. Cyanide returns are seldom complete, on account
of unwillingness of certain cyaniders to give particulars.

Clarke’s Brilliant Worcester and Victory, the
pet mine of Charters Towers, has had a heavy fall owing
to a poor crushing—only an ounce when, it is alleged, two
were looked for.

Vic. Govt. Geologist has issued a preliminary
report on the Mt. Deddiclt silver and lead deposits. “ The
field, as a whole, covering an area of 24 sq. miles, is yet in
its prospecting stage, and, although the veins of galena
are small, they are sufficiently numerous to lend hope to
the ehnethat furtherdevelopments in the crosscutting may
reveal larger masses of galena. It must be borne in mind
that the ore already discovered bears but a small propnr-
t.on to the mans of quartz or other gangue forming the
lodes. I cannot enter into further details respecting the
permanency of the fields in a commercial sense until the
survey novv being carried out by my assistant, Mr. Fer-
gusson, is completed.” Details thus far have sufficed to
send Mt. Deddick shares down to 6s. at time of writing,
six “bob” being equivalent to £6 in the original un-
watered stock. As for Deddick “ pups,” the latest report
from the Scentrai is a pathetic squeal:—“ Work on new
lode shows bottom of drive, carrying lode very strong, and
in face of emphatic assertion of Govt, geologist that lodes
will go down, and with increased value, have reason to be
satisfied with present state of affairs.” The Deddick camp
was going to prove “ another blow for the croakers” when
its true character was investigated, and the railway was
put in hand, and the shares rose to fabulous prices.
Seems to be Stone-Deddick at present.
* * * *
The half-million Mt. Lyell depreciation of last
week was not the shock the daily papers have made it out
to be. Inspired paragraphs and interested barrackers
have alleged the Mt. Lyell deposit to be not only enormous,
but rich, and the public seems astounded at what (it is
nnw plain) should have been foreseen months ago. The
Mt. Lyell deposit will last a generation, but the great pro-
portion of poor ore and the enormous amount of dead
work in the way of removing over-burden puts the pro-
bable dividend at something under 15s. per annum with
the present plant, and only a pro rata increase with the
smelter extension now in hand. And on £l2 a share 15s.
is 6} per cent. Mt. Lyell is a sure enough spec, to be
regarded as an investment, but 6j per cent, is nothing to
howl about in mining : a fact evidently now borne in on
the over-sanguine who talked of the great body as if it
were 10, and not well-under 4, per cent. ore. Suppose
copper fell to £4O, where would Mt. Lyell be?
• » » »
Writes “D.W.D.”: Even at this late date I
must answer “ H.” re the North Lyell. He says that the
tunnels which I described as being bent at an angle from
their original course to strike the new deposit were begun
with the definite intention of striking the deposit. That
is to say the tunnels were put in to cut an ore
body which wasn’t known to exist till some
months later. I pass over his contradiction of
the theory of the existence of such a thing as a con-
centrative attraction in big lhdes and deposits. His
statements are about as vague as mine were. Let “H ”
put, the following in his pipe and smoke it. Up to the
discovery—accidentally made in the cutting of a track—
of the “new” ore body, the N, Lyell was one of the
wildest-looking specs, on the Tasmanian market, which is
saying a good deal. Yet, prior to the discovery, the
London Board was talking airily of millions of tons of ore
which, to put the thing very mildly, nobody on the spot
could vouch for, and securing capital to build a railway to
carry away the visionary ore aforesaid, and to construct
smeiteFs to produce the copper which existed in the
imagination of the Board. And meanwhile the tunnels
aforesaid were being driven to cut the lode which was
then undiscovered and unknown !
What has become of the Great Lake Dora
(Tas.) districts, where the copper was protruding into the
climate in two-ton slugs?

An Australian Premier, whom Earl Dufferin
humorously ticketed at Jubilee time, tells his
friends that he has since received, from an autho-
ritative London source, a letter informing him
that, if he can transfer himself to England, a seat
in the Commons will be placed at his disposal.
Bishop Thornton, of Ballarat, now return-
ing to Australia, has had ten offers of “livings ”
in England during the term of his protracted
visit. The last was the offer of a vicarage worth
.£BOO a year. He wasn't offered a Bishopric, how-
ever, and it is said that is why he comes back
to his Australian “Poll.”
Told of “ Wooster "-sauce-Lea that, when
Lord Sandys, who had given him the recipe,
called upon him and said that, as so much coin
was being turned-in over the sauce, he (Lord S.)
thought he was entitled to a share of the “oof
Lea replied that he was willing to part —if his
lordship would come behind the counter and sell
for him ! The additional big ad. didn’t come off.
Cairns municipal council has half-decided
to shift the naughty Ynm-Yums out of the centre
of the township ; but there is an alleged difficulty
because the council collectively says it doesn’t
know a Yum-Yum when it sees one. Individually
the aldermen know quite a lot, but somehow
they’re too shy to tell.
Commencement of an address to General
Booth, drawn up by “16,387” —confinee in U.S.
San Quentin prison and sergeant-major of the gaol
S.A. corps :
As Providence does not permit us to be personally
with you in ymir campaign, nevertheless we shall be with
you in spirit and in prayer.
Avery special Providence, indeed !
Horatio Bottomley, whose Westralian co’s
have come such a cropper on London Stock Ex-
change, was 14 or 15 years ago a well-known
Fleet-street shorthand writer. He launched the
little Debater, on which he felt his feet and got a
taste for finance, and in a year or two was in the
thick of the Hansard Union and other mammoth
enterprises. Horatio's fatal fondness is for the
ballet; and impecunious managers and others
have bled him much.
“ Democratic ” antagonism to the Federal
Convention Bill appears to be full of the sugges-
tion that Australia in general is an ass. There is
nothing in the Bill, says somebody, “to prevent
the Governor-General selecting an Executive
Council from kanakas, blackfellows, or Chinese.”
By the same token there never was, is, or will be
anything in any bill to prevent a united People
from insisting upon a decent management of their
own business.
It isn’t all fun to be an Afghan in the
West. One Said fatally knifed another Said in
the Coolgardie camp lately. Said 1, being noto-
riously off his head, was at once suspected and
charged, and he quite as promptly confessed.
Then the doctor who examined the accused at
Fremantle Gaol gave evidence that he believed
Said was insane ;. but after Acting-Judge Janies
had remarked that the medical examination was
made 10 weeks after the murder, and then only
with the help of an interpreter, the jury found the
man guilty and he was sentenced to death.
Which, as it wasn’t Said’s fault that the Govt,
hasn’t a doctor who can speak his language, and as
it also wasn’t his fault that the delay in examin-
ing him occurred, seems pretty rough. And
rougher still when Said's sworn-to reputation in
the Afghan camp is remembered.
The gunboat Monocacy, which took part
in the battle of Manila, is a composite paddle-
steamer alleged to have been built for a Hudson
River ferry co. Her guns were old - type
muzzle-loaders on wooden carriages. She was the
shabbiest of the shabby U.S. squadron in the
Pacific in ’B7, arid too tender to be sent the. long
voyage home. Bv the "ay, H.M.B.’s survey-boat
Dart was originally the “ cruiser ” of the Royal
Yacht Club. She has been over 16 years “map-
making ” in these waters, and is virtually one of
the “institutions” of Australia. Mdler’s old
yacht is her sister-ship. The Dart is noted for
carrying the heaviest top-sails and main of any
vessel of her class and tonnage. Also, she’s been
of more real service to Australasia (wanted, a
new name of not over seven letters—a new one,
not the old one boiled down and served up cold as
hash) than all the other men-of-war put together.
Surveying-officers are mostly brainy men without
influence —the “ butterflies ” are kept on the fancy
stations, such as the Mediterranean.

SOCIETY
Papua is becoming Christianised under
compulsion. The thing is worked in this wise :
The Native Regulations make education (three
days a week) in the neighborhood of a school com-
pulsory for all youngsters from five years of age
up to 13. A fine of ss. may be imposed on the
parents of any child who does not attend. But
all the schools are of a semi-religious character, and
are conducted by missionaries. See?
* * * *
Mt. Barker was long represented in S.A.
Parliament.by Dr. Cookborn, who had most to do
with securing tile recognition of women’s political
equality with man in that province. So it was
appropriate’that the Mt. Barker court should, the
other day, have to deal with a charge brought
against Naomi Appleton of leaving her husband,
Reuben, without adequate means of support.
Rube, it seems, was coaxed into making over his
farm sections to Naomi; and, later on, Naomi
having made things sultry, he went “to his
sister’s.” Argued, of course, that as it was Rube
who “left,” and not Naomi, the case must be
dismissed; and so the lady scored again.
• * * •
Attending a funeral often
means antedating your own. Graves
sunk in clay retain the surface water
that drains into them, and when the
grave is re-opened the water (now a
strong cadaver-solution) is baled out and
thrown on the surface, thus stirring up
the germs and giving them a show to
get in good work amongst the mourners.
Moral : prime up with good Scotch —or
stay away ! There is lots to be said, after all, in
favor of whisky at funerals.
• » * *
The lady-doctors scored a point the other
night when the Port Melb. branch of the Austra-
lian Natives’ Association unanimously decided to
place on the medical list of the association the
name of the popular Miss Elizabeth O’Hara, M.B.,
B.S. This is the first time a lady has been ap-
pointed medical officer to a local branch of the
A.N.A. The branch (remarks a cold-blooded
Bulletin correspondent) might, however, con-
sider the advisableness of allowing a member to
be on two doctors’lists at the same time, as many a
man might consider the lady med. quite fit to cope
with the missus and “ kids,” and yet have grave
doubts about trusting his precious self to her
mercies.
• * • *
A maintenance case with points was heard
at Stawell (Vic.), the other day. The husband,
a bee-farmer, did not see that he should be called
upon to support his wife, as he was opening nego-
tiations for her sale. A letter was read in court
in which he declared his intention of letting her
go cheap to a syndicate composed of her mother
and a male party. He asked >£2so cash for his
wife and all the children, 40 acres of land, and a
few etceteras like a cow and a clutch of chickens.
The letter concluded :
I would have no power to interfere, because you are
sold, with 40 acres and buildings and fruit-trees too.
The Bench found for complainant, and adjourned
ere defendant could offer to allow 2| per ceut. off
for cash if the other parties would close. By the
way, divorce by auction is not at all a bad idea.
m * * •
A mother was quoted in Bulletin, the
other day, as replying to daughter’s inquiry about
Miss Blank’s faux pas, that, at all events, it
wasn’t a pas seal. But N.S.W. Full Court has
just decided that a slip of the kind may be a pas
seul. The finding that respondent A. committed
adultery with respondent B. does not, it is held,
establish the fact of B.’sadultery with A. “Things
which are equal to the same thing are not equal to
one another.”
♦ * * *
Gumleaf : “ Victorian authorities are
rightly careful about the wholesomeness of the
imported banana and orange, but what about
the local peach and apple? Crossing Princes
Bridge, the other morning, at 3 o’clock, I noticed
on several cartloads of fruit coming into market
that the boxes were old and filthy, and stained
with manure liquid. Happening along the same
way later, I saw the carts going home again-
loaded with horse-manure, from which the liquid
ran out beneath, and a cloud of steam rose above.
The now empty fruit-boxes were lying higgledy-
piggledy on top.
* * * *
Jones : The humors of market-gardening.
Cabbages in Melbourne from 3d. to 6d. apiece just
now ; cauliflowers are almost unobtainable.
Brighton and neighboring gardeners who planted
a few months ago mostly lost their whole crops
through the dry weather, yet a Chinaman from a
little further out drove a huge load of cauliflowers
—about 80 dozen—into the Victoria market last
Saturday week, and sold them as they stood in one
lot for £4O ! Yet I know white gardeners who have
water laid on and should be able to raise green
stuff.

Dear B.—Concerning Coroners’ inquests.
I agree that, in ordinary cases, the magisterial
inquiry is sufficient, especially if some notice
thereof be given to all putative witnesses. In a
gaol-inquest, however, such as took place at
Wagga, and of which I wrote to your columns,
the six prisoner-jury men are a safeguard to a
certain extent against systematic murder. Asa
rule, however, the prisoners are carefully (officially)
selected old-timers, long - sentence men and
habitual visitors—and, therefore, valueless.—
Scotty.
A_ peculiar case of pre-
natalinfluenee at Carlton, Melb.
The mother, some little time be-
fore her confinement, was trying
to split a piece of pine with a
sharp axe, and, missing her
aim, cut two of the fingers off
her left hand. In her conster-
nation she carried her injured
hand to her face. She noticed,
later, the marks of three fingers
in blood on her nose'and fore-
head. The child, when born,
had the three marks reproduced
npon its nose and forehead in
exactly the same manner, ac-
cording to the mother’s assertion.
Anyhow, the marks on the
child’s face are dis-
tinct] y those of
blood - stained fin-
gers, and the efforts
made at the Chil-
dren’s Hospital to
remove tin* stains
have been quite un-
availing.

Dear Bulle-
tin. Our boasted
civilisation depends
upon the abolition
of the dog-tax in
favor of a cat-tax —
thusly. The change
would result in a
large increase of
dogs, which would
cause a great dimin-
ution of cats, which
would lead to an enormous in-
crease of mice. The mice would
scare more women into matri-
mony and the formation of those
homes “ which are the basis of true civilisation.”
The Single-tax cry is all right—but it should be
for a dog-tax.—K.K.
* * * *
B.E. : No wonder the man-on-the-land
finds it hard to make a living, considering the
prices he charges for his produce. Went up
country lately for a holiday. First day trotted
about a mile to nearest farmhouse for provisions.
Leathery old party didn’t seem particularly
anxious to sell even at —eggs, 2s. Bd. a dozen ;
cabbages, Is. each ; pair ducks, 7s. (id.; apples (the
ground was covered with decaying fruit), l|d. each.
Reckoned I’d struck Klondyke, and tried the
township second day. Paid 4s. for a small shoul-
der of mutton, and sd. for a loaf. Stood it two
days, then sent to Sydney for a crate of potted
meats, flour, and et ceteras, and saved about 75
per cent. Solid fact!
* • • *
Dear Bulletin.—Went up from Melb. to
Box Hill the other morning. In the carriage
(smoking) were two men handcuffed together
accompanied by a uniformed policeman. They
were decent-looking fellows in their working
clothes, and were afterwards discharged. Why
should merely-accused persons be subjected to
this unnecessary disgrace ? Were the constable
in plain clothes very few would have noticed
them.—F.C.
* * * *
How truly self-sacrificing are the clergy !
At last meeting of Presbyterian divines in Bris-
bane, the “ meeneesters ” from the Bakblox ven-
tured to express an opinion that they would like
a change now and again from the desolation of
the West to the comforts of the town —in short,
an occasional parochial “ swap ” with their
luckier town brethren. And didn’t the town-
brethren rage at this reasonable suggestion ! No
talk about gripping staff and girding loins and
doing a fair turn in the Never-Never, but a grind-
ing of teeth, flashing of eyes, and ejaculation of
nastinesses even after the fashion of the Un-
godly !
• * * *
Ezrom : Two pranks of Fate. Not long
ago a young Tasmanian married couple went to
tlie stable to feed their quiet old buggy horse,
and, when the husband jestingly said, ‘I think
I’ll sell the old moke,’ the wife replied ‘ Not while
I’m alive, at all events.’ With which, as she had
often done before, she patted the animal on the
rump, whereupon it lashed out, landing one
foot on the poor girl’s face, and killed her almost
instantaneously. No. 2 was within my personal
experience. An apparently very old man used to
call daily at the office where I was employed, and
the clear-headed manner in which he transacted
his business caused me to ask him his age. ‘ I’m
88, sir,’ he said. ‘ And what do you think of
things after such a long experience ? ’ ‘ Well, sir,
I thank God for my long life ; and, if my time to
go is this very afternoon, I’m ready.’ Hardly had
he walked away 10 yards when, in stepping from
the pavement, he tripped, fell heavily on his head,
and fractured his skull. He survived less than an
hour.
* * * *
The lady superintendent of the Elizabeth
Fry Retreat, Melbourne—a dreary-looking insti-
tution where life for the penitents is one eternal
washing-day—announces that the soapy show is
now being conducted “on faith lines.” The
collector who used to whip round for subscriptions
will not call upon keeind friends any more. “We
■shall be glad of help from any the Lord may
incline to send through,” says the advt., “but
shall not ask any.” Asa matter of fact, the Fry
Retreat is, or ought to be, a very self-supporting
laundex - ee, considering the labour that each able-
bodied lodger devotes to her tub during, say,
twelve months of washing away her sin, and the
state of poverty in which she is eventually let
loose to find a situation as “ general.”
* * * *
Anybody with anything Australian to sell
—pictures, books, old clothes, ash-barrels, no
matter what—take it to England at once! The
Australian boom is keeping up manfully. It
started with the economists’ proofs that Britain
without her colonies was doomed to become a
third-rate Power ; was cemented by Queen Vic.’s
Jubilee ; and capped by the big fright which John
Bull got in the “ splendid isolation ” epoch. John
has realised that he’s got to be very good to Aus-
tralia ; and the products of this country have gone
up nine notches. The newspapers flatter every-
thing witli a eucalyptus twang about it; and
flattery means money. Let us get under the
spout 1

SUNDAY SCHOLAR (reading text on wall): “I won’t need to, Miss
Smith, will I? ’Cause I’d a new mother last week.”
A nasty story is current in Brisbane. A
detective of 10 years’ clean record was summarily
discharged from Q. police for using language
w hile under the influence. A fterwards, he obtained
a billet as wharf-constable with the A.U.S.N. —
from which post, it is stated, he was summarily
sacked at the instigation of a police official.
I’tuple who know Commissioner Parry-Okeden do
not believe that he approves of his subordinate
officers hounding dismissed members of the force
to prison, drink, and perhaps starvation, by pre-.
venting them obtaining and retaining work. Is
there any other side to this story?

At Dunedin, the other day, a very
high Maoriland Anglican ecclesiastic
appeared before the police-commis-
Bioner to explain his position
mortgagee of certain property.
He stated that 18 years ago he
advanced A1000 on the pro-
perty. People had since in-
formed him that the tenants
were not respectable, but he
had no personal knowledge as
to their character. The com-
missioner smiled. The round-
aboutly-suggested idea of a
cleric in a bagnio hunting
up his rents seemed-to high-
ly amuse these sons of Be-
lial. But the ecclesiastic
himself went on to say that
he had offered to cancel the
mortgage if he were paid
back half of the principal.
The mortgagor could not,
however, find the money,
and so the matter stood,
Lais and Phryne must now
feel that their business is in
a measure sanctified. A
portion-of their profits goes
to paying, interest to the
Church^—the Church which
is ready to compound with
the Devil on a half-way
basis. And an absolutely
unreliable M.L. paper says
the interest is 10 per cent.
» * *
In one of the Aus-
tralias . recently a youth was
charged with embezzling moneys
which he knew were rents of cer-
tain disreputable houses, occupied
by immorality and rascality of vari-
ous kinds, and said to be owned by
an exalted public functionary and a
lady of large society tonnage. It leaked-out,
however, that the youth’s solicitor purposed
calling these gilded persons with a view to
questioning them in the witness-box as to
whether they did not take advantage of the smell-
fulness =of their tenants to extort much higher
rents than would be realisable from decent persons
—whereupon the Crown promptly discontinued
the. case, and • the accused, upon undertaking
not to proceed for malicious prosecution, was dis-
charged. Now, who is to prosecute the Crown
for compounding a felony ?
* * * *
S.b. : “Melbourne runs to funerals on
Sundays. Any well-advertised burying—that of
a murdered party, or a sensational suicide, or a
jockey, or a bishop, or a ballet-girl—happening on
the Day of Rust is bound to ‘ take well and give
pleasure to all.’ The daily papers are quite reliable
for once in their notices of the funeral of the
Yarraville murder victims. There was a crowded
house, and the audience was demonstrative. St.
Kilda cemetery was so rushed that the manage-
ment found itself compelled to close its doors an
hour before the arrival of the hearse, and then it
should have hung out “Pit Full” to have pre-
vented a great crush and a battle royal at the
gates. Inside, during the progress of the cere-
mony, the crowd perched itself on the tombs of
lamented persons of great respectability, and
scrambled over the ashes of its fathers, and
climbed obelisks, and urns, and other sacred
masonry, and the tumbled female fought her way
to the front every time, with her back hair loose,
and her hat over her eye, and trod down the grave
decorations. I think this Sabbath appetite should
he turned to account. When a popular corpse is
to he buried, give the business into the hands of a
good manager, make a charge at the cemetery
gate, and let the proceeds go to a fund for the
regeneration of the Melbourne Sunday. At (ire-
sent the' Sabha'h is so dull that a funeral is a
comparatively cheerful function, and every second
man envies the corpse.”
• * * *
The spectacle of some hundreds of people
hustling and soruffing each other in Melbourne
cemetery to get a look at the coffins of two mur-
dered children was a smack in the eye for our
“advanced civilisation.” The impulse, indeed, is
but a variant of the morbid zest for the horrible
which used to bring the crowds to gloat over a
hanging—with this to the greater shame of the
Melb. ghouls, that, while the Tyburn and New-
gate rabbles were largely composed of the dregs
of society, the Melb. “push” wa3 well-dressed
and, presumably, well-educated. But no educa-
tion, when 'the occasion arises, seems to be proof
against an aboriginal relish for the brutal and
horrible. And the longer one lives the more con-
vinced one becomes that, in mouthing abdtat the
“higher destiny of our race,” we are thinking
with our elbows and talking through our hat.
I ** * *
A JBPLijKTIN man was attracted the other
day by a good-sized crowd in Pitt-street, Sydney,
and stopped to see how the fight was getting on.
When he found that the excitement was all be-
cause a man was sitting on the edge of the pave-
ment mending an umbrella, he made some dis-
gusted remarks and passed on. The sort of things
a crowd stops to look at are as good a test as any
of the prosperity of a city. In the Boom days,
when everybody had money enough to go to the
theatre and to Manly and Chowder and the dog-
fight, nothing less than a stand-up fight and a
skull-fracture would draw a big street audience.
Now, in the Gloom days, the crowd has to occupy
its mind .-with anything that costs nothing, so even
a man mending an umbrella attracts an audience.

COMPLIMENTARY.
NIECE OF TALENTED AUTHOEESS: “ Aunty, I am sure you would get published in the ‘Fleet-
street Magazine ’ —they do put such awful things in!”
There will probably be some startling
shipping-disaster news from a point between Ade-
laide and Albany shortly. Reports have already
been published of “ harmless contests ” between
rival passenger-boats “ timed to leave port at the
same hour,” of one “virtually challenging” the
other and the other “ gamely responding to the
invitation.” Also, there are reports of “yet
another struggle ” between the same steamers ;
but unfortunately there were head winds and
other drawbacks, so perhaps “ when they meet
again a more exciting struggle for mastery ” will
be witnessed. Quite likely—with the possibility
that the struggle will not be confined to the two
boats.
Melbourne Presbytery started recently to
talk over the ever-interesting subject of the col-
lection, and one reverend suggested a great idea
for persuading every wor.-bipper to contribute.
The reverend’s plan was simply that the plate
should stop short in front of every worshipper,
and not budge until at least a copper was forth-
coming, or until the unfinancial person rose up
and went out. Then, as it would be no use to
bold the plate in front of the empty place where
he had been, it must either go on to the next man
or else decamp out of the church and down the
road after the defaulter, and continue the argu-
ment until he either parted or gave way to anger
and kicked the plate, right back into B.C. The
only possible improvement on this idea seems to
be that female as well as male elders should be
appointed, and the congregation should be seized
and searched after service and all its coppers
taken off it, and where there are not so many
coppers on any worshipper as there ought to be,
be or she should be retained until the other 2d. is
forthcoming, or should leave part of his or her
clothing as security.

The Sims divorce-case—heard in Melb.
last week, was a very ordinary pale-brown dis-
agreement about a mother-in-law—there were no
purple streaks of sin in it and no rich sunset tints of
iniquity, and it was a perfectly moral and respect-
able row all through. The one bright patch was
Sims’ wailsome letter offering to have his tail
trodden on to any extent if his wife would only
come back :
I have, darling wife, as I promised, resolved this time
in earnest to turn over a new leaf, and by God’s help to
leafl a totally different life in the future to that of the
past. Ethel, I once was a Christian resolved to follow
Christ, but since I came to Melbourne and joined the
Railway department I went from bad to worse through my
want of faith and conception of Jesus Christ. . . . Your
poor, penitent, heart-broken husband. . . . burdening my
poor soul with mountains of unnecessary deep-dyed sin.
... I promise faithfully, in the presence of Almighty
God, to be a perfect comfort to you, and to behave as a
Christian gentleman should -true, kind-hearted, Christ-
like, prayerful, zealous, attentive and neat in dress. . . .
I am going to dress nicely for the future and be a credit
to you. ... If they will accept my proposals I will make
a’.l my property over to either you or your mother at
once as a guarantee of my good conduct and kindness to
them in the fnlure.
The letter e ided with twelve pages of original
h rain about the Need of Repentance. But though
Si ris held cut the olive branch of this great liter-
ary effort, the breach didn’t heal worth tuppence.

The extraordinary allegations of “ cronk-
ness ” made by both sides in a recent Melb. will-

“A man who knows”: Poultry farming
will not pay more than seven miles from Sydney.
Reason : cheap food by the purchase of hotel and
other waste; saving of freight
and commission ; high selling
price by dealing direct witii
purchasers. Ducks are far better
than fowls.
The demi-monde in Collins-street, Mel-
bourne, are occasionally chivied by the plain-
clothes police, and with tightly-drawn dresses
make for the tram or cross street. They are now
utilizing this effect for “biz.,” and Johnnie is
surprised by a young lady whose knee-kilted
garments expose delicate lingerie and shapely
ankles, suddenly seizing his arm, and with tearful
voice begging to be allowed to walk a little way
with him, as those nasty police were about. The
ruse is generally successful; even a well-known
Flinders-lane magnate, of weight in religious
circles, has been known to escort one to the nearest
corner, and with “Go home, keep out of trouble,
and be a good girl,’’ hand over a sov.
A wild-looking character, in a lamentable
state of bush-rum and snakes, pot at the Numur-
kah (Vic.) police for a night’s board and lodging
lately. He called in with a horrible tale about
how he had murdered one Cohen in ’92, by putting
dynamite under his bed, and connecting it by 90ft.
of fuse with an adjacent hut. Then, when Cohen
was asleep, he lighted the fuse, and by-and-bye
there was a fearful explosion, and for half-an-hour
after it rained Cohen all over those parts. The
police took him in on the strength of this tale, and
told him to prepare for execution. Next day they
learned, to their utter disgust, that no Cohen had
blown up in ’92, and their prisoner was a harmless
beer-wreck, who saw Deadwood Dick visions when
in liquor, though at other times he was an inno-
cent individual who ran after cows for a living.
The tale of Cohen’s death was so large and crude
and gory that the Numurkah police are now
ashamed of having been taken in by it, and desire
that the unpleasant subject shall be dropped.

A correspondent of Australasian Chemist
AND Druggist declares that a few months ago ap-
plications were invitpd in Mdb. Argus for the
position of “Nursing and Dispensing Matron,’’
to have charge of a country hospital under the
medical officer, at a salary of =£so per annum.
This is given as an extract from a late letter of
the successful applicant:—
I like this place and the people, very well, the doctor
especially (happy doctor). Ido all of his dispensing, hos-
pital and private. He has a very extensive practice.
There is no other doctor nearer than one way and
the other way (40 miles one way, 60 miles the other).
So you see I often have lo act resident doctor. lean
stitch scalp wounds, set fractures, extract teeth. I charge
2s. Gd. for teeth. lam allowed to keep that. I have 19
patients in now. We admit about 25 a month. They
generally stay about a week or two. A lot suffer from
sore eyes. I have six cases of typhoid. You will be sur-
prised to know the normal temperature (of the blood)
here is 99.4 deg. Fahrenheit. . . . I have sole charge in
the doctor’s absence. ... I make the out-patients
pay Is. a visit. I have got for the month about £'4.
Doctor is going to ask the committee to give me 2s. in
the y. for all money I collect, so that will make my salary
a little more in the year.
Try to imagine one individual carrying out with
efficiency all the following offices : —l. Dispenser
for hospital and private patients within a radius
of 50 miles. 2. Resident surgeon in absence of
qualified medico. 3. Dentist. 4. Hospital col-
lector. 5. Matron. 6. Head nurse. And all,
presumably, for the fat remuneration of a fraction
over 2s. BJd. per day !

They have flourishing Mormon communi-
ties at East Brisbane and at Ipswich. A giddy*
holy institution is about the only thing that could
flourish at the latter dreary town, which is the
dumping-ground of more Little Bethels than any
other place this side of the line. Also, the birth-
certificate giving the putative father as “ J. Smith,
residence unknown,” is greatly in evidence at
Ipswich.
Not a house of easy virtue now exists in
Exhibition-street (Melb.), and in Lonsdale-street
remain only those priestesses of Venus who own
their own property. This clearing-out of the city
(of course absolutely useless, as it merely means a
shift to the suburbs and a return to old quarters
when things have quieted down) has been carried
out at the instance of P.M. Panton, who has an
idea that the two streets named ought to be occu-
pied by residential flats and city mansions.

The Turner Government proposes to repeal
or radically amend the Victorian Act for the
more speedy starvation of illegitimate children.
Melb. police are continually down upon
tbe Asiatics in the slums, for that over-crowding
which the health authorities ignore. This class is
of the lowest moral and social
grade, and special legislation is
necessary to enforce some slight
observance of the. ordinary laws
of health. A doctor, called re-
cently to a couple of patients
(consumption and pneumonia),
found them in a room 10ft. sq.,
crowded with occupants, sleeping
<i*d cooking amidst an intolerable
frencti. Then hawkers’ packs were slung
on the wall, and doubtless would spread
disease broadcast on the exhibition of their
contents in suburban households. And, alas for
the “silken bonds” ! —these people are “British
subjects.
* * * *
The splendidly-situated beach-reserve at
Manly is in a disgracefal state. It is a mere grass-
less eyesore, given up to banana-peel, old news-
papers, empty tins, broken bottles, and general
smellful frowsiness. If the local municipality
can’t afford to keep its reserves in some sort of
trim, Government should shell-out more liberally,
in view of the fact that the Manly reserves are, at
one time or another, the resort of all Sydney.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, May 24, 1898.
Mr Dear Myeb—
I have been taking lessons this week from a
bright little book with a lady in red on its cover
and advice within to ladies who write. Until yon
digest the innards of the production, you might
think the pictured lady over-gaudy for a “pen-
hen ” who spreads her bread with Mrs. de
Browne’s family paste, trims up her old black
grenadine with Lady Gros-gros’s last season’s
chiffon, and treats herself to a winter hat
made from the summer roses that adorned Prin-
cess Maudie’s bonnet at the opening of the Mad
Cats’ Institute. But, when you learn of certain
lady fash ion-writers whose wage in bigger than a
Cabinet Minister’s, the gaud assumes a perfect
correctness. And, when you are brought face to
face with specimens of the famous Mrs. Aria's
composition (Mrs. A. is the most conspicuous
fashion-writer in the world), you wonder why the
ladies who do another kind of mangling, and the
ladies who clean orfices, and the ladies who tear
each other’s clothes to pieces with nails instead of
pens, don’t devote themselves to gaud and jour-
nalism straight away.
What do you think of these two specimens? I
offer them because the perusal may pour oil upon
some Cabinet Minister’s wounded pride. “I have
no objection to the lion lying down with the lamb,
the Persian lamb, or rather, I should say, to the
sable being allied to this fur, or to the combination
of black caracule or sable with ermine, any two
furs, or indeed three furs put together. I recognise
as appropriate and elegant, but the frivolous work-
ing of furs with colored satins and silks now
obtaining the affections of the many is not at all
to my taste.” And the next goes one better :“ A
single row of white pearls next to the fair or even
dark throat of a woman has always a beneficial
effect upon her complexion. And, talking about
a beneficial effect upon a woman’s complexion, let
me mention once again the exceeding becoming-
ness of the new shades of blue, these being rather
of a sugar-paper order of blue, but a little lighter
in color, perhaps, yet having that vivid tone about
it. This is freely mixed with dark blue, the
lighter shade being used for making trimmings for
bodices, or indeed to make an entire bodice. The
hat which completes it will take every shade of
blue.” And of such is one of the largest incomes
earned by any woman in the world !
Yet this lady is allowed the last word on dress
in all dressing capitals—prophesies fashion with-
out a failure, shows chiffons the ways they are
bound to go, condemns, invents, introduces, and
has published a big fat boast that she scorns to
treat of fabrics whose cost price is under so many
guineas a yard. With her own income in the
background, she is pitiless in pushing women
along the road to dressmaker’s bills and ruin.
When Australian women get into the grip of her
advice, the husbands of the bills and gilded gowns
may thank me for offering the above specimens on
which the unkind words of the bill-payers can
wreck themselves with the scorn of a just cause.
With that end in view, I recommend the mascu-
line mind to get these gems by heart.
This nice little book is written by a man, and
calls itself a practical guide. But, notwithstanding
Man, some of its hints should be served with a
large amount of salt. The Art of Corresponding
with an Editor opens with a protest against
family history, “your excellent paper which I have
always admired,” and omission of name, address,
and stamp. There’s virtue in that, but then, just
as his cause is winning, the author gives up the
game as meekly as a mouse. “ Resolve to see your
editor, face to face. . . . Ask for him in a firm
voice. . . . Requested to state the nature of
your business, make it as vague and enticing as
possible. ... If the editor is engaged, ask for
an appointment or wait. . . . Refused an ap-
pointment or told that to wait is useless, call to-
morrow or the next day ; be pertinacious, and by
pertinacity you will eventually see him.” And
that little book will go all over the world. And
all over the world women will be hard at work
inventing “vague and enticing business.” And
a new brand of editors, without any teeth and
without any hair, will curse the name of E. B.
Bennet with curses that stay out all night,
because they are too heavy for the ordinary
poultry-roost. Strange to say, this Bennet is
himself an editor of a big woman Vinagazine, and
one’s inference is that he is trying to palm off on
his pals some of his own bad times.
Last Friday, the ghosts of Claudians, Hamlets,
and OtheUos had Her Majesty’s to themselves,
and, Saturday, “ Sign of the Cross ” hoisted itself
aloft again. Cne more lightning stroke was added
to those electric flashes that go wherever Wilson
Barrett goes. In no less than four plays of this
actor’s staging, the lightning fizzles and the
thunder bellows just in time to reveal the wicked-
ness of some bold, bad person. “Claudian,”
“ Ben-my-Chree,” “ The Sign of the Cross,” and
“ Virginius,” are all illuminated by these spasms
of heavenly ire. A big house welcomed the
“Cross” people with the same old welcome.
Everyone was applauded. Wilson Barrett thought-
fully sent bouquets to all the leading lights, and
milk-and-water, and religion, and lightning, fed
and clothed and warmed the audience as comfort-
ably as ever.
Miss Ferrar still springs a surprise on everyone
when she owns up to her affection for Marcus
Superbtis. Julius Knight is credited with the re-
mark that he is as much surprised as anyone when
Mercia tells him lightly that she loves him. The
family likeness between the voices of Miss Ferrar
and Miss Jeffries is more noticeable than ever.
By the way, Maud Jeffries’ photographs have sold
so lavishly in Sydney that one bookshop owns to
doing .£7O worth of biz. in this line alone. What
a lot of people make money out of a popular
actress ! The florists who name their own prices
to her adoring vegetarians, the man who takes her
photographs and sells them to the shops, the shops
that sell them to the people, the dressmakers,
hairdressers, milliners who assist in her attiring
and use her name as a brand for goods that won’t
go off without a brand. To say nothing of the
fashion-writers who desoribe her frocks, and —but
stop—l see the long and doleful line of Johnnies
who have lost by her, and I haven’t the heart to
follow up these reflections.
A second visit to “ The Middleman ” revealed
Mr. Styan in a new black tie, and Charles Tlnirs-
by in a clean shave that put away with the
moustache all likeness to Captain Feilden. But
the change that was most needed had not occurred
at the time of my visit. The house was as little
and thin as ever, and Cartwright’s magnificent
acting was still pouring itself out to comparatively
empty benches.
All sorts of reasons are afloat for the profound
coolnesswith which Sydney play-goers have ignored
these Criterion performances. One reason, which
is just as feeble on its legs as any other reason that
has no right to an existence, states that people
don’t go because Miss Lamb is not Miss Nether-
sole. The impetuous Olga, with her big black
eyes and unreserved stores of passion, was cer-
tainly a witching lady. But why should a golden-
haired actress, with blue eyes and a statuesque
face, be blamed because her eyes don’t flash black
sparks ! As far as the public is concerned, that is
about the depth of their objection. All through
this Australian tour Miss Lamb has never been
taken fairly “on her own.” Miss Nethersole’s
astral haunts her at every step, and all that she
can do goes for nothing, because she cannot play
on Olga’s warm dark lines.
Miss Lamb and Miss Darragh were the victims
of another frost last Thursday. The very high-
toned committee of the Factory Girls’ Ball invited
these ladies to judge the fancy dresses, but four
dresses do not require much judging. The prize-
winner was little Nessie Law, who deserved
another prize for shedding one brief beam along
the dullness of the evening. Dressed as a sprite,
with shiny little wings, she and her bicycle
whisked round the room with much vivacity, and
the gratitude of those people who didn’t know
where to put their yawns was long and pro-
nounced.
There was no wild wrestling over the. Federa-
tion prize. One Mr. Flannigan dressed up as
Henry Parkes, and had the whole field to him-
self. He accepted half his prize-money with a
meekness I cannot think Parkes would have
approved of. The much admired Thursby also
was guilty of a disguise. But I was disappointed
with the Factory girls. Perhaps they didn't go,
or perhaps l was not clever enough to distinguish
Society and Factory without their labels. Any-
way, there was no one there of whom I could say
positively, “ she’s a factory.” On the whole, the
dance was not the cheery entertainment it deserves
to be. These factory girls with their long, hard
hours are a worthy charity, but somehow this
particular committee never draws the public in to
any great extent.
The fashion of the day is still tucked. A light
grey cashmere, pale as smoke on a white cloud,
adorned an at-home lately with hundreds of tiny
tucks that overlapped each other from the neck to
the item. No trimming was needed; the sleeves
were tucked, the grey chiffon sunshade was tucked,
the plisse velvet toque pretended to be tucked, and
a hot crimping iron made imitation tucks over all
the lady’s golden hair. But the simplicity of these
tucks is one of the things that are not what, they
seem. Tn leed, the hair is the only thing that may
be subjected to a home-made treatment.
Sydney musicians are waking from their summer
dreams this month. Some wake with songs on
their lips, some with waltz tunes, and one with an
anthem. Time was, when nobody with proper
self-respect failed to write at least one waltz
a lifetime. And some respected themselves to the
number of two, and some to the number of three,
and so on, until the revolving bubble burst with
self-respect and want of cash. And now, only a
very hardened case puts a waltz seriously before
the public. Self-respect lets itself loose in song
to-day, and the whim of the moment buys warbles
where it used to buy waltzes. Melodies from
popular c-oinic operas have snatched the laurel-
crown from original dance compositions. Local
music-makers will soon make a fortune as readily
from a varsoviana as from a waltz or a polka.
You will be glad to hear that Sydney Hoben,
creator of “ Melons ” and “ Cromaboo,” has
written a graceful little love-song, of the twilight
order, “For L"ve of Thee” (mind you, it is
dedicated to Mr. Burns-Walker). From Mel-
bourne comes a patriotic- cry, “ United We Will
Be,” inscribed to the people of Australia, who
might make a good thing of this song if they all
rose and cried it at a given signal. Otherwise,
there is a lack of excitement in four or five folk
singing united we will be around the piano at an
evening party. Dr. Mcßuruey is responsible for
the music and William Carrington for the words,
and the words have overlooked no correct, con-
ventional national sentiment of my acquaintance,
also the music hides a dearth of originality beneath
a capable mnsicianly cloak. “ The Treasure
Trove” waltz, by F. R. Campbell (Paling & Co.),
is dedicated to Major and Mrs. Ferguson’s infant
son by special permission—of the infant son, or of
his nurse, or of whoever gives permission to infant
sons to accept these dedicated goods. “The
Treasure Trove ” is a painstaking composition
that changes its theme half-a-dozen times, and
desists from wearing its coda into shreds as the
modern waltz delights in doing. And now I come
to my anthem, and am met with the jolly injunc-
tion—-“ Let us make a joyful noise 1 ” (W. H. Glen
and Co.). Of all the music before me, this is the
only muse that alludes to itself as a noise, and
courtesy impels me to recommend the work to
everyone who is unable to make a more joyful
noise of his own. The opening chorus is quick
with melodious life, and the solo is a pleasing
little air with top notes that will be an acquisition
at a monotonous church service. The musician in
this case is Mr. H. Hadwen Chandler.
It is not every musician will lie so missed as
Harry H.vndes, who died in Sydney last week. He
was popular everywhere, and belied his physical
largeness with an affable tranquillity of nature,and
of pianoforte playing. All sorts of happy parties
have revelled among the aesthetic drapes and
lacquers of his rooms at Paling’s. With Harry
for a host, a party could not but be jolly. I feel
sure the harps and violins have brightened up on
the Other Side since ho passed over. It is said
that private means protected him too securely
from over-exertion. Otherwise his thirty-four
years might have run on into the seventies. But
even at seventy his passing would have left a big
cold blank behind.
To lecture on Napoleon Buonaparte is rather an
heroic undertaking in these days, when “ lectures ”
have been pushed into the background by variety
entertainments, and solid, serious discussions are
not permitted out of church hours. Madame
Conolly’s exposition at the Centenary Hall last
week was a quaint performance, embellished with
a white satin empire gown, red roses, and a chic
but baffling foreign accent. The Frenchy-looking
lecturer had a naive liveliness of manner that
made her little audience laugh more than once.
And to make a little audience laugh is a much
more difficult matter than to tickle the fancy of a
big, combustible crowd. Broad smiles broke into
sounds when Madamedescribed the little corporal's
methods when conversing with women. Other
subjects failing, he would ask them how many
children they had. Then a love-letter from Nayj.
to Josephine was read aloud, and its fervent,
flowery fallacies were supplemented by the artless
comment, “/tink it is lofely.”
There is nothing new in the lecturette. No re-
flections, no deductions, and not an overwhelming
store of knowledge. Yet the empire gowns, and
the foreign accent, and the red roses helped along
an entertainment that a serge frock and thick
boots and a twang would have chilled and killed
in two minutes. I gathered from the card that
every sort of patronage, from Government House
downwards, had been bestowed upon this lecture.
Yet there was no one in the five filled rows that
looked like a patron, and it seems that the men-
tion of V.R. patronage is losing its charm even on
the very outer circles.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
‘‘Classical music is having a turn in Melbourne
this winter, to make up for the absence of it last.
r ihe Marshall-Hall orchestral concerts and the
Elsie Hall concerts are in full swing, and there
are other things looming ahead. They are all
well-attended, though all strictly classical, with-
out a bar of rumty-tum-tura, or a song of the
‘ I kissed her in the moonlight ’ order. Thus, at
the first of the Chamber music-concerts, the music
was entirely by Brahms, Schubert, Schumann,
and Raff. Best of all : all this music is inter-
preted by resident artists, not the specially im-
ported and largely-boomed variety ; and is, in
point of fact, all the better for that reason. There
isn t a finer pianist out of Europe than Benno
Scherek, and his playing in Schubert’s Quintet in
A major was something to make one’s hair curl.
‘‘ The Athenaeum Hall was more than two-
thirds filled for the first of Allan’s Chamber
Music Concerts by an audience (mostly women
and very unobtrusively trimmed) that took its
two hours’ entertainment with evident thankful-
ness. A Brahms trio, a bit of Raff and Schubert’s
good old Trout Quintette, got the kindest of
treatment—long-haired Herren Scherek and
Hattenbach, and close-cropped George Weston,
looking less than ever like a fiddler, being c iefly
instrumental in making the other people feel glad
to be there. As an introduction to the Quintette
performance, Herr Rudolph Himmer (what
should we do without our Germans?) gave the
Schubert song of the Trout rather faultily, after
he had warbled ‘Du bist die Ruh ’ with taste
and feeling. The ‘star’ of the afternoon was
Herr Seharf, who tackled Schun.ann’s ‘ Etudes
Symphoniques ’ in masterly style, and was twice
called back to bob his head at his admirers.
Seharf has seldom been heard on a Melb. platform
since the Musins went away, and one had almost
forgotten what a clever pianist he is.
“ In Melbourne the domestic servant is practic-
ally extinct. Suburban registry-office keepers
supply much-sought-after damsels with a list of
competing ‘missuses’ to visit and decide upon
at leisure. One sprite called lately under Ma’s
wing to inspect a possible crib. 'After a stiff
cross-examination of the would-be employer,
mother and daughter withdrew, the former re-
marking austerely ‘We have other interviews
this morning and, of course, Queenie can't accept
the appointment without consulting her Papa.’
A candidate of older growth offered to give the
billet a show on three conditions. (1) House-room
for small harmonium. (2) Christian privileges
(unspecified). (3) Title of ‘ Miss,’ because, as she
