“ It’s always tin* lie-householder who raises Cain
when laundry or culinary results are not up to the
mark. Theoretically, however, he likes to cham-
pion the down-trodden slavey against the alleged
oppression of tyrants of her own sex. A local
Benedict, ‘Jack’ by name, at one time always
deprecated Jill’s criticism of their then parlor-
maid. ‘1 ou don t take her the right way,’ he
would say. ‘ Look how willing she is to oblige
me.’ When Abigail’s day-out came round
‘ Missus ’ went into the absentee’s room to unearth
the books habitually accumulated by che inmate
from all parts of the house. As she dug out the
volumes front under curl-papers and sieh, her eye
was caught casually by her own and Hubby’s
Christian names writ large on a document spread
open amongst the hotch-potch. Small blame to
the intruder if she promptly consulted the context
of what proved to be a letter from the recipient’s
young man. ‘Jack is too mean to live,’ he wrote
- Wouldn t I like to chew his ear. Suppose
Jill still scrapes her fiddle. Does she keep any-
where nearer the tune than she used ? It's
enough to give you the pinny-ache. ‘
“ T’other evening, when a crowded suburban
train was raided, a ticketless lady waxed pathetic
at the thought of detection, vowing that her dis-
grace must bring down Hubby’s bald head with
sorrow to the grave. A gallant fellow-passenger
gave her his ticket, first noting the number on a
scrap of paper. When the collector reached his
corner the dodger bridled up and said, ‘You’ve
got my ticket already.’ The official denying it,
the argument waxed warm. Porter announced
‘ There are 12 passengers here and I’ve only got 11
tickets.’ Traveller retorted, ‘You’ve got mine
that is all I know.’ Supposed culprit, when asked
to get out of the train, demanded instead that the
station-master should be brought to hear his com-
plaint against the careless employe. The chief,
impressed by the passenger’s cocksureness, said he
was very sorry, but he must accept his subordi
nate’s statement. Then accused brought his last
ship into action, and blustered, ‘ To prove that I
had a ticket, I’ll tell you its number,’ which he
did. The flabbergasted porter, snubbed by the
station-master for his ‘ mistake,’apologised to the
traveller, who pompously pardoned him. What
price chivalry ?
“ Harking back to the ‘ Royal Divorce ’: how
poor Josephine Ferrar’s dress-standard deterio-
rated as the play rolled on ! Beginning with
spangled white brocade, Crown and Imperial
mantle, she sank from short-waisted silk sacque
to matronly purple cloak and Empire bonnet,
draining' the last dregs of all in hooded black
cover-alls. Still, according 1 to contemporary
chronicles, bloated dress-biils first rifted the love-
lute of Napoleon and his Josephine, so an old age
of compulsory frumpdom was a well-earned doom
for the wastrel.
1 ‘ By all accounts, female royalties, past and pre-
sent, seldom loud though often dowdy, are apt in
real life to manifest dislike or jealousy by frigid
disdain rather than slap dash insolence. As love-
sick Berenis, of ‘The Sign,’ Miss Elliot Page
looked patrician anyway.; but the second Mrs.
Buonaparte’s arm-akimbo, nose-in-the-air deport-
ment while visiting Josephine, would sit better
on some purse-proud she-bounder than on a
‘ Daughter of the Caesars.’

“ Somehow men (schoolmasters excepted) seem
even more easily melted than women by juvenile
woes, real or imaginary. T'other night a he-
ininate of the d.c. mopped hard all the evening,
but when it became evident that the consumptive
cherub meditated instant heavenward flight, this
torn-down spectator broke up and blundered
blindly to the back, where he blubbered behind a
post, while his ‘ girl ’ kept her seat and chewed
their joint supply of chocolate walnuts. Mrs.
Maesmore Morris, just as pretty as of yore,
and less amateurish, wears, to begin with, a
delightful silk blouse in broad stripes of rose and
white with skirt of dull-rose cashmere. Later on,
as the bride of her Majah, she trips about in
black-and-white check Russian blouse costume
and toque of geranium chiffon. Zouave of silvery
passementerie brightens her black brocade dinner-
gown, and a midnight.alarm enables her to air a
classic robe of yellow cr§pe. Tea-gown, this gar-
ment must be, though in the same scene Emilie
Hughes’s vestment (white nainsook, all inser-
tioned in front) is own cousin to a robe de
nuit. Afterwards both ladies rush off in these
rigs to rescue stabbed Titheradge from the mid-
night streets. Considering that Mrs. Thornton
(Emilie Hughes) need not consider expense, her
feelings towards the ‘ mitherless bairn,’ when once
he stands revealed as somebody else’s chick, in-
cline towards the leathery. Evidently a tigress
woman, who licks her cubs only because they’re
hers, like Thenardier in ‘ Les Miserables.’
“ Nowadays, girl-tourists get back from ’Ome in
black-cashmere frocks, jewelled belts, and hair
packed up into the crowns of Tudor toques. For
dinner-wear, they bring their topknots forward till
they overlook the forehead, and fasten big blowsy
white roses in their low-cut black-satin bodices.
For ball-gowns, white satin is the fabric of the
hour, and, altogether, fashion droops in the clutch
of a reaction from the mad, merry color-mixtures
of recent seasons.

thousand per annum. Young, graceful, and fas-
cinating, this lucky damsel, having completed her
contimrital education, only waits to be presented
at Court before returning to her native heath.

union of the son of Winter-Irving, a squatting
magnate with a large stucco mansion in Armadale,

wedded at All Saints, St, Kilda, where so many

“ Overheard in a suburban train a few short
months ago:—‘Freddy, dear, open the window.’
‘No, Gladys, darling; if I do, you’ll catch cold.’
‘ Please, Freddy dearest.’ ‘ No, Gladys sweetest.’
‘ Well, if you don’t. I’ll get another Freddy.’ The
window went up with a loud bang, and the whole
carriageful of tired business-men grinned behind
their ’Erat.ds. The little moral which I would
carefully pin on to the above fragment of endear-
ing converse is that Gladys did get another Freddy
eventually. Freddy the second, for whom No. 1
was mittened, has much coin, and she wa* married
to him the other day.
“The Australian Church choir, with outside help
and Lyric orchestra conducted by A. Levy, suc-
cessfully wrestled with ‘Stabafc Mater’ before a
crowded churchful on Thursday evening. W.
E. Nott, at the organ, played two little things of
his own which pleased the company, and the
orchestra has been worked up to professional
pitch by the conductor. It is the best amateur
band in Melb., and a residuum of good taste left
by the Cowen performances in extravagant Vic-
torian orchestra days, which makes one think that
the money wasn’t altogether wasted, after all.
The sensation of the evening was the plump little
soprano in pink reaching for the high C in the
‘ Inflammatus,’ that languorous, passionate Italian
love-song, which, because it is labelled * sacred
music,’ good people listen to in a church where
they would be horrified at any ‘profane’ min-
strelsy. The soprano was so little and the note
was so high that many in the back rows stood up
at the critical moment to get a full view. But she
came out of the ordeal with great triumph, and
was the success of the concert.
Yours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULI.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Dr. Short, Adelaide’s first Anglican Bishop,
had the honor of being for a time tutor to
Gladstone.
R. E. O’Connor, of Convention Bill fame,
is credited by two of the Premiers, who attended
the sittings in Melbourne, with being the most
expert draftsman of Parliamentary measures in
the polities of Australia.
King Malietoa, of Samoa and general
South Sea Island fame, and auctioneer Hardie, of
Hardieand Gorman, were schoolfellows, and still
occasionally correspond by means of presents. Mr.
Hardie’s mother taught the dusky royalty to read
and write.
The Age follows the Argus in office
changes, and soon chief-of-staff Shuler will take
his place among the leader-writers, while Prancis
Fox, late of the Standard, steps in as chief re-
porter. During editor - Windsor’s recent illness,
Shuler has occupied the editorial chair.
S. Gov. Buxton departs for ’Ome in
Dec., ahd it is probable he will not return. H. Ex.
is ambitious to enter the Commons, but it is doubt-
ful if even an English constituency could be found
to elect this homely old man as its member. He
has already made two attempts and failed.
Mr. A. INCLIS-CLARE, the new Tasmanian
Judge.
Never introduce your wife co any man
named Alexander. In M -lb. Divorce Court, the
other day, no less than three Alexanders were
cited as co-respondents. Alexander clearly doesn’t
mean “ a helper of men ” for nothing ! Alexander
means Sandy, and Sandy means Ginger, and
Ginger means—er—co-respondence.
“The Grand Old Man” was the last to
linger of a notable band of intellects born in the
same auspicious year—lBo9. Of these Mendel-
ssohn, the composer, was the first to go in ’47.
Abraham Lincoln followed in ’65, then Darwin in
'B2, Alfred Tennyson in ’92, Oliver Wendell
Holmes, ’95; lastly, W. E. Gladstone, 19th May,
’9B.
The disinterment by Sydney “ Ninety-
Eighters ” of “insurgent-chief” Michael Dwyer’s
bones recalls the gruesome blunder by which,
when the exhumation of D. H. Deniehy’s relics
was in hand, the remains of a hanged man went
very near to playing the leading role in the solemn
procession to Waverley cemetery. Deniehy’s
troubles, too !
Murray Smith, in a silk-lined frock-coat,
an aggressively-glazed white shirt-front, and
giving off a lavender odor, speaking at the smell-
ful Democratic Club : “ A Constitution is a
serious business. It is not to be changed as you
change your shirt.” Single-shirted Democrat,
from back of hall, ruefully : “That’s if you have
one to change.”
Died in Adelaide, May 13, Philip Levi,
who, half a century ago, had a good part of the
northern suburbs of the S. A. capital in his sheep-
run. But, though he turned to city warehouse
business as well, Mr. Levi, like three-fourths of
those who took up large tracts of the northern
areas of S. A., lost very heavily, and died com-
paratively poor.
Chief Justice Madden does not believe in
juries. “No jury can equal a Judge,” was the
gist of his evidence on this point before the Law
Commission.
Nine tailors go to make a man,
A good till or a Lad un,
Blit tijetve mere laymen never can
Quite make a man like Madden !
The Argus learns with surprise that
William Archer, most scholarly of British dra-
matic critics, has a connection with Australia,
inasmuch as he wrote a lurid melodrama, mostly
about bushrangers, for Sadler’s Wells Theatre in
his youthful days. Though a Perth Scotsman by
birth, the Ibsen prophet is the son of an ex-Agent-
General for Queensland, and was out here with
his family in 1876-7.
T. J. Byrnes is the first Roman Catholic
to attain the position of Premier in Queensland.
John Douglas was charged with having ’verted
to R.C.-ism while Premier, in consequence of
Bishop O'Quinn marrying him, but he publicly
denied the statement. Queensland Governments,
however, have almost all been so partial to R.C. in-
terests as to suggest that , the church gains most
when she supports “ libera] ” Protestants.
Major Reay, of Melbourne ’Erald, who
was merely a journalist in uniform when he went
’Ome to the Jubilee with the Vic. contingent, is a
raging loyalist and patriot now-a-days. Reay
shook hands with a princess or two, and Queen
Victoria has sent him an autographed portrait of
her royal self. Reay is undone. He whoops for
the Hempire, and can’t sleep in his bed for wonder-
ing what a man of his distinction ouylit to do next.
If a small boy shouts “ Hoo-reay ! ” the ill-fated
editor takes off his hat.
H. : “Dully, in his autobiog, mentions it
was by bis advice that Marcus Clarke truncated
‘His Natural Life’ for the London publisher,
Bentley. Clarke humbly sent the whole story, as
published in the Australian Journal, to Duffy,
who said that both the introduction and ending
must be cut away, while the central part would
serve, suitably written up to and down from.
Clarke altered the story to improve the motivation
—always a dangerous process. In the original
Lalor is Peter Brawler.”
Millionaire Gov. Brassey’s solicitude for
the people of Victoria and his desire that they
should receive his greetings was characterised by
his usual regard for economy. Did he march to
the nearest telegraph office in London, and pay
like a man for sending out his precious greetings ?
Not he ! He went to the Agent-General’s and
got them cabled at the country’s expense. Now,
if the Audit Office is courageous it will disallow
the cost of that cable, and insist on having it
deducted from Brassey’s screw.
N. : “The clerical remark about the
Crotty bequest giving work to the poor in build-
ing the useless St. Patrick’s spires, recalls a yarn
about Spurgeon. Pastor Hatcher, from theU.S.,
visited this idol of his, at Beulah Hill, the nicest
spot in London, where Spurgeon had his snug
earthly mansion. Says the severe Hatcher:
‘ Brother, this ought to have been given to the .
poor.’ Spurgeon: ‘So it was, brother, so it
was. But I made ’em work for it. You wouldn’t
have me encourage Sloth, would you ? ’ ”
“Saw Phil May the other day,” writes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent. “His
Annual has now been bought by VV. Thacker and
Co., of India and London, an enterprising pub-
lishing firm, and May expects to benefit materi-
ally by the change. ‘ Harry ’ Thompson, formerly
of Melbourne, and now of the Daily Mail, has
been asked to take the editorship. Phil has
changed but little, and although he explains a
malformation of the jaw prevents him saying
‘ no,’ is doing better work than ever—judged by
the sketches for the summer (June) number of his
Annual. ”
The-'answer to “Who was the founder of
Sunday journalism in Sydney?” is not W. H. L.
Bailey, I. R. Cohen, or any other of the crowd
named in current controversies. George Howe
and the Botany Bay Governors were the founders.
The Sydney Gazette, the first newspaper at this
end of the world, started as a Saturday, but, by
* order of the Governor and for the convenience of
the printer, was published on Sundays from the
year 1803 to 1843. That is, N.S.W. had a Sunday
journal for 30 years before any of the present
claimants were born.
Fitz Gibbon, of Melbourne, has given birth
to a great idea. The other day there was a
Masonic celebration at the grave of the late Grand
Master, Baronet Clarke, and Fitz arose, and hit
his chest, and spouted much about the deceased
baronet. Finally he got down to his idea : “As
thousands in England gather about the statue of
Lord Beaconsfield on Primrose Day, so, I trust,
will the people of Victoria mark the day when Sir
William was called from their midst.” This is the
climax of Eitz’s folly, but the Poet of the Sewers
is evidently sincere in his respect for the late
Grand Master, or else he would have written a
poem about him.

John M’Elhone, M.P. (N.S.W.), no doubt
died from the indirect effects of the over-exoite-
ment incidental to his Parliamentary career. His
denunciations in debate were invariably delivered
while the speaker was at a white heat of both
mind and body ; his speeches, or rather his pro-
tracted series of yells, could be heard 100 yards
away from the Assembly. The present writer,
when living in Macquarie-street opposite Parlia-
ment House, has more chan once been long kept
awake at night by M’Elhone’s yells, escaping
through open windows right at the other side of
the street.

“ The ha’penny Daily Mail moves into
.£120,000 offices shortly. It threatens to reach a
sale of 750,000 daily if the Spankee war breaks
out, and has started a special office and staff in
New York on the chance,” writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent. “ The present circula-
tion is 440,000, and the new machinery will allow
of this being outputted almost in an hour, at the
rate of 7000 copies a minute. The managing
editor, S. J. Pryor, is 32, and was 10 years in
American journalism, and has profited thereby.
Of the same age are the ‘smartest news editor in
London,’ Lincoln Springfield, and his assistant,
Melbourne ‘Harry’ Thompson, one of the five
Australians or ‘ half - and - halfs ’ on the staff.
George Bull (erstwhile of Sydney Star), one of
the oldest hands, has done well : Crossley, sub-
editor for many years on the S.M. Herald, and
Dr. Rubie, son of Edward Rubie of Freeman’s
Journal and the Express, and Arthur Lynch,
Paris correspondent, are the others. Rubie was
born and bred at Bathurst, and holds the Sydney
University M.A. It may, therefore, be assumed
that the ‘ liveness ’ of the Australian atmosphere
is in some way responsible f<>r the Daily Mail
having the highest advertising rates in England
AN EYE FOR COLOR.
STRANGER : “ What became of that artist chap that was prospecting up these parts? ”
NATIVE : “ Oh, he struck a reef of the yellow stuff, sold out, painted the town red,
blued all his money, and cleared out !”
Gladstone was a great intellect., a splendid
worker, and a good Christian, as ambitious world-
lings go, but nevertheless his cabled nnirmurings
of the Lord’s Prayer on his deathbed remind one
of Labouehere’s bitter gibe at the Grand Old Man
—“ I wouldn’t complain of the cunning old fox
always having an ace up his sleeve, if he didn’t
pretend that God Almighty had put it there.”
The best Gladstone anecdote ? It would
be hard to beat Palmerston’s measurement of his
dialectical skill in the Garibaldi story. “ What
shall we do with Garibaldi ? ” asked a cabinet
colleague in 1848 when the continued presence in
England of the Italian Liberator was becoming
embarrassing. “Oh,” said Pam, “marry him to
the rich Miss X , and then get Gladstone to
explain her away !”
A very well-known N.S.W.
citizen of Irish extraction, who has
been married more than twice, was
asked recently if certain of the chil-
dren were by his present wife.
Oh, no !’’ was the response
“ they are by my future wife.”
An old-time Vandemonian
settler was George Anstey —father
of Chisholm Anstey, a notable of
the British House of Commons 50
years ago. Old Anstey was a man
of one phrase. He summed up the
resources of the English tongue in
his constant ejaculation—“ None
so dusty ! ” Did he like his dinner?
—t’was “ none so dusty.” How
was his health? —“oh, none so
dusty.” On a sermon preached by
Bishop Nixon at Campbell Town
the old chap bestowed bis highest
praise with—“ none so dusty, my
lord—none so dusty.” And even
when he was dying, and the doc-
tor asked him how he felt? he
gasped, “ None —so —dustv ! ” and
passed out.
That celebrated “ tough,”
Bill Nash, was once cock of the
gambling walk in Sydney. He
was an emancipist, and retained all
the habits and language of the
chain-gang. But he had his'vir-
tues, for once when a callous
youth with more money than brains
bad a dicing bout with Bill, the old
sinner, after he had scooped the
youngster’s cash, said to him,
“Look here, my chicken, when I
was a kiddie like you, I did just
•what you have done—l lost my coin
to a sharper. And it made me so
mad that I set to work to learn the
trade. And that sent mo out here.
But your sort could never learn how
to do it It needs an extra-sized
oocoanut to nm the gambling biz.
Besides, you couldn’t do two years
and be the better for it. So, see
here. Give me your word, on the
honor of your mother, that you’ll
never touch the bones again, or the
pictures, or anything else in the
gambling line, and there’s your
money back.” The youth gave
the promise, and kept it; and now,
as an old man, he has kept a kindly
thought for Bill Nash.
Another sample of the working-miner s
pluck. In a Kalgoorlie shaft two mates, Wall
and Symonds, fired a shallow 20in. hole, and got
in the bucket. But the edge caught—did you ever
try being hauled up a shaft in a bucket?—and
Wall fell about 25ft. Symonds signalled first to
stop and then to lower (he was down 225 ft.), and
got to the bottom in time to nip out the fuse and
save his mate. It was all the pluckier because of
the shortness of the fuse and the certainty that at
best he could have only a few seconds to spare.
Numbers of Q. papers keep on repeating
the assertion that T. J. Byrnes is the youngest, of
Queensland Premiers—an assertion which is in-
correct, R. W. Herbert having held that position
at the early age of 28 years and six months. Geo.
Thorne held the position in his 37th year—so
Byrnes’s laurels (in this respect) are slightly faded.
It is, however, perfectly true that Byrnes has been
continuously in possession of a portfolio : true also
that T. J. 13. has attained his Premiership without
ever having sat in the cold shades of Opposition —
a fact that speaks volumes for his luck, but is nob
an absolute voucher for his pugnacity and staying
power. '
A branch of the Westralian Leakes settled
iu Tasmania while it was still known as
Van Diemen’s Land. Bought land on the
Macquarie River, and waxed rich. One scion
of the family, known as “Billy,” was pretty
dotty. Bought COO bonnets once in one lot.
“Come in handy some time,” he remarked
with a knowing Morinonite wink. Another time,
as a wedding-guest, Billy walked in amongst a
flock of ladies in his hostess’s room and calmly
proceeded to take off his riding-breeches. “Don’t
be alarmed, ladies,’’ he observed as the women
scuttled away —“ I’ve got two pair o’ trousers
on !’’ Billy was just one shingle short of a
genius, and, as it was, he wrote “poetry.” and
never had his hair cut.
Gladstone had no saving sense of humor,
and his grave speeches on ridiculous occasion*
provided much easy joking for the Hinglish
“ comic ” papers. Notably the incident of a large
deputation journeying down to Hawarden to
present him with a blackthorn stick. He care-
fully inspected the gift and spake : “It is an
excellent stick, beautifully polished, light to the
hand, and very serviceable to a walker. I would
not like to get a blow from a strong arm with this
stick. Ladies and gentlemen, let us all try to be
as useful in our several capacities as this stick is
in its humble capacity.” The “comics,” of
course, burst forth with imaginary G.O.M. ad-
dresses in acknowledgment of presentation hats,
boots, &e., from his devoted disciples. Glad-
stone held ridicule in contempt, whereas Disraeli
Beaconsfield was keenly conscious of its political
value. Dizzy condescended to snub an inane
jingo—one Tracy Turnerelli —who had procured
through public subscription a chaplet of laurels
wrought in gold for the head of the Tory party.
Gladstone would have accepted the same bauble
in all seriousness. Probably he would have worn
it for a few minutes whilst delivering a discourse
on the signification of laurel-chaplots in ancient
Rome.

Aboriginalities.
T.H.M.: “The best bit of rough-riding I
ever saw was done in the ’6o’s, on Tin-pot station,
Weddin Mountain district, by one Maher. The
yard was full of colts, and he bet a fiver that he’d
ride any of ’em without bridle. He was taken up;
and they picked an old gelding that had killed
two black boys. He was put in the crush, where
Maher mounted him, and got him into a spare
yard. We all thought the rider mad, but the
horse was madder still. Didn’t wait to buck, but
went straight for the outer gate, which he hit full
butt, knocked over, and was off along the road.
We got our horses and followed, and just managed
to keep in sight—or, rather, in sight of the dust—
for 15 miles. By this time the brute had steadied,
and the rider was trying to steer him with a
waddy when we came up. We managed to yard
horse and rider that evening at the Bald Hill sta-
tion, 40 miles from where they started by the
way they took.”

X.Y. : “Talk about ‘living on the pro-
ducts of the land,’ they do it in real earnest up
New England way. A saddler lately settled in a
small N.E. township with a view of establishing
a business, and, after waiting three weeks, his
first ‘ customer ’ entered the shop and paralysed
the leather-man by asking for a pair of stringy-
bark stirrup-leathers. The saddler also noticed,
with further disgust, that his would-be constituent
wore vegetable-marrows with the ends cutoff and
centre cut out by way of leggings 1 ”
• * • *
Pounce : “ The man from wayback has
hard feet. Saw a bushman milking cows one
winter morning, heavy frost on ground. He
scorned boots ; Nature’s covering suited him.
Bailed up strawberry cow, butted his head firmly
against her body, and started work. Cow kicked
and then placed her hind hoof square on foot of
milkman, who calmly milked on, and, when done,
pulled his foot by main force from under hoof of
cow, hitting same against fence to restore circula-
tion. Then he whistled, and tackled the next
cow.
* * * *
When Robert Simson ran his station on
the lower Boyne (P. Curtis), the hands had rather
a good time. A favorite occupation of theirs
when the old man was away was shark-fishing
near the mouth of the river. One day, after doing
a little branding at the yards, they adjourned to
the river close by to indulge in their customary
recreation. When three or four sharks had been
caught, one of the party, inspired by the devil of
cruelty and mischief, heated the branding-irons,
clapped the station-brand on the twisting brutes,
and then, with the help of his mates, turned the
branded fish back into the river. A month or so
after, a newly-arrived jackeroo, while fishing on a
"lay, hauled out a voung shark and found, to

Jvosciusko: Ke Jinda on the extremes
of cold on Kosciusko and the Snowies. I was in
Kiandra in ’9O to see the snow-shoe races. Snow
was only about 2ft. deep in the open, but the
recorded temperatures were very low, and rarely
as high as 32deg. at mid-day. The New-Chum
elevated tail-race beside the town was fringed with
icicles 10 to 15ft. long and several feet thick.
Early in the mornings the telegraph wires were so
thickly coated with frost that they were the size
of a ship’s hawser. One night when the tempera-
ture was 48deg. below freezing point all the fowls
in town died and remained rigidly frozen to their
perches until a thaw set in a couple of weeks later.
All the beer and stout in the hotel was frozen, and
the boys told me that in severe winters even the
spirits froze, and the barman would then knock the
heads of his barrels in and serve out drinks by
chopping a sliver of rum or whisky off with a
tomahawk. This practice of chewing one’s drink
is said to fully account for the fact that all the old
hands in the locality have their teeth worn to
stumps.

S.S. : “Read a par. in ‘ Aboriginalitiea ’
recently, in which it was declared that pigs are
proof against the bite of a snake. Evidence to
the contrary : A Drouin (Vic.) farmer tells of the
destruction of five pigs in two days ; cause, black
snake or snakes. The bereaved agriculturist saw
the reptile dart his head from under the feed-
trough, and fix his last victim with a nip on the
snout or under lip.”
• * • •
So remarkably good has the season so far
been in S.W. Queensland and far N.W. N.S.
Wales that many stations west of the Darling and
Paroo have tidy stacks of Mitchell and other
native grasses; and the question is discussed
whether something can’t be done systematically
towards cultivating them. But, unfortunately,
the trail of the bank and the mortgage co. is
heavy over that part, and about as much is likely
to come of the discussion as of the long-talked-of
and very necessary cultivation of the edible
bushes and scrub.

Dear B.—Please shut down on your
jackass-cam-snake controversy, for it is under-
mining the faith of Young Australia in his lesson-
books. On page 53, 2nd class reading-book, he is
told : “It (the goburra) also kills young snakes
whenever it can catch them, and so prevents those
dangerous reptiles from becoming too numerous.”
-P.
* * • *
Ed. Bulletin.— Re that good old fly-bung
subject, let me state with certainty that the fly
does not sting; the mischief is caused by a small
red-colored louse upon the body of the fly. This
parasite is thickly covered with minute spines,
contact of which with the eye causes the “bung."
The N.W. aborigines settled the point for me long
ago.—Bulger, Roebourne, W.A.

The Brisbane clergy (or at least a section
of them) seem to be rather jealous of the success
attending Q. Govt’s late treatment of aboriginals.
There is too much unholy, secular simplicity
about the Mestonian scheme of drifting tne un-
happy remnant of the nigs, off to an island and
allowing them there to lead the natural life of
savages as they did before our advent. Parson-
dom wants permission to run a farm of 128 acres
with the aid of aboriginals rescued from the claws

of Satan. If this be not done there is danger the
blacks may actually drift bade to the deplorable
condition they were in before the arrival of the
“Blessed Gawspel.” And the Q. Home Secre-

tonian or pagan system in favor of the system par-
sonic, wherefore the hapless Q. aboriginal will
continue to shoot cheerfully down to Sheol in the

Spot: “ lie black cockatoos’ nests. In
’BO, at Station Creek, a tributary of Tower Hill
Creek, a black showed me a nest in the hollow of
a gum-tree. The hole was not more than Gin.
deep, and there was one young bird in it. The
Barcoo niggers in those days often got nests, but
never containing more than one young bird.”
* * * *
Boiardo : “Re the Q. black cockatoo, I
have found several nests with young in them on the
Lower Barcoo, between Isisford and Stony Point.
They build in hollow sprouts, much the .same as
galahs, and other parrots. Very hard to rear the
young ones, have tried it several times, but about
six weeks was the longest time I had one to live.
Never found their nests anywhere else.”
* * * *
“ In the old days in the bush on the Man-
chester Block the mosquitoes were so numerous
that the men employed in felling the forest used
to catch them in handfuls, roll them up into balls,
squeeze the juice out, and afterwards use the
liquid, diluted with a little gin, as a cure for
sprains or bruises.” — M.L. print.

W. y. M‘C. : “ ‘Some more’ bigger loads
of wool. In ’93 a bullocky brought 14 tons odd of
wool from Gurley Station to Narrabri, and, next
season, the ‘ carrion choppers ’ (horse-drivers), not
to be outdone, sent one of their number in with
17 tons odd from Barton’s Burren Station, pulling
it through the Namoi River at ‘ Mollie ’ crossing.
A few weeks later Dick Turbot—bullocky again—
landed in with 18 tons odd from Edgeroi Station.
Then Tom Scott’s horse-team loaded up 20 tons at
Gurley, and pulled it until his waggon, anew
broad-tyre, broke down.”

Snell : “ The best and most stirring bun-
yip-scare I ever struck was in the vicinity of a
small Tas. mining town. For three or four nights
there had been strange talk of a horrible monster
in the vicinity of Bullfrog Lagoon, a swamp sur-
rounded by scrub and timber. Several people had
heard its bellowings, and one or two had come
running into the township, wild-eyed and grey
with horror, stuttering frantic yarns about a white
beast as big as a horse, with a long, black
head like a hippopotamus, seen on the edge of
the lagoon. The last of these induced a small
party to take guns, and start a bunyip-hunt at
midnight. I chipped in, and after an hour’s
walking and another hour’s hunting a muffled
bellowing and a yell from Angus brought us all
together, breathless, at the water’s edge.
There stood Angus, paralytic with various
emotions, pointing a trembling hand across
the swamp towards a weird white animal dis-
tinctly visible in the moonlight, standing knee-
deep in the water, and tossing its barrel-shaped
head up and down, whilst uttering a blood-
curdling call and breathing smoke. Two guns
went up. * Don't shoot! ’ gasped Angus. ‘ Take
him alive, and our fortune’s made.’ One remained
to watch : the rest started to stalk the bunyip.
We stalked with such care that we were over an
hour and a-half getting near enough to the beast
to discover that it was a cow. The animal’s head
was jammed in a powder-keg that somebody had
used as a flour-barrel; she was covered with flour,
and when she ‘mooed’ with more than ordinary
vigor still blew a respectable cloud. We removed
the unfortunate cow’s muzzle, and went sadly and
silently home.”
Face: “The only Australian tobacco
growers who really knock a living out of the game
are the Chinese—and not many of them. Also, it
is a very poor living. The common or “cabbage’’
Chow is a bloated aristocrat compared with the
tobacco Chinaman, who mostly subsists on rice
and ’possum, and sleeps up a hollow log.
Even with this, it takes all a Chinaman
knows to make it pay. In Riverina, the
writer has seen the Chow, after he had
cut and hung the tobacco-leaf, sprinkle it
over, first with water, then with dust.
Many dustings on a few thousand
leaves puts a bit of weight
on, and the smoker has a
little dirt to flavor his
“ toby.”
Johnson was an old
campaigner passing his last
few years on a 20- acre-
clearing patch out Binda-
roo way. One day he
mounted his nag and rode
off for a week without in-
forming the neighbors of
his intention. A blazing
log set the hut a-fire an
hour or two after his de-
parture, and by the time
folks arrived to extinguish
the conflagration the place
was in ashes. Where was
Johnson ? Careful search
among the ashes brought
to light a number of bones
and buttons—and, as John-
son must have had bones
and buttons of his own, it
was decided these were the
remnants of Johnson. Lo-
cally-empannelled jury de-
cided further it was a case
of accidental death by
burning. Bones were ac-
cordingly buried, and a
collection taken up to de-
fray funeral charges. . . .
Johnson rode back just in
time to be asked to give
a sub. to bury himself de-
cently.

* * * *
Another fact re the eternal tick question.
A tick-infested cow will lie down, and imme-
diately the fowls will make a rush at her and
commence to gobble up the pests with which she
is infested. Fond of them ? Should think they
are. Won’t touch maize while ticks are plentiful.
* * • *
Nil: “ Have any of your bush naturalists
noticed that the great majority of calls of Aus-
tralian animals are produced in duo by two of the
same species (male and female) ? The cross-cut-
saw-like cry of the koala (native bear), the ‘ ehee-
cheedle’ of the peewit, the ‘t’zeek, tz-z-zeek ! ’ of
the grasshopper, and the very similar sound of the
big cicada are all examples. ’.Possums also vive
alternate cries ; and the familiar call of the land
* curlew ’ is really the combined notes of two indi-
viduals.”
* * * *
A cheerful correspondent from up Mun-
gundi way :
Dear Bulletin. — I’ve been up at Mungundi, and now
proceed to let down the tailboard of my intellect. Mun-
gundi stands—or rather staggers—on both sides of the
Barwon. It is, therefore, divided by water, but that
element never otherwise causes dissension there. It con-
sists, principally, of five pubs, and a brewery and two
stores. So much sack to so little bread ! It is the cham-
pion drinking town of the district—and the dustiest.
Everything is dusty in Mungundi-'-houses, timber and
people. All but the girls. They’re not so dusty. One
half of Mungundi gets drunk one week and the remainder
next week. That’s when no one is in with a cheque.
Then, the collective manhood of Mungundi arises in its
mighty thirst, leaves “the missus’’ to shepherd things
and abandons itself to a blissful delirium and booze,
until the new-comer is reduced • to chalking up “ Jimmy
Woodsers” on his own. The biggest cheque ever taken
into Mungundi melts in a week. If it lasts so long, the
Govt, usually provides the coffin. The programme of the
visitor with a big cheque, is Ist day, drunk ; 2nd, very
drunk : 3rd, beastly drunk ; 4th, jim-jams ; sth, jumping
jim-jams ; 6th, assorted jim-jams with streaks of sheol;
7th, Planted. One fate meets all visitors ;in one sense or
the other they are soon stiff. There is a legend that one Q.
drover, whose inn’ards had been tanned by Barclay table-
land home made grog, saw a cheque through for a fort-
night in Mungundi, and then won, first, £IOO, and then
a citizen’s wife, at two-up. But this is a mere fable ; not
only because no Mungundi citizen would have £IOO while
there was a pub in the country, but also because his
calm sagacity would prompt him to stake the old woman
first. This slander is attributed to the malignant jealousy
of Collarendabri—a one-dog township down rhe river,
that can t raise a decent drunk—even on Sundays. Why,
they never had but one case of the horrors there in five
years, and he was a Mungundi man ! In Coll., etc., they
only get up twice a week, whilst at Mungundi they seldom
go to bed at all.

“I thought I had a Stamp.”
Many inquiries having been made at this office foi
the above humorous picture by “ Hop ” which
appeared in The Bulletin as a coloured supple-
ment some years ago (and which has been long out
of print) Mr. Hopkins has, by special permission of
The Bulletin, executed an etching of the work,
and a limited number of signed artist’s proofs
may now be had only on application to this
office. The proofs are printed on Whatman’s
“Creswick ” drawing paper, 14 x inches ; size
of plate, x 12. As every detail has been carried
out by the artist himself (including the printing)
these proofs have all the value of an original
drawing. This will be an exceptional opportunity
to possess an example of “ Hop’s ” work, which is
rarely to be seen outside the pages of this paper.

The Australian Star, in its special anti-
Billious issue of May 18, fired off once more the
old, bald assertion that “ everything points to the
Federal capital being in Victoria.” Everybody
knew perfectly well, from the day the Bill was
drafted, that the Federal capital wouldn’t be m
Victoria nor in any of the other states, because it
is expressly provided that the place selected is to
be taken from the province which now owns it
and made into a separate Federal territory. But
the good old lie still goes round just the same.
C.C. : Soon after my arrival in Newcastle
some years ago, I was “ doing the shipping for a
local paper when an old square-at-both-ends dere-
lict was hauled under the cranes to load sudden
death for ’Frisco. The first truck-load dropped
into her with a dull, sickening thud, instead of
being lowered gently into the hold, and up popped
a red-faced ruffian above the bulwarks. “Good
Gord, haul tight ! ” he yelled, shaking his fist at
the crane-boy. “ D’ye want to drop the coal right
through the blanky old hooker!” “ Keep yer
wool on, ole man,” retorted the crane-boy, , it s
all large coal, so yer needn’t be ’fraid o’ the
screenin’s gitting through ’er.” I passed on,_
humming— u make the punishment fit the crime.

considering the possibility of reclaiming Bondi

“ Ishmael Dare ” writeajapropos Lawson
and his book of prose sketches .
This is the book of Australia-the man who claims that
name, not from an accident of birth, but because his
whole life and character have been shaped in spit* of
himself by the land that owns him. The Britisher, to
use a convenient Yankeeism-is much the same all the
world over as long as he is the master, dominating and
taming the new land of which he has taken possession
In minor habits, in the use of new’ varieties of colloquial
speech the man of Johannesburg may differ from the
Victori’an, the Anglo-Indian from the frosted Ourack,
but they are all the same “ Britisher at bottom. It is
when the land takes possession of the man that differen-
tiation really begins. And Australia, the land of hug ,
unvarying distances, of sheep by the thousand and
rabbits by the million, where a sudden drought brings
the millions and the thousands down to hundreds and
tens and a good fall of rain runs them up into
thousands and millions again-where the main range
with its barren level ridges typifies the worst
side of democracy, every hummock as high as another
and none really high-the “ reckling of the continents,
the gawky, long-limbed, red-haired child that nature m
her shame kept out of society as long as possible—Aus
tralia has wound these long arms round many a man
w'hom physical weakness or dull intellect has handicapped
against his fellows, and has made of him the swagman,
the sundowner, the spieler, the unique Australian type of
failure. And here we have him set down at last by a
man w'ho knows him. Globe trotters have noticed him,
sometimes as a picturesque object (which he isn t very
often), sometimes as a moral lesson. He is a drunicara
and a gambler-he has very little moral stamina-he is a
God-forgetting scoundrel in a God-forgotten country.
The "-lobe-trotter sees him and moralises : also he writes
about him, and gets good money therefor-but he doesn t
think of paving his model. But the globe-trotter has
never even' seen, has never had the most remote
experience of the conditions under which he lives.
The great plains swallow him up, and their mono-
tony deadens his brain more than all the liquor he drinks
—delirium tremens has at least the saving grace of variety.
The burning sun evaporates his conscience, and the soak-
ing storms when they come wash all the morals out of
him-he survives only because he is thoroughly adapted
to his environment, and has a wild cat s tenacity of
life. Small wonder if he has in him a good deal
else that is animal-not much encumbered by finer
feelings.” But virtue does not leave him because
it is virtuous. Such virtues as will help mm in
the struggle remain to him, and increase mightily.
And the greatest of them is the virtue of mateship.
“ Gawd send us a trusty chum 1” says Kipling s typical
Tommy. The swagman’s chum is not always as trusty as
he might be, hut the swagman knows howto allow for
that; and, after all, it is rarely that the most un-moral of
swagmen cheats his mate. It is this mateship, broaden-
ing to include all fellows, that for a long tune
strengthened and made impervious to the squatter s soft
speeches the unionism of the hush trades-unions. In its
more personal form it is tha chief, and often the only,
force making for good in the life of the regions Out Back.
Just there it is as well to make one point quite clear.
We have called this swagman-type the typical Australian
native. Let it be distinctly understood that he is typical,
not of the mass of the population, but of the influence of
the country. He is the Australian as the kangaroo is,
not as mutton is Australian ; but even now the sheep
vastly exceed the kangaroo in number. His qualities are
the typical Australian qualities : wherever you find men
country-bred, shaped at least partially by the typical
Australian environment, there you find the drunkenness,
the easy-going gambling propensity, the comradeship
that at best is heroism and at worst “chivalry upside-
down.” The townfolk are of the everyday British
pattern less eduoated, perhaps (their opportunities
have been fewer) than
the British middle - class
—less hide-bound, too, and
less coherent in their
familv life—but not essen-
tially’ different, from their
relations at home by reason
of the land they live in.
But on bushmen the bush
leaves its mark, and most
deeplv, most untnistakeably
on the weak, the shiftless,
the failures. I have spoken
of Australia as a gawky child.
But a gawky child may have
good eyes, and may, more-
over, grow up into an alto-
gether splendid woman. The
land is not all bush, not all
Out-Back. But its beauties
—and it lias them—go about
four pennyweight to the ton.
So do its great men at pre-
sent. We hope with confi-
dence that the gawky child
will some day carve great
statues in marble; at pre-
sent she is modelling in very
soft putty. And this book
is the photograph-album of
her models.
Legal verbosity dies
slow. In the M. L.
‘ ‘Coroner’s Handy Book, ”
by Judge Johnston (1868)
one finds “useful forms”
for verdicts. As, a man
being blown up with an
engine, the jury are di-
rected to find that “ the
said (boiler) burst and ex-
ploded, whereby a great
quantity (to wit) 500 gal-
lons of the boiling and
scalding water and steam
then being within the
cavity of the said boiler,
and a large quantity
(to wit) five bushels of hot
and burning coals, &c.
. . . accidentally, casu-
ally, and by misfortune,”
&c.
The biggest bottle-and-rag business in
Melbourne—it hires carts to about 30 men— is
run and managed entirely by a woman, and a
mighty smart woman too.
Why don’t magistrates know something of
the rudiments of law ? Magistrate Addison, of
Sydney, the other day indignantly asked an able-
bodied vagrant why he didn’t “ volunteer to go to
the war.” And if he did volunteer the Foreign
Enlistment Act provides for putting him in gaol
for helping to shoot at the subjects of a friendly
State.
Indian papers relate how, when a moun-
tain battery was shelling a rebel position, one of
the enemy kept signalling in regular practice-
fashion—“ High, to the right,” and so on; and
even when the guns were trained on to the signaller
himself he continued his derisive messages, until
a shell exploded so near him that the kieked-up
dirt knocked him over. But the moment he
got up he signalled—“ Position untenable—About
to retire,” which he promptly did. Of course, a
deserter—and a humorist!
“Woe to a land whose king is a child.
Spain !
The “ Sporting Reminiscences,” mostly
Melbournian, of Thomas Haydon are not entirely
the fault of Ethel Haydon’s papa. It seems,
from the preface, that various people at divers
times egged Thomas on to do this lamentable
deed, and at last he “ yielded to the persuasive
eloquence of an old friend,” and dedicated^ his
volume “(by permission) most respectfully to
Lord Hopetoun. The author’s gratitude for his
Lordship’s permission is evidenced on page 17:
Lord Hopetoun, however, eclipsed all his predecessors
in the matter of style and pomp, his carnage, with four
magnificent horses and postillions, being quite a novelty
to many of the colonial born. . . . A more genial
host never presided at a banqueting table, and both ne
and the Countess were positively beloved by every one
who had a drop of sporting blood in their veins. . . •
Lord and Lady Hopetoun’s arrival in and departure
from Melbourne will never be forgotten by those who
witnessed those quite State ceremonials.
Tom Haydon tells scarcely anything worth hear-
ing, and he tells it in labored journalese. His
“ Reminiscences ” at the best are dry chestnuts,
at the worst they are babble
Flemington racecourse is, without doubt, one of the
show places of Melbourne, and justly so. . . . A horse,
now in England, called Paris 111. distinguished himself
by winning the Caulfield Cup twice, in ’92 and 94, carry-
in" on the last occasion the very respectable impost oi
9st. 41b. ... A more charming personage than Mr.
Dailey it would be impossible to meet. He was affability
itself, and I treasure up in my memory the hour I spent
with him, discussing various topics over an excellent
bottle of claret, in his bijou residence overlooking
Sydney Harbour, &c., &c.
For a man who was onetime secretary of the Vic.
Trotting Club, worthy Haydon has about the
smallest fund of anecdote on record. He states
facts and chronicles circumstances (which are
seldom old enough to be newly interesting to Aus-
tralians) after the manner of a dull globetrotter
imparting information to his friends at Ome.
He deals with two sudden deaths in paragraphs
that, in his own way of speaking, have ‘ seldom
been equalled and never surpassed by any author
of a well-bound book
I must not omit to mention a most tragic aim un-
looked-for finale which occured at the Princess one night
It was on the occasion of the representation of laust,
wherein a most capable vocalist and actor named Fredencl
(Federici, by the wav) met his fate. The opera had gone
particularly well this evening (first night of the produc-
tion by the wav), and Frederici, as Mephisto, excelled,
himself, but his final disappearance down the trap proved
his final appearance on any stage, as he fell dead when he
reached the basement, a heart affection, I believe, being
the cause.
Then the paragraph describing the Caulfield Cup
catastrophe of 1885 :
Women shrieked and fainted as, one after another, the
injured jockeys were carried to the casualcy-room. How-
ever plenty of medical assistance was at hand, but when
the roll was called one poor lad, Donald Nicholson, failed
to answer the summons. He was killed outright. He
was a respectable and much-respected jockey, and his
death cast a gloom over the subsequent proceedings,
which, unfortunately, did not interest him any more.
Ye gods 1

MISSING AT LLOYD’S.
The old vessel Anti-Bill, which was lost on an inhospitable coast during a severe storm on the night of 3rd June, 1898. There was no insurance, the vessel being considered
an unsafe risk.


THE N.S.W. GREASY PIG.


Out-Back postmasters have some very try-
ing experiences (writes a Bulletin correspondent).
Instance : a teamster lately bowled up to our local
P. 0., handed in a “ way-bill ” showing him to
have six tons of loading on board for Narrabri,
and explained that, as he wished his wife to have
the document “ immediately,” he wanted it sent
by telegram. On being informed that such a
thing was not possible, he roared out, “ How ther
(etcetera’9) that? Aren’t I sent cheques an’
money-orders by telegram, an’ if I kin sen’ them
why carnt I sen’ this ’ere thing?” About
half-an-hour’s explanation had no effect on him ;
he departed cursing the unfortunate postmaster
for a “lazy blanky disobliging loafer.”
C.H.R. : “ Writer came across some
papers, the other day, connected with a curious
incident which happened at Woolmers, Longford,
Tas., many years ago. Thomas Archer, owner of
Woolmers, his wife and niece, with James Hortle,
Superintendent of Police, were startled one night
to bear a whistle at the door of the room in which
they sat, followed by a voice which said ‘Joe
Wilders ! ’ This occurred twice. The premises
were searched thoroughly without success. Next
morning the body of Wilders was found pinned to
the ground by a honeysuckle limb where he had
been cutting wood the previous clay. That he
had lived some hours was evidenced by the holes
scratched in the turf by his feet and hands. Now,
who whistled ? ”

The opponents of the Bill in N.S.W. :
Wealth and Want.
Pontius Pilate Reid (loq.) : “I am inno-
cent of the blood of this . . . man.”
Premier Reid’s attitude is that the Federal
Bill is a very had Bill on account of the things he
helped to put in it, and he is going to vote for it,
and implores the public not to do likewise.
Riots in Italy and Spain ! What are they
to the war-riots that might break out in England ?
Its growth of wheat, as Mu 11 hall shows, only suffices
for two months’ supply in the year, and for ten
Eon ths it is dependent on the wheat of America,
ussia and other places.
All Yankee'opinion doesn’t advocate the
war. N.Y. Life asks why the States don’t hib :
somebody their own size ; and N.Y. Critic poeti-
cally accuses Uncle Sam, young and .strong, of
bullying “a.stoop’d and graybeard foe.” Which
is mighty near the truth of it.
Vic. ; Our civil-servants are in a pelter
over Federation. Even those ostensibly not to be
moved are frightened, because it is said that the
host who are to be ousted will be provided for
soinp’now. The Civil Service Association, a per-
fect Vehmgericht. of secresy, is understood to have
ukased No.
At Bathurwc last Saturday, Premier Reid
told his audience that civil-service reform was
rendered necessary “ through the introduction of
utterly unfit persons into the service by the
Governments which, preceded us.” Which sets
one thinking of the famous occasion when Reid
laid before Parliament his bogus list of Dikb;’?
Civil service appointments, and the exposure which
followed, and the painful spectacle of a fat
politician agreeing to take it all back, and admit
that it was a mistake, or a mean slander, or
almost anything, .so long as the subject was
dropped.
The present
seems to offer a
great opportunity-'
to Charles Powers,
ex-Q. leader of Op-
position, now in re-
tirement. Bowers
was Pos.tmas'ter-
Gerier al in the
short -lived More-
head ministry and
is young,’of'regu-
lar habits, fluent
speech almost as
bad to report as
Gladstone much
ability and genuine
patriotism. He im-
poverished himself
and lost a tine Bris-
bane practice by al-
liance with Drake
and the Democrats when he might ensily
•have secured a portfolio from the' Boodlers.
In 1896 lie abandoned polities in disgust and
settled in Bupdaherg to practice his profes-
sion. Now everyone, even the Worker and
the Courier, are calling upon Powers to save
Queensland from chaos and a free-lance Op-
position. Thoso who know Powers best say
• that lie is enthusiastic to again enter the
political fray, hut a large and growing family
and an attenuated exchequer hold him back.
The chief weakness of the Spanish
military organisation is the boy officer of
“aristocratic” birth—brave enough on the
“fools step in,” etc., principle, but obviously
useless as an organiser. In the main, these
officer boys are responsible for the whole pre-
sent trouble. The war in Cuba meant full
pay, and it was their game to perpetuate the
struggle. Quite possibly the Maine episode
was instigated by some of them. Execrated and
irreconcileable Weyler was the officer-hoy’s idol.
S.A. has three large tobacco firms, and
two of them are spending much money to upset
the Convention Bill. Another peculiar circum-
stance is that the S.A. Oppositionists are being
strongly supported by the Hebrew community,
who have provided a good proportion of the cam-
paign funds.
Premier Retd, who complains about the
large Federal representation to be granted the
smaller provinces, voted for the proposal, tu give
all the provinces the sfime number of senators,
which item is the cause of the trouble. In fact,
Reid voted for most of the things that he is now
most indignant about. - . - ,

At N.S.W. Local Op-
tion League’s recent annual
meeting, Postmaster-Gene-
ral Joe Cook again boosted
the good- old fallacy thab
everything socially amiss is
traceable to the accursed
drink. It never seems to
occur to people like the
smug Joseph that the more
contemptible sort of crimes
are nearly al ways those qf
the outwardly strict liver,
or that the most hideous .
conceivable immorality has
always been rife amongst
non - drinking nations. If
the world had never known
the taste of alcohol it might have gained a lot
more holy sneaks, hut it would hardly have lost
one of its monumental criminals.
N.S. Wales Opposition-leader and anti-
Bill Lyne will have some difficulty in find-
ing and recombining the shattered party which
followed him a few months ago. Protectionists
see more chance of a permanent settlement of the
fiscal issue by Federation than in a three years
tenure of a Lyne-Schey administration, with the
probable bonleversement at the end.
No party is more thoroughly split on the
Federal issue than the Single.- 1 taxers, which is all
the more singular because, up to date, they have
contrived to keep remarkably solid. The Vies.,
led by Hirsch, are strongly for the Bill ; and a
good part of their Beacon is devoted now to
showing how hopelessly befogged are their S.A.
and N.S.W. “comrades,” who are in opposition.
Since the press attracted attention to
the recent gigantic grab of Papuan territory by
Bowles, M.P., and Somers Vine, the visit of ex-
Prenr.ier Nelson and Gov. Lamington to Gov.
William Mac-gregor has a fresh significance.
WAY-BACK REPARTEE.
HARDFACE (to the red-faced, fat, new landlord): “Say, boss, it's the
usual thing for the new landlord to shout."
NEW LANDLORD : “ Take me for a chump, old man? Do I look it?"
HARDFACE : “ Blime, no! You. looks like a cove wot'd drink all your
own beer-engine slops."
Premier Reid, in his recent speech at
Bathurst, told the people that Braddon’s clause,
which provides that three-fourths of the Federal
customs and excise revenue are to be returned to
the States, “.will compel the Commonwealth
Treasurer to raise four times the amount of the
Commonwealth expenditure because he will have
to return three-fourths of it to the States.” H re
Reid, assumes that the Federal Treasurer must pay
all his expenses out of customs and excise, which
is ludicrously absurd. A few weeks ago, in his
Sydney speech, Reid informed his audience that
the .Federal Treasurer would most probably prefer
to pay his expenses out of direct taxation, because
if he wanted- .£250,000 he could simply raise
£250,000 by direct taxes and keep the lot, whereas
to fill the same gap out of customs and excise he
would need to raise £l,ooo,ooo—the other £750,000
having to be paid to the States, '): Will the slip-
pery politician kindly explain himself?
The assurances of Reid,
Heydon, Want, and others,
that if N.S.W. will only
reject the present chance
of Federation they will start
another Federation Con-
vention immediately, evok
ed a rather sour reply fr .
Premier Turner, of Victo-
ria. Turner says, in effect,
that N.S.W. wrecked the
Federal Council movement
by stan ding out; and
ruined the .’9l Federation
scheme, which the other
provinces took up at her
request, by dropping it
without explanation'. And,
if N.S.W. wrecks, this one
(also taken up at her in-
vitation), Turner reckons
that the other provinces
will regard the next invi-
tation from the same quar-
ter as a wild Cornstalk
joke, and will turn'the deaf
ear with the plug of cotton
in it.
The famous Braddon clause did not, as
the anti-Bills allege, get into the Draft Constitu-
tion by being hustled through a sleepy. House at
4 a.m. It was certainly first considered and
accepted at 4 a.m. Then it was reconsidered and
debated at length when the Convention metagain,
and again it was accepted. Also, it was discussed
again, and again affirmed four days later. Lyne
supported it all through, so the plea of that emin-
ently slow gentleman that he voted for it in a
moment of surprise won’t stand dose inspection.
From all accounts, Q. Premier Byrnes is
determined to buy popularity at any price. Dur-
ing his triumphant Northern tour he promised
to build railways, breakwaters, piers, harbors,
colossal public buildings, to sink a myriad of bores
clean through the earth if necessary, to subsidise
every imaginable industry, increase local-govern-
ment endowments, hospital subsidies, and civil-
service salaries —in short, if Toinjoe carries out
half his reported promises, he will raise Queens-
land’s yearly interest bill to something like the
size of the British National debt. Tomjoe has
clearly overdone it, and the reaction threatens to
politically stiffen him.
J. H. Want resigned the N.S.W. Attorney-
Generalship to. as he said, save his honor. -His
chief had announced a determination to vote for
the Commonwealth Bill, and as Want intended to
speak and vote against the Bill, he considered that
the honorable tradition’s. of Parliamentary col-
leagueship compelled him to come out from under
Reid s wing. To-day,. however, we have Cook
and Gould and Young, other members of the Reid
Ministry, cursing the Bill from Dan to Bersheeba,
and calling on the country to blast it at the ballot-
box, and yet wholly untroubled by the scruples
which drove Jack Want abroad. Is it because
,they liavo less “honor}” and more sense-or
because, if they let go a good thing, they know
they have not Jack’s chance of getting it back ?
The war has been going on a good while
now, and the United States’ army is still striving
to get out of chaos, and it seems no nearer being
prepared for invasion than it was at the beginning.
If the enemy had been a rich military and naval
power, like France or Germany, it would pro-
bably have dumped 200,000 soldiers down on top
of the huge, chaotic, unready republic by this
time and (jccupied every big city on the Atlantio
coast. The United States’ alleged preparedness
for war seems to be about a dozen times as con-
fused and helpless as the unpreparedness of the
French Empire in 1870. The courage of the
Americans no one doubts, but when a country
which hasn’t seen war for 33 years, or run against
a military State for over 80 years, starts business
with one or two hundred thousand raw militia
and recruits, the results are apt to be very weird.
Somers Vine’s Papuan land-grab syndi-
cators no doubt consider themselves a modest mob
of Bulls compared to other land-grab co’s known
to Australian history. Vine asks 250,000 acres,
and is willing to pay 2s. per acre. He stipulates
for other valuable considerations, it is true, but,
looking to the land and stated price, and looking
back at the almost successfully engineered Wes-
tralian land-grab of 1825, Vine must think he has
acted quite handsomely. At the date mentioned,
a company, headed by Pell, Vincent and Schenley,
presented a memorial to the British Colonial Office
asking 4,000,000 acres of Westralia for Is. 6d. an
acre. The co. engaged to settle 10,000 immigrants
on the land, but, in the working of the scheme,
this would have further redounded to the co’s
profit. The proposal was actually assented to by
the British Government in 1828, but, owing to a
flood of criticism, was afterwards so modified that
in 1829 the project was abandoned by the origi-
nators.
In Sydney, a week or two ago, Jack Want
urged that there should be a Federation of
N.S.W., Queensland and Victoria only, with the
three allegedly rapacious small States left out.
Up in the North, however, the same Want has
lately been warning the sugar-growers that
Federation will ruin their industry, because
Queensland will come in later on, and then the
nigger-grown Queensland sugar will have free
access to N.S.W., and will wipe them out. At
the same time Want was a member of the Reid
Ministry which voted to let Queensland’s nigger-
grown sugar have free access to N.S.W. even
without Federation. And all this- time Want’s
friends in Sydney are yelling that it will be fatal
to N.S.W. if she federates without her “natural
ally, Queensland.” Also, a lot of these same
people damned Federation in ’9l because Queens-
land was in the movement, and the idea of letting
aTory and nigger-loving province have a hand in
framing the Consitution was too utterly horrible.
The grand old Hinglish example cropped
up at last in the Federal discussion. David Storey,
M.L.A., implores the people of Australia to
reject the Bill,and wait till they can get “ruleby
the majority ” in the good old British way. In
the good old British way Ireland has one member
for, every 45,600 people, and England only one for
every 60,000 ; and 1637 electors have one member
in Kilkenny, while 15,151 have also one member
at Chester-le-Street, and half the political power
rests'with the House of Lords, which is wholly
independent of the popular majority, and where

“Georgeo Reidium." The new chameleon can,
beside changing its color, extend the tongue to some
considerable length, thus striking its prey with
astonishing quickness.
the qualification for membership consists in being
the last member’s- eldest son. As some demo-
cratic satirist once wrote: —
There was an ocean pilot and his eldest son was blind,
And deaf and dumb from childhood, likewise vacant in
his mind.
But of course he was a pilot when his daddy’s couise was
run,
And navigated vessels as his father’s eldest son.
There was a learned justice who sent criminals to gaol;
He’d ah eldest son —a forger vvho'd absconded from his
bail;
But when the Judge went upwards through a tin-tack in
a bun
His vacant place was taken by his outlawed eldest son.
David Storey, M.L.A., actually wants Australia
to wait till: it can get the kind of majority rule
they have in the country which is bossed by the
House of Eldest Sons ! .Rabbits ! !

Many enquiries are made to us, asking if our guarantee
covers American or Canadian Dunlops, and we again
beg to inform the public that we do not guarantee
these tyres, owing to their being too light, and there-

roads. We have found that tyres, to give satisfaction in
the colonies, must be manufactured of heavier material
than those used elsewhere. We now make all tyres
specially adapted for colonial roads, and have the utmost
confidence in guaranteeing for twelve months all roadster
Dunlop covers, and roadster Dunlop tubes that bear the
above trade mark ; but in no case does our guarantee
apply unless the tyre bears our trade mark. Claims
under this guarantee should be made direct to any of the
Company’s Branches.

The evils attendant on the presence of the
casual boarder in a married couple’s house— that is
an old story. But when the casual boarder is a
“brother” in the Cause, or anything like that,
his virulence is immeasurable. For instance,
Collins, who got such a gruelling front Justice
a’Beckett in Vic. divorce-court, was a grand speci-
men of the tearful, religious hypocrite. He was
named as co-respondent in the case of Middleton
v. Middleton, and, although there was no appear-
ance of the wife, Collins turned up and raised the
defence of contributory negligence on the part of
the husband. Collins sat opposite his counsel
during the proceedings; and when he wasn’t
whining in the lawyer’s ear he was gazing at the
Judge with his eyes full of crocodile tears and his
hands uplifted in the attitude of supplication. In
his evidence he detailed how he had been seduced
by the woman whilst she was sewing a button on
his coat. He did this in a way that, had the_ op-
portunity offered, would have won the best kicks
of everyone in court, from Judge to the Agb
copy-messenger. Here is a beautiful letter written
by Mr. Collins aforesaid :—
Dear Brother Horace, —I hardly know how to address
you. I am so sorry for you. and the great wrong that
has been done you, and so ashamed of my vile flesh and
weakness that I hardly know what to do or what to say.
I did not know exactly what Brother J. and you referred
to in your notes ; although I knew my great, guilt, I did
not want to admit more than Sister M. had said, so that,
if possible, I could shield her. Now, I know that she
has told you all ; and, oh, Horace, I am so sorry that
there ever was anything to tell. You now (sic) the rest,
know what a vilely passionate man I am, and Sister M.
is passionate also. It all came about without the slightest
premeditation. I did not set out to win her from yon,
and have time and again spoken for you. Ask her if
this is not true. I have never attempted to poison her
mind against you; never. Oh, Horace, sometimes she,
sometimes I, and again would both determine that we
w > dd, transgress no more. I have never broken bread
smce that I have not asked God to forgive us, and resolved
that I Would not sin again. But my resolves were as pie-
crust when the .temptation came. ... It has all gone
by, Horace, and God grant that we may never offend
again. I will not ask your forgiveness for myself, but,
Horace, I would humbly ask you to forgiveher. She may
he passionate, but she would not have sinned had it not
been for tne. And, Horace, remember that you have been
hard upon her, and perhaps that helped to turn her to a
sympathiser. Be gentle to her, to her, and forgive her,
and I'm sure she will be a good wife to you yet. Oh, I
am so sorry for the pain you are hearing. I would readily
give my right hand if I were as guiltless as before I saw
her. I know not how to attempt to comfort you, Horace.
I can only tell you of my intense sorrow for you. Horace,
stick to the truth. I have all mv shame to bear, the
brethren to meet, hut, if I gave up the clear, shining
Truth, I would go to the d o j s at once. Stick to the
truth, Horace, and God will comfort you. I will have to
bear my fate at Christ's hands, according to the sincerity
of my repentance. I’ll he home about the 23rd. I am,
your unworthy brother in the Truth, Alex. F. Collins.
A LIGHT SUBJECT IS ALWAYS ENTERTAINING.
This fact is also a matter of great consideration -viz.,
that the residents of Victoria and N.S. W. can save their
time, money, and tempers by sending or calling at our
respective warehouses fur up-to-date Hardware. Amongst
this season novelties will be found the Royal Bath Heater,
burns wood only, and produces scalding hot water in a
few moments. Price 65s. complete. Pamphlets posted.
Also, the great Galopin Lights, for kerosene, which gives
the user the same faciTti“S as gas. Bargains in all
departments, at McLEAN BROS, and RIGG, Ltd., Mel-
bourne and Sydney.*
Two years ago the Dutch Government
quashed an agreement drawn up by an Amster-
dam syndicate for the purchase of nearly one-half
of Dutch New Guinea. Dutch New Guinea is
about half as big again as British N.G., and the
Hague grabbers offered a substantial price, but
the Government snuffed them out.
Premier Reid had the incredible effrontery
last week to tell a Bathurst audience that there
was only one of his promised reforms which had
not been carried out —the reform of the Council.
What about the promised Local Government Bill,
and the Land for Closer Settlement scheme, and
the State Bank of Issue, and the promise to make
the people of N.S.W. Jolly and Fat, and the pro*
mise to square the finances, and all the rest of
Reid’s broken pledges ? The earth is strewn with
the brittle promises of the Reid Ministry.
The two Parisian journalists, MM. J.
Barrrere and R. Gorini, who recently arrived
in Sydney on a world-circling trip begun in
1895 without a penny—have supported them-
selves en route by a curious variety of occupations,
having acted, amongst other things, as scene-
painters, commission agents, “drummers,” and
cooks. They have done England, Canada, U.S.,
and Mexico, and the remaining itinerary, which
is to occupy another two years, includes the
Philippines, Ceylon, India, and Palestine. A
curious condition of their undertaking is the issue,
in every country visited, of an illustrated paper in
the language of the country in question.
It is a curious circumstance in connection
with the proposed New Guinea land-grant to a
British syndicate that the repeated applications
of Theodore Bevan, the Papuan explorer, for a
land concession in the country he had done so
much to exploit were met with the assurance that
no land could be parted with for speculative pur-
poses. In addition to being denied the “common
rights of first application, recognised all over
Australia,” Bevan appears to have been bluffed
off and bamboozled in all directions in the interests
of a lot of British land-sharks, who are eager to
profit by his labors and hardships. The whole
business needs vigorous stirring up, regardless of
the smellfulness thereof.
Since 1890 there have been three invita-
tions to federate —those of Parkes and Reid to
Australia in general, and that of Dibbs to Vic-
toria in particular, so all the requests have been
addressed by N.S.W. to the other provinces. Yet
Heydon, M.L.C., got this off at Tenter-
field : “ This province is in a more prosperous
condition than any other, consequently the southern
States are anxious for Federation in order to get
some of the spoils.” And A. G. Taylor has been
lamenting in the same key in the Star, and
Labor-member Ferguson wailed last week about
how the other provinces were pursuing N.S.W.
with a view to plunder. Would the anti-Billioug
people recognise the solid Truth if they happened
to meet it ?
Just before the Federal Referendum, every
N.S.W. elector will receive a special copy of
Sydney Daily Telegraph, containing many
“ facts ” about the Bill, and giving reasons why
N.S. Wales should vote against it. As aforesaid
electors have been receiving free copies of the un-
reliable paper named from various quarters for
some time past, and have now about “got the
strength of its facts,” this looks like a needless
extravagance on the part of the Telegraph pro-
prietary—or the people who are paying for the
free distribution aforesaid. Already “ Anti-
Bill,” with his 80,000 start, is getting desperate,
and sprinting with a Buck-like sprint, whilst
“ Bill ’’—good old “ Plugger Bill ! on scratch,
has about got the measure of his opposition.


TUESDAY, 21st JUNE.
BY AUCTION.
Tenham Cattle Station-
TO be SOLD pursuant to a judgment of the
Supreme Court of Queensland, made in an
action, Hammond v. Hammond and Others, num-
bered 55 of 1897, with the approbation of the
Honorable Mr. Justice Cooper, by Messrs. B. D.
MOREHEAD & CO., of Brisbane, Auctioneers,
in conjunction with Messrs. HILL, CLARK &
CO., of Sydney (the persons appointed by the said
Judge), at Mary-street, Brisbane, in the Colony of
Queensland, on the 21st Day of JUNE, J 898, at
Eleven O’Clock in the forenoon, in one lot,
TENHAM STATION
(Outside Tick-Infested Area),
Situated in Gregory South district of Queensland, con-
sisting of 276 square miles of country (more or less), of
which 190 square miles are Leasehold, having 17 years to
run from the Ist July, 1897, and 86 square miles of country
held under Grazing Right, under “Crown Lands Act of
1834.” Together with
6500 CATTLE (more or less), Fifty (50) HORSES (more
or less),, STATION PLANT, STORES and IM-
PROVEMENTS thereon or belonging thereto.
The Country is situated on Cooper’s Creek and Kyabra
Creek, about 40 miles south of Windorah and about 230
miles west of Charleville railway station. It is very rich
fattening country for cattle, consisting of lightly timbered,
open, mulga country, with occasional black-soil plains,
abundance of salt-bush, blue-bush, cotton-hush, and other
edible shrubs. TENHAM is splendidly watered by the
main channel of the Cooperand its many branches, be-
sides springs. The Cattle are quiet, and have been well
worked. In the herd there are about 1000 head of Bul-
locks and Cows fit to start for market. The Improvements
are sufficient for the efficient working of the station.
Particulars and Conditions of Sale may be had gratis at
the office of Messrs. Macdonald-Paterson and Hawthorn,
Edward-street, Brisbape; '-.Solicitors for the Plaintiff ;
Messrs. J. F. Fitzgerald and Power, Queen-street, Brisbane,
Solicitors for the Defendants ; or at the offices of the said
B. D. Morehead & Co., Brisbane ; Messrs. Hill, Clark &
Co., Sydney ; Messrs. Ryan, Hammond & Mates, Mel-
bourne ; and Messrs. Barker Bros., Adelaide, and at the
place of sale.
Dated this twenty-fifty day of April, 1898.
' (l.s.)' W. A. DOUGLAS,
Deputy Registrar.

G. Sutherland, the determined young Maori-
lander who won the 1, 5 and TO miles championships at
the last Mao riband Cycling Championship meeting, and
who in the past live months has won 28 firsts, six seconds
and ten thirds, rides Dunlop tyres.*
* # * ' *
The Dunlop Tyre Co. states that the work of
driving a bicycle with tyres inflated to a pressure of
161 bs. per square inch is exactly double that of a cycle
with the tyres blown to 351bs. ; and the wear and tear on
the. tyres is at about the same ratio. Kor everv-day r
road-riding in Australia the correct thing is' from 251bs.
to 3011)8. per square inch of pressure.*
• * • *
Some startling similes have been made by
cycling and other journals as to the speediness of Swift
'Value in general track fixtures : “ Like a lightning-flash
—Like greased lightning—Like an eloct' ic spark,” Ac.
A bush editorial, however, recently takes the simile cake
with the rather unscriptural statement that the Queens-
lander “ went like the very devil ! ” As to how fast the
mythological gentleman of brimstone fame can pedal an
up-to-date Dunlop-tyred wheel there is, of course, not
much information of a reliable character. Anyhow, if
such a match could be arranged, it is certain that the
betting as regards the man from Bananaland would still
be even monev.*
* * » •
A. C. and A. A. Middleton have changed their
mounts. Roth these sterling wheelmen are prominent
factors in the Dunlop-tyred Victorian cycle landscape.
• The two Middies now push the speedy Swift wheel.*
The modern (new) “Guinea” pig has a
very long tale.
Last week the Brisbane Boodle Govern-
ment auctioned 50,000 acres of Central Queens-
land soil at 10s. an acre, over 30,000 acres going to
form one big estate. The craving to eat up every
available acre of Central land and secure the
money for tiie Southern Treasury, before Separa-,~
tion comes is the' one consistent feature in the
Boodle Government’s policy. It may wobble like
grass in the wind on every other matter, but its
great stfeal is founded on a rock.
New Life on Circular. Quay.—lt is
pleasing to see the old produce stores around here
showing signs of business activity. -On Friday
last Messrs. Pitt, Son and Badgery, Ltd., cleared
a great catalogue of 3700 bides—by far the largest
of the day. This company have only been about
a year in the wool and produce trade, but their
good principles as agents only has come along
from their stock trade, and the satisfactory prices
obtained lias rendered it necessary to increase the
accommodation, and has infused new life into the
Quay.*
