SPORTING NOTIONS.
Considering' the state of the weather lately,
Sydney Turf Club had luck in haying a perfect day last
Saturday for the opening of its meeting at Randwick.
Four favourites won right off the reel : Honeydew 11. the
Hurdles; Lo'ngford the Amateur Welter; Sailor Boy the
Royal Stakes; and Ditto the Steeplechase. Ace of Dia-
monds went out favorite for the T.C. Handicap, but he
failed-to run into a place. Town Clock winning from
Charge. The wins of Longford and Sailor Boy, owned by
Humphrey Oxenham, were as usual most popular, he
being one of the very few owners whose horses the public
can depend upon getting a run with.
• • •
Another attempt will be made at the annual
M.L. racing conference to raise the minimum weight from
6.7 to 7st. A sensible proposition. Horses have improved
vastly in quality since the last minimum was struck, and,
as the low-markers have to continually put up over-
weight, owing to the paucity of. good lightweight riders,
the proposed rise would do no harm.
'■ • • - • •
Levanter, likely to be sent from M.L. to Aus-
tralia shortly, is the best ’chaser his native land owns.
He has never baulked or fallen ; is sound, and was a bar-
gain the other day at 255g5.
*** f * 1
Tote-shop running has been brought up with a
round turn in M.L. (writes a Bulletin- correspondent).
Tiiree prominent Christchurch layers, raided recently,
went to court with a pile of notes, expecting a stiff fine.
The P.M., however, sentthem up for two months, so that
the shop industry is likely to languish. But if layers
don't keep shops they have emissaries on the streets, and
somehow the force cannot see ’em, although it spends
most of its wages trving to pick winners.
. • • * •
For strict economy re. racecourse “adjuncts” a
Maori club can’t be beat. ’At a'recent Orakei “grand
horse carnival ” the paraphernalia consisted of steel-yards
(for weighing), a "brass hell and a blackboard. That was
ail. You had your lunch before you went. The black-
board, used as an equalisator, was worked by a Day and
Martin mathematician,-resplendent in a top hat, a shawl,
and pair of tan boots. The grand stand was a row of
willow trees, and when anyone accidentally fell off on to
the crowd below there was a bit of a row, nothing more.
* S' • *
Waiukn, one of the best all-round horses ever
raced in M.L., is a St. Leger out of Musket mare. He
won Wellington Thompson Mile Handicap lately under
10.3 for the third time, and next day humped 10.7 in
Autumn Handicap over 1J mile, being’ beaten by a neck
by Target (Dreadnought— Straightfire), who had an
advantage of 3£st. Waiuku’s record—6s races :22 firsts,
10 seconds, 5 thirds; 2S unplaced; stakes, £3933. The
ex-hack has a N.Z. Cup on his list. His great rival, St.
Paul, has started 42 times, won 18, and gathered in £4937
stakes.
* * • *
“Newhaven TL, now at 22 to 1, debuts next
week in the City and Suburban,” writes Tits Bulletin's
London correspondent, “and should win. [He ran
second.] He d'd a fine gallop over the distance (lm. 2f.)
before Dan Cooper, anil his trainer now has the 1 form of
this year’s best up his sleeve. The two most-to-be-feared
antagonists are Brayhead. 4yrs., 6st. 101 b.. 9 to 1, and
Rosenjerryn, 4.vrs , 6st. 121 b., 20's. ‘ Mr. Jersey ' owns
the former, and the latter is Rothschild’s, whose Fosco
so ignominiously whopped Maluma in the Crawford
Plate, three-quarters, this week. The Malua mare had
only 7st. 71b. and £5OO of Lily’s money on, starting
favorite. After the race trainer Webb,' in reply to
‘What was the matter? Didn’t she stay?' said, ‘ She did
nothing,’ and then used verbal lashes that, applied across
Maluma’s flanks, would have made her win by a tail at
least.”
• * • •
In Sydney football circles on Saturday the
hopes of the adherents of the newly-formed club' the
Sydney, that it would follow up its previous Satur-
day’s victory over Wallaroo, got a shock when Randwick
scored a goal under three minutes from the start of play,
half-time scoring being: Randwick 8 points, Sydney 3
points. On resuming play, Light made a kick on spec,
and fluked a goal, and that was Sydney’s share of the
game,’Randwick winning by 21 points to 7. Randwick
was simply too good all round, and whatever the new
club may develop into during the season, at present it
is not up to championship form. Little interest was
taken in the Wallaroo-Pirate game, Wallaroo winning by
16 to 9. - •• V.- . .
* • * *
The influence of the money-grubbing Yic. Foot-
ball League is obvjous"as the matches open. When the
League was started at the beginning of last season by a
combination of the leading clubs of the association, they
loudly denied any intention of taking good players from
the inferior clubs they had frozen out, and, in support of
the contention, depided that no player in the jettisoned
clubs during the season of '96 should be permitted in
League teams of '97. But '97 is over now, and there has
been a general exodus of the cracks of the smaller teams
to the Leagueclubs—the clubs who throughout the whole
history of Vic. football have the most smellful records for
professionalism and general cronkness of play.
. * : * . - * ' *
Capt. Marshal], who engineers Vic. rifle-team to
Bisley this time, will be far better known to young Aus-
tralia as sec. of the old Vic. Football Association. Mar-
shall was all for the' rigor of the game, and, although a
Carlton club delegate, would sit on a Carlton stousher as
relentlessly as upon a misdemeanant of any other team.
To Marshall also are due nearly all the improvements in
the game during the last 15 years, and it must gall the
old man to see what a wreck Vic. football is to-day.
Mediocre play, bad attendance, and the old association
supplanted by a greedy crowd, eager to prevent the
weaker clubs getting a share of the plunder. No wonder
Marshall is getting hint to a gunnery !
* • * »
Coolgardie, like Charters Towers, plays its foot-
ball premiership matches on Sunday, but the authorities,
unlike tljo.se of the .Northern town, forbid “ the- gate.”
As a consequence, the “ plate” has been in evidence this
season at every entrance to the ground, and prosecutions
are pending thereanent.
w * * •
Considered probable that a M.L. Rugby-team
will tour England next year. A whit° combination, of
course. England has forgotten all about Warbrick’s
Maoris by this time.
* • * •
The Sydney police were sent out on a false scent
one day last week to Kensington, whilst “ Chiddy ” Ryan
and “Ginger '*.Toohiey brought off their little affair with
the raw 'tins out Sandringham way. Ryan cutToohey up
pretty severely, until his hands and condition went hack
on him, and the loyal policeman arrived at the moment
when Tooliey had a good show of pulling up lost ground.
The crowd dispersed, and the fight was declared drawn.
• • • »
Thebrutality of prize-fighting as against football,
gauged by last week’s Sydney doings. Ryan and Tonhey
fought with bare knuckles.for upwards of an hour, and,
when the police appeared, both men ran away with the
crowd, Ryan's worst hurt being a puffed hand, whilst
Toohey*s eye was black and his nose was bleeding. In
the Randwiek-Sydney match ot> Saturday, Light hud his
face cut open, Johnstone injured his leg and had to he
carried off the field, and Duggan had a finger severely
hurt. There were minor mishaps, but, in the three men-
tioned, play was stopped for some time whilst, the men
were taken off the ground to have their wounds dressed.
• * • •
A year ago 'Fitzsimmons, when he announced
his retirement-after defeating Corbett, picked upon six
men as likely for the championship -Corbet', Choynski,
Goddard, Maher, Jackson, and Jem Hall; and to-day not
one of the lot stands a chance. The only man of the
crowd who, a few weeks ago, seemed to have “ a possible ”
was Maher, now beaten in a round by the greatest dere-
lict of the lot, Goddard. Australia, which a few years
back held the championships at all weights, does not at
present own a man with a chance for the championship in
any class —bar, perhaps, Fitzsimmons, who seems to have
developed into a newspaper fighter only. The three men
who have the best look-in are Jeffries, Sharkey, and
Kuplin, all big", immensely powerful men.
• • • •
One of thf» biggest surprises sprung upon the
pußiiistlo world for many a day happened last week,
a cable told of the defeat of Peter Maher by Joe
Goddard. But a few weeks ba<:k Jeffries, who heat Peter
Jackson, simply wiped the floor with Goddard, who was
described as having lost all his old-time hurricane dash.
Apparently, however, very little hurricane was required
for Maher, as the cable says the affair did not last a round.
Goddard beat Maher some six years ago in less than
three rounds, and the remembrance of that tornado act
may have had something’ to do with the present defeat of
Maher, who has always been credited with a weak spot.
* * « *
Melb. Bicycle Club, v which opened new club
rooms with an evening-dress banquet the other week, has,
at the present time, £‘3300 invested in Melb. Met. Board
of Works debentures. So there is no immediate prospect
of the M.B.C. making a second private composition with
its creditors. It donates a “ hundred of the very best”
to the local hospitals, and hopes to make another £101)0
profit on next Austral jig.
. Mdlle. Serpolette, the French lady cycliste, now
in Adelaide, is a fetchingyoung creature with a charming
accent and, allegedly, only 20 years old, who looks far too
fragile for cycle-racing. She states that she intends to
wear skirts on the track, and is anxious for the scalps of
all local nian racers. Another illusion gone. Australians
were looking for a big, lantern-jawed female with washer-
woman arms careering round th*- track in tights and look-
ing for the judges during the week armed with a gun.
There is danger, says an impressionable Bi’U.ktin' scribe,
that racing men will be unable to resist a glance from
those dark eyes when coming up the straight. The ar-
rival of Mdlle. adds a new terror to the position of cycling
judge. The ladj has a smile that would corrupt a saint,
and the ordinary Australian cycling judge is but mortal.

The price quoted was “ a cool thou’ ! ”

Plugger Bilmartin’s first race, after his return
to his native land, proved a soft thing for the genial Bill.
Terrill, his opponent, never bothered him from the jump,
and Bill simply wore him down and won as he liked.
This is Bill's only show in ’Frisco, for, directly after the
nntch; he left for the East-presumably to enlist for
Cubin service. Bill, on a high-gear, is calculated to
scatter dismay in the Spanish ranks.

The Britisher is proverbial for his fair play—
when there is no chance of his being licked. The latest
specimen is the refusal of young Ten Evck's entry for the
Henley Diamond Sculls. Ten Eyck, a mere lad, son of the
Yankee sculler who was rowing in the days of Trickett
and La.vcoek, won the Sculls last year, completely out-
pacing the best of the English amateurs, and for tlie first
time the Diamond Sculls left England. The Henley
regatta rules allow the refusal of an entry without ex-
planation as to the reason. Young Ten Eyck’s was so
refused this year, the Yankees saying that the only thing
against the lad is t.haE he is a durried sight too fast.
* * * *
Quite a crowd collected in Queen’s Walk, Melb.,
on the morning of Saturday last week. The occasion was
a common dog-fight; combatants, a small brawn poodle
and a hybrid animal of evil propensities belonging jointly
arid severally to the members of a ladies’ club in that
locality. The club dog is tough from long experience,
but the poodle is game, and the battle raged furiously
for some minutes. Then the poodle’s owner, a stylishly-
dressed lady, emerged from an adjacent shop. She didn’t
scream or faint; she just walked up to the scene of the
unpleasantness, and, putting out her gloved hand
grabbed her dog hv the tail and pulled. The dog also
pulled. Then a man in the crowd grabbed the other dog
likewise, hut the club-dog wasn’t that sort of tyke—he
promptly turned. round and hit the man, who thereupon
let go hastily, and used language. Then somebodv sw-
gested to the lady that she should hold a lighted sulphur
match under the Jog’s nose. The crowd jeered. Finally
the lift-hoy came along and seized the club-dog by the
fore-paw and squeezed it hard, and the performance was
over. It is a good tiling to know that a dog can’t bite you
if you squeeze its fore-paw very hard.

Cricket (and religion) as she is played in the
South Seas, illustrated by D.T.’s Samoan correspondent:
“ The Native Church of the London Missionary Society of
Apia is about completed. One novel scheme the Samoans
resorted to for raising funds was with cricket-matches.
Once a week the chiefs ordered all the people, men and
women, boys and girls, to play the game. Anyone who
disobeyed this order was fined a dollar. This did not
often happen, for all usually mustered on the ground for
play. The matches were played in different parts of the
town, and from 300 to 400 players would take part in one
game. Each player of the losing side had to subscribe a
shilling towards the building of the church. The scheme
became very popular, and, after a while, folk of all-
denmnin itions, people from other parts of the world,
white and black, desired to participate in the game. It
was finally arranged that any person not belonging to the
church could indulge in an innings upon payment of one
shilling an innings to the church. A number of young
men, who otherwise would not have subscribed a cent to
the church, became large supporters and subscribers, and
many shillings were gathered in each day of the play.
After being bowled or caught, or otherwise put out, it
frequently happened that the disappointed batsman or
batswoman would express a wish to have another trv.
This was allowed on payment of another shilling, and now
the Apia natives are in the proud position of bavinv one
of the finest and most handsome churches in the islands,
and entirely free of debt.”

The theatre-order-tax idea, gravely announced by cer-
tain mummers in a Sydney paper as a “ new path ”
discovered by Mr. Waiter baker, is a path which has
already been pretty well-trodden by Tub Bulletin. This
pappr doesn't remember exactly where it stole the idea
front, but it floes distinctly remember calling attention to
the subject and pointing out that, to the best of its belief,
such a system was then actually in practice at London
Lyceum. “ Mr. Walter Baker” may therefore promptly
take this particular feather out of his cap.
Veteran George Coppin, founder of the Vic. Old
Colonists' Association, is the sole survivor of the first
office-bearers. Yet he opened the Old Col. carnival last
week with quite a sprightly speech.
There are nine plays advertised for performance in
Mell). Age, at time of writing, and in not a single one is
the name of the author so much as mentioned 1 The im-
pudence of these mummers surpasses everything. When
Marshall Hall advertises Beethoven’s “ Pastoral Sym-
phony ’’ for performance—as is the case in the same day’s
paper—it is advertised as Beethoven’s ; but, in the case
of “Hamlet,” Shakespeare is nowhere—it is only mummer
Barrett, who is mentioned. Not that Shakespeare minds,
but still
Miss Katharine Hardy, the pretty songstress of the
B. and B. co., is now- on her return journey from India to
Melbourne. Her many friends in Melbourne and Svdney
were horrified at the announcement—by one of the daily
papers—of her death from malarial fever in India, and for
a time no confirmation or refutation of this report could
be obtained.
Be Clement Scott's apology to the lydies of the stage,
it is indeed remarkable that these conspicuously virtuous
females waited so long before deciding to become in-
dignant. Scott, has been writing the same sort of thing
for years, advising young girls by no means to go on the
stage, because the life is so wicked, atid so forth. But
everyone took it for granted that it was all true, and no
fuss was made until the appearance of the Blathwayt
interview. Anyone who chose to look over the Theatre
magazine while C.S. edited it, would find numerous
remarks of a similar character re the stage as no place for
“good ladies, and its almost inevitable contamination,
with such a proviso as “ Mind, I do not say there are no
good women on the stage,” which was as much as to say
“ I feel sure there are not many.”

Caleb Porter successfully put in a few nights in Vic.
provincial towns lecturing on “The Sign of the Cross,”
with limelight effects. “Nero” gives the doleful story
with all the unction of a converted pagan, and spouts
lurid bits of the dialogue for the edification of the unco-
gude. The lecture has all the sprightliness and vivacity
of a sermon, and cast" a visible gloom even in Geelong.
Dear Bulletin'. —Cursed are those who have said our
good things before we had time to chip in—or words to
that effect. I have been waiting for somebody to point
out _ that the emendation “ paddock ’’ (toad) for
“ pajocke"—which Mr. Wilson Barrett claims as the
fruit of much independent study of Hamlet on his part—
was made about a century and a-half ago by a party
named Theobald, now dead, who, though not an actor,
was reckoned to know something about Shakespeare. He
still has that reputation amongst students—though, of
course, past masters in everything, like Mr. Wilson
Barrett, are not to be taught anything. Which recalls
another thing. Samuel Foote was asked once to repeat
the wittiest thing he had ever said. “ Well,” he said (I’m
not quoting the exact words, though one should speak by
the card when letter-perfect mummers are on the war-
path), “1 once saw a chimney-sweep mounted on a mag-
nificent high-stepping horse ; ‘ There,’ I said, ‘ there goes
Warburton on Pope’!’’ Mr. Wilson Barrett on Shakes-
peare is a shade more awe-inspiring. Or mirth-provok-
ing.—A Country Galoot.

Pollard’s Lilliputian Opera Cos. has had a triumphant
time in S. Africa. At Durban, particularly, the success
was brilliant. “No co. that has e'ver visited this town,”
says a local paper, “ has had such uniform support for
three coinseeutive weeks.” Of Alf. Goulding, in particu-
lar, it is said,. “ the more this little.fellow's extraordinary
talent is witnessed 1 , the more remarkable and fascinating
it becomes.” The booking was so heavy that the pit had
to be temporarily abolished, and at the close of the season
the juvenile performers were presented with a warhilv-
complimentary address—formally commencing “Ladies
and gentlemen.” At Pietermaritzburg, also, the co. did
enormous business, and t he enthusiasm over the “astonish-
ing little Australians” almost reached the hysterical.
Johannesburg critics are not enthusiastic* over W. J.
Holloways production of “The Sign of the Cross.” W.J.
himself gets an ambiguous kind of Credit for “ succeeding
so well with a part for which he is so unsuited.” Dun-
robin’s Nero is more favorably spoken of, but the only
thing, like hearty commendation is reserved for—the
scenery.

Attendants at a metropolitan theatre some time ago
sorrowed over the departure of a loving couple, who *tvere
for long the life and soul of the place, and “whooped it
up with such abandon that the lives of all behind the
scenes were spiced with variety of the most riotous kind,
and their business-hours made glad. The differences
always arose over little things, and they gathered and
swelled till they filled the whole house behind with dust
and hair and incoherent exclamations. Hubby lifted up
his voice and stormed till the slates jigged on the roof,
and wifey struck a monotonous squeak and a mechanical
war-dance, and " went on something awful.’ - . The insur-
rection ended in exactly the same way every time : Hubby
slowed down, and drifted into expostulation, then into
cajolery ; after which tears, embraces, and huggv-muggy
before the grinning company. “ Blow me,” says the
scene-shifter, “we see the last of the bloomin’ show, after
all."

Upon the “trade” of a literary barrister, lias played one

who provokes the pub-keeper’s daughter into calling him
“ You beast-! ” Mr. Hodges deserves great credit for the
aforesaid performance. It is a line iliustiatiori of a com-
petent actor's ability to appear thoroughly natural in a

Dear Bulletin. —l think the Sarah Sands
had on board the 35th —Royal Sussex —concerning
which regiment a curious item. For betting at
the mess-table before the cloth is removed there is
imposable a fine of either a dozen of fizz or
the amount of the bet. On one occasion a man
had got so far as to say, “ I’ll lay you ” Then
he chanced to catch the president’s eye, and-he
finished his sentence with the name of a very
necessary but much disrespected household utensil.
He was fined that article, and duly provided one
in silver , which always afterwards decorated the
mess table until it went down in the Sarah Sands.
It was subsequently replaced by a smaller one
used as a tobacco-jar. I was honorary member of
the 35th mess for two years, but Col. Blomfield,
of Albury, was in the Royal Sussex and knows
more about it than— Scotty the Wiiinkler.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
27 of this issue.*

“ Steff Cassander ,! : Indian coolies in Fiji
must show a written character before you en-
gage them as servants. And they always ask you
for a reference, even if you have employed them
only a week. ' Some of these documents are very
odd, and the innocent holder smiles serenely while
you read : “ Bearer is an excellent servant, but
none too honest,” or, “ Bearer was my cook. Took
him on immediately after his release from gaol.
Tries his best to go straight, but is easily led
away,” or, “ If this fellow can’t do anything else,
he is splendid at chopping wood.”
THE MAN WHO CAN’T GET ON.
[For The Bulletin.]
w e come across him every day,
The man who can’t get on ;
And nothing seems with him to pay—
He never can get on.
We knew him well long years ago,
When things were very far from slow,
But even then Fate seemed to know
That he would not get on.
The times have changed, but he’s the same —
The man who can’t get on ;
And still he’s at the same old game,
But yet he can’t get on.
A goodly few have made their “ pile,”
And on their oars can rest awhile ;
But struggling in the same old style
Is he who can’t get on !
’Tis not because his style is old,
Or that his skill is gone ;
Or stress of competition bold
Prevents his getting on.
But some defect within must lie—
A shortage of the full supply,
And Fate alone can answer.why
He fails in getting on.
One consolation he may know—
The man who can’t get on—
That life is, after all, a show,
And, show-like, quickly gone.
And, mayhap, in some other sphere,
Since Fortune seems to shun him here,
Fate will not show a face severe,
And then he may get on !
M.L. Alex. B. M'Ceeady.
To the regret of certain Queenslanders
who claim to be hereditary rulers in that province
their boss commandant, Logan, lies interred
among a lot of rubbish on Garden Island, Sydney
harbor. More unlikely things have happened
than that this acrid, sour-souled slave-driver’s
ashes shall be, in some fever of enthusiasm, rooted
up, carried North and given honored sepulture in
some sunny Brisbane knoll —for future generations
to prostrate themselves before. N.S.W. should
yield the ashes without a murmur. Logan bossed
Moreton Bay between 60 and 70 years ago, when
all there were convicts or gaolers, and his lash
bears the repute of never having rested. He was
so successful in subjugating wild spirits that
Sydney used to send him its most incorrigible
villains. Logan broke them in or killed them.
But he himself was killed near Ipswich, either by
the blacks or convicts, and so highly was he
appreciated that Sydney had the body embalmed
and entombed on Garden Island. Queenslanders
can have him back, if they want him, any day.
N.S.W. has heavy stocks of his kind.

This company is being formed for the purpose of ac-
quiring from the owners a Gold Minihg Lease, No. 3827,
containing an area of 30 acres, in name of James Buckley,
Esq., of Bright, situate at Ovens Valley, near Harrietville,
and issued by the Mines Department in Victoria.
A considerable amount of developmentary work has
already i, on done on the property, from which trial
crush mgs, taken from different levels, up to date, have
given the following results :
No. 1 crushing of 11 tons yielded 74 oz.
Ho. 2 „ 5.) „ 36 oz.
No. 3 „ 17* „ 54 oz.
No. 4 „ 45 „ 131 oz.
Total 79 294
The above is equal to close upon 3} oz. per ton, and as
the gold has realised £4 3s. 9d. peroz., the prospects.of
the mine becoming highly payable are at once apparent,
as it affords evidence of the mine becoming equally as
great a gold producer as some of the mines in the'im-
mediate vicinity, mentioned by Mr. Buckley in his report,
to which particular attention is drawn.
The vendors show their confidence in the mine as to its
permanency by taking the principle cash consideration
out of the gold to come from the mine, the immediate
c.onsidctalion being paid, merely covering the outlay up
to date.
It is considered that the amount which is being placed
to the credit of the company will be ample for the erec-
' 'on of the necessary reduction plant and opening up of
the mine.
Splendid access is afforded to the mine, which, com-
bined with the exceptional facilities for economical mani-
pulation of the lode matter, should enable the company
to treat the ore at a cost of only about 4 or sdwt. per
t in, thus allowing a handsome margin for dividends even
t.pon *oz. stone.
The Vendors have applied for the necessary machinery
f- : tes and water rights which will be available to the com-
pany.
The promoters place this property before the public
with every confidence, feeling assured that its possi-
bilities, beyond what is already known, are practically
unlimited.
A large number of shares have been already applied for
on the field, and early applications are invited io avoid
disappointment.
Shares will be allotted in order of priority of date.
Prospectus and further particulars can be obtained at
the office of Messrs. FEWCOFSKI and HILL, 60-70
Queens-street, Melbourne, where rich gold specimens
from the mine are also on view.
REPORT BY MR. JAMES BUCKLEY.
Bright, Ilth May, 1898.
To Messrs. Fewcofski and Hill,
Melbourne,
Gentlemen,—ln compliance with your resquest, I beg
to submit the following, bearing upon the “ Smoko ’’
lteef and Ovens Valley, near Harrietville, known as
“ Duffy and Gallagher’s ” show.
Without entering into details as to the merits of the
property under notice—which is being dealt with by ex-
pert evidence and opinion—it will be sufficient for me to
mention its relative position to other well-known mines
in the district, in order that some idea may be formed as
to its future prospects.
It is not generally known that the Bright district has
produced close on seven million pounds sterling from its
gold resources, and that there are mines in this district
which have produced immense fortunes; hut unfortuna-
tely for the district, the principal mines have been owned
and worked by small cj-operative parties, and on en-
countering a fault., which is to be met with in all reefs,
the owners—having made their fortunes out of their
mines—deemed it advisable to retire on their good luck.
The property under notice is situated in the midst of a
group of mines which have yielded hundreds of thousands
of pounds to their owners.
For instance, the “Oriental,” situated immediately
north, on the same range, yielded £780,000 sterling, this
enormous amount being taken out in a few years, the
whole of it coming from above the 300 feet level.
The “ United Miners,” situated S.E., yielded over
£250,000 sterling.
The “ London,” situated to the west, yielded £80,006
worth of gold in four years.
The •• Myrtle," “ South Myrtle" and “ Red Ruth" pro-
duced £120,000.
The “ Richardson,” in the same group, yielded £45,000
worth of gold.
The “ Hit or Miss” and “ Shamrock ” mines, situated
south, and adjacent to the property under notice, were
worked with satisfactory results for 25 years.
It is generally supposed that the shoots in the district
are short, and that the reefs are not continuous, but this
is NOT the case. For instance, the “ London,” “ Myrtle,”
“South Myrtle" and “ Red Ruth" were all separately-
owned mines on the one line of reef. The “London"
shoot alone was over 1000 feet in length.
The “United Miners” was worked for over 600 feet
along the line.
The “ Shamrock ” was worked for 25 years and to a
depth 6T600 feet without a fault, engaging up to 200 men
at one time.
“ Bulgarian” was worked by separate parties fora yield
of 6000 oz., and subsequently by a second party for
4 ',OOO oz.
“ Shouldri't'Wooder," on the same range, was worked
by three parties, and was subsequently sold by the third
party to an English company, 20,0000 z. having been taken
out of the mine.
The “t-one Hand," owned by E. Duncan, yielded,
w ithin the last eight years, £42,000 worth of gold.
The “ Monarch," a recent find, and owned by an Eng-
lish company, has reputed to them a crushing of 25 tons
for £3OOO worth of gold.
From a great number of other reefs in the district that
can be quoted, although not in the immediate vicinity to
the one under notice, ft criterion can. be formed as to
a 'mt the district can do in the way of gold-production.
The most notable, the “ Pioneer ” reef, which was crush-
ing for 12 years with 24 head of stampers, putting: up the
marvellous record of having produced 600 oz. of gold within
72 hours.
The “ Bed Jacket" produced over a quarter of a million
sterling in a few years.
The “ Home Line ” of reef has been worked for over half
a mile along the line by different parties, with satisfactory
results, which, taken with others, proves conclusively a
continuity of the lines and the yields, which will surpass
those of more favorite localities. For cheap manipulation
of stone, the district is second to none, there being
abundance of water in most places for milling purposes,
thus saving the heavy expense of engineers and fuel, and
costly driving machinery, and most of the reefs can be
worked by tunnels; saving the cost of winding and pump-
ing plants, and the district is abundantly supplied with
timber for mining purposes.
In conclusion, I might mention the foregoing records
were compiled from the Ovexs axd Murray Advertiser,
district papers, and Mining Registrars’ reports.
Yours faithfullv,
(Signed) JAMES BUCKLEY.
REPORT BY MR. T. R. TOBY.
Harrietville, 2nd April, IS9B.
To Messrs. Fewcofski and Hill,
Melbourne.
Gentlemen,—ln accordance with your instructions, I
have \ isited the mining property near Harrietville, Vic-
toria, known as “ Duffy and Gallagher’s ” show, and beg
to hand you the following report tnereon.
The property consists of one 30-acre lease situated on a
main spur eight hundred feet (800) abo.ve Harrietville
and Bright-road and. Ovens Riier.
There are five lines of reefs outcropping the lease.
No. 1. The lode outcrops for a distance of about twelve
chains, and where opened upon shows a width of three
feet three inches, the stone being good auriferous quartz.
Eleven tons of stone taken from the southern end of the
outcrop gave a yield of 740 z. of smelted gold.
A tunnel has been driven 112 feet, intersecting reef at
one hundred and twenty-two feet helow its highest out-
crop, and being 678 feet above the Ovens River, 'he aver-
age course of the lode being north 4odeg. west with a
S.W. underlay of one in one, carrying well-iieiined walls.
The reef has been here driven on 86 feet N. At 22 feet
from tunnel a rise was put up, and gold met wi;hat 72
feet. A crushing of 5$ tons obtained here yielded 360 z.
gold.
At 42 feet in rise an intermediate drive was put in
north, and a parcel of 17J tons was crushed for 540 z. At
62 feet in lower level, north of tunnel, a rise was put up
to connect with intermediate level, gold being met with
at 23 feet.
From these workings a parcel of 45 tons is now being
crushed at the Harrietville Company’s mill, and stone is
now being broken from the 122 feet level, 25 tons being
at grass.
As proved by developmental work, the chute of gold is
dipping north at an aiule of about one in one.
At 86 feet in lower level, the reef has opened out to
6 feet, and from present indications will reach a still
greater width, gold being obtained from wall to wall.
The four parallel reefs west of No. 1, from surface indi-
cations, will junction at about 80 feet from present work-
ings with No. 1 reef, thus forming one large xold-bearing
formation.
No work of moment has been done on these reefs,
except “ loaming, ’’ gold being obtained in several places
along the line.
POWER.—For milling purposes sufficient water can be
obtained from Ovens River by construction of about 25
chains of water race to drive 50 head of stampers in
summer, and an unlimited number if required in winter,
thus saving all the expense of firewood, skilled engine-
drivers and other expenses.
To connect with mine an incline tram of about 25
chains, and a sideling tram of about 10 chains, could be
constructed it small cost, there being abundance of.
splendid timber for either mining or constructive pur-
poses on lease.
PROSPECTS.—-This property is admirably situated for
carrying on mining operations by tunnelling, and can be
worked to a depth of 800 feet, being limited only by
length of tunnels and expense of driving them, and if
economically handled is well situated for cheap manipu-
lation of quartz, there being an ample supply of timber
on the ground to* all purposes. A sufficient quantity of
water for working a large number of stamps, and no
hauling quartz or pumping water,-the lodes being well
defined, and returning by crushing good payable results,
and when opened out the quartz should be obtained and
handled at a very small cost.
Ail these circumstances greatly enhance the value of
the property, which I believe will pay handsome dividends
for years to come.
I beg to remain, gentlemen,
Yours obediently,
(Signed) T. R. TOBY.
REPORT by J. B. KING, Esq.
Melbourne, 12th May, 1898.
To Messrs. Fewcofski and Hill,
Melbourne.
Gentlemen,—
As requested, I beg to submit my report on the pro-
perty situated at Harrietville, Victoria, and known as
“ Duffy and Gallagher’s” show, and containing 30 acres,
leased from the Victorian Government for gold-mining
purposes.
I visited the above-mentioned property in company
with Mr. Buckley, one of the proprietors, on the Bth May,
1898, and inspected the various workings, with the fol-
lowing impressions, viz.:—
There are three lodes outcropping on the property,
which is in mountainous country.
No. 1 Lode outcrops for a distance of about 12 chains,
width of same 3 feet, one foot of which is proved to be
worth 3 to 7 oz. gold per ton from actual crushing results,
the balance, which is stacked for future treatment, I
estimateTo yield about joz. of gold per ton.
The course of lode north 45deg. west. Underlay west
one in two, carrying well-defined walls.
A tunnel has been driven into the hill a distance of 112
feet, cutting the lode at a depth of 120 feet from the sur-
face, and from this point the reef was driven on about 86
feet north.
At about 20 feet along the drive north from where the
reef was cut in the tunnel, a rise was put up on lode, and
a crushing taken, which yielded 7oz. of gold per ton. At
a distance of 42 feet, driving north at an intermediate
level, a crushing was taken out which yielded 3oz. of gold
per ton.
At about 60 feet in a rise put up to connect the upper
workings, same class of stone being raised, which I esti-
mate will go fully up to the average of stone which has
already been crushed.
At a distance north about 90 feet from where lode was
first cut in tunnel, the lode matter is about 6 feet wide,
of which one foot will average fully 3oz. of gold per ton ;
the balance, I should say, would go about 6dwt. of gold
per ton.
No. 2 Lode outcrops on the surface for a distance of
about 10 chains, width of same about 10 feet. A few sur-
face cuts have been put in places, and lode proved gold-
bearing.
A tunnel is being cut into this lode at about a depth of
40 feet from the surface. On the occasion of my visit
the lode was met with, carrying a little gold. Further
development may prove this a payable lode.
No. 3 Lode.—l consider this merely a surface blow, but
would advise a little work being done on it at a future
date. -
GENERAL REMARKS.—In conclusion, I must say that
it has given me great pleasure to inspect .the property,
which I consider a very valuable one.
There is an abundance of water power for all mining
purposes, as also exceptional facilities for opening up the
mine, and with economical management should prove a
good dividend paying concern.
Yours faithfullv,
(Signed) J. B. KING,
Tasmania.
EXTRACT FROM THE “ OVENS AND MURRAY
ADVERTISER,” 22nd March, 1898.
The reef at Smoko, owned by Messrs. Buckley, Duffy,
Gallagher and Co., is showing excellent prospects just
now, and a large quantity of Stone is being got ready for
the battery. The number of hands has been increased,
and the mine is being opened up systematically at several
levels. The reef was first found in the “ early days ”by a
prospector named Bird, but he got nothing of ariy conse-
quence, and it was only recently that Mr. Gallagher
obtained some good prospects which led to the reef being
opened up. In tracing it along the surface it was found
that there are two lodes of stone forming the reef, one of
which onjy carries a little gold, and this is evidently the
one Bir4 prospected without obtaining anything of a
satisfactory nature. Up to the present all the stone put
through the stampers by Messrs. Buckley and Co. has
averaged 6 oz, to the ton, and what they are saving now
appears to be fully equal to all that has been treated in
the past. 1 had an Opportunity to visit the mins recently,
and found the proprietors are making preparations to con-
duct operations on a more comprehensive scale than have
hitherto been adopted. When the course of the reef had
been definitely ascertained, a tunnel was decided upon,
which—when in one hundred feet—intercepted the line
of reef with about 120 feet of backs. The line was driven
on some distance (about 20 feet), when the chute of stone
was met with, and a rise was pub up to the surface,-with
the result already stated. Stoping is now being carried
on at three different levels, the bottom 80 feet and 60
feet, and the high grade stone is being broken in each
instance. The lode averages a little over 3 feet in width,
and the line of reef has been traced along the surface for
several chains.
This valuable property furnishes a striking instance of
the absurdity of supposing that the quartz reefs of the
district have been all exhausted because some prospec-
tors have travelled the hills in vain for some considerable
time.
Here was a reef lying practically on the surface, not
more than a stone’s throw from the main Harrietville-
road for over a quarter of a century, available .'or any
one who would put in a few surface trenches ; and there
can be no reasonable doubt but that there are hundreds
of others equally rich in the district, in localities which
have been only partially prospected.
Reports have been in circulation to the effect that the
stone now being raised from Messrs. Buckley and Com-
pany’s reef is equal to a return of some score of ounces
to the ton, and though this may he slightly exaggerated,
there can be no doubt about a large percentage of the
stone being extraordinarily rich, and I am able to say so
after having seen several prospects crushed and panned
off, which were taken at haphazard from the reef. Up to
the present all the crushing has been done at the Har-
rietville Co.’s battery, and the stone now being taken out
will be treated there also, but with the large body of
battery stu'” available, the necessity of erecting their
own crushing appliances will be forced on the attention
of the shareholders. The mine is so situated that a plant
would be erected in such a position that the quartz could
be sent down by a self-acting tramway, and there is
abundance of timber for all mining operations. The
company hold a lease of 30 acres, known to contain a
couple of other lines of reef beside the one on which
active operations is now being conducted.
These lines will be tested at various points to ascertain
if they contain any auriferous chutes, but just at pre-
sent they will not be interfered with, as all attention for
some time must be directed to the working of the rich
reef the tunnel has been driven into. The conduct of
affairs on the mine is entrusted to Mr. J. Duffy, one of
the shareholders, and he appears to be utilising every-
thing to the best advantage.

“A man whose grammar is defective and
whose methods are curious, but who has the
ability to make money.”—London Wheeling on
Millionaire Hooley, who, by the way, has acquired
the remainder of the term of the Lee-Metford
rifle patents, and is arranging to float them —prob-
ably at a capital of .£5,000,000 or thereabouts.
Rev. Prof. Gosman (Melb.), who is a
Unitarian, stated in a recent lecture that Chris-
tianity was a glorious thing as compared with the
dreary monotony of rationalism which “brought
no comfort to the human heart.” Even the most
liberal of parsons still cling to the pathetic idea
that man is the centre of the cosmos. Prof. Gos-
man’s remark recalls the observation made by
Hagel, the yellow-eyed philosopher, to Heine:
“Do you think then that because you have not
poisoned your father and mother you should be
rewarded with immortality ? ” Or words to that
effect.
“ A.G.P.” to The Bulletin
Re typhoid spread along the Hunter, your correspon-
dent is a hit out. Fact is, every creek and every billa-
bong in N.S.'W. and Queensland is a culture-bed for
typhoid, cholera, or any other bacillary disease. Along
many of the small tributaries of the Hunter the latrines
are built over the creek or gully, presumably so that each
freshet may do the sanitary cleansing. The “other
fellow” gets the benefit, so “our troubles!” I’ve seen
it at scores of places—from Maitland to Allynbrook and
Murrurundi. All the billabongs near the track in way-
back N.S.W. and Q. are used for the same purposes as
above, and hence it seems to me that only for the sun’s
heat and long droughts, typhoid and such would have
swept off every mother’s son from the Lachlan to the
Thompson. The carcases of cattle floating past Bourke
have nothing to do with the epidemic. Travelling once
from Mt. Hutton to Booroonda we got off the track and
the water-bag gave out. We struck a waterhole at night
by following up a scrub-bull at a safe distance. We had
a “ long one,” then, I can tell you. Next morning we
were horrified to find that the “aroma” of the water was
due to 20 odd carcases of cattle in advanced stages. Meat
and drink.

E.F. : A writer in the Windsor, discourses
upon Australia under the title of “The Imperial
Heritage,” and this is a chunk of his knowledge
concerning one of the heritages, Queensland :
Not even the African jungle has taken its toll of brave
human life more inexorably than the land wherein lies
the dust of Leichhardt, and of Burke and Wills. Truly
to every son of the British Empire, to every man who
values courageous deeds, the spots whereon these men
died must ever be holy ground.
If Mr. Williams, the author of the above gushing
par., will point out the exact spot of “holy
ground” where Leichhardt died, he will solve a
long-existent mystery. Also the combined dust of
Burke and Wills does not rest in Queensland.
The only one of the party of four which blundered
across the continent whose “ dust ” rests in an un-
known grave in Queensland is poor little Grey,
whose death practically lies at the door of the
brainless leader of an expensive mistake. To any-
one who knows the rights of the matter, this con-
stant eulogising of Burke and Leichhardt seems
very silly. Leichhardt, through sheer good luck,
got through to Port Essington ; his way ran
through the best-watered travelling country in
Queensland. After that, he made a mess of every-
thing. He was not at all a heroic character. He
did not hesitate to backbite his companions and
deny them any credit, nor do other acts generally
called mean. Burke —who is always spoken of as
the gallant Burke—also blundered across over good
country by sheer luck. Placed in a situation
wherein an ordinarily good bushman would have
pulled himself and men through, he wilted and
died, never displaying any of that wonderful
heroism of which so much has been said and
written. If Mr. Williams wants somebody’s dust
to weep over, why, perdition is full of better men.

flr. Williams’ PINK PILLS FOR PALE PEOPLE

RHEUMATISM.
A SUFFERER 80 YEARS OF AGE.
A MARVELLOUS AND PERMANENT
CURE.
But for Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People,
Mr. David Robertson, of Byron-street, Kew, Vic-
toria, could not, at the ripe age of 80 years, have
successfully resisted the effects of an 18 months’
war, waged upon him by that dread enemy of
peace and health—Rheumatism. He was attacked
most virulently by rheumatism—first in the right
knee, next in the left hip, and then in the shoul-
ders wrists, hands, knees, and legs. He was
rendered entirely helpless. Every friend he had re-
commended different remedies in turn—sulphur,
kerosene (outwardly and inwardly), citric acid,
marshmallow, fomentations, hot sea baths, several
bottles of a largely-advertised cure for rheumatism,
and other articles were all tried without the
slightest benefit accruing. Ultimately, Mr.
Robertson came to the conclusion that his
ailment was incurable. A little while later,
however, a friend induced him to try Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills. He followed the directions
carefully and patiently, and in about three weeks
or a month after first taking them he noticed a
considerable improvement in his condition ; he
had then taken two boxes. He persevered with
the remedy, and, little by little, his ailment gave
him less and less trouble. He continued in this
manner until he had taken about 10 or 11 boxes,
the time occupied being from six to seven months.
At this stage every trace of rheumatism had left
him, and he was absolutely and perfectly cured in
every particular. He is now quite free from pain.
Mr. Robertson, in-conclusion, said he had recom-
mended Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills to many of his
friends, and they had received most beneficial
results from their use. He was well known in
the locality, and would certainly continue to re-
commend them.
This case is one which amply demonstrates the
marvellous curative properties of this universal
medicine under the most adverse circumstances.
If an old gentleman of 80 years of age can be
cured of rheumatism, what may not be effected in
tho.se cases in which the sufferer is a much younger
man or woman ? The answer is too obvious to
need insertion.

SCIATICA.
HE INTENDED GOING TO THE
HOSPITAL.
BUT DR. WILLIAMS’ PINK PILLS
EFFECTUALLY CURED HIM.
Mr. George Hynds, a- well-known resident of
Blayney, was recently interviewed by a Blayney
Advocate reporter about his serious illness and
remarkable cure. He obtained the following in-
teresting narrative, which Mr. Hynds said he
would be glad to have published :
“ About two years ago I fell a victim to sciatica.
I was drawing wood at the time. The complaint
forced me every now and then to take a spell.
The ailment kept getting worse, until at last I
could not even work, and had to go to bed, being
unable to walk. It affected my right leg from the
hip to the ankle, the pain being worse in my
ankle; I also suffered from terrible pains in my
back. I was in bed for four months, during which
time I suffered excruciating pain. I had a doctor
attending me all the time, but he gave me no re-
lief, although he used every means in his power
to do so. My ankle felt just as if it were broken,
and it was always as cold as ice. I used to
be led by my wife to the fire, and there I would
sit as long as I could, warming it. The trousers
I was wearing were all scorched by sitting
over the fire. The doctor put about a dozen
mustard and fly blisters on my leg, which left it in
a terrible state, and did not do me the least bit of
good. At last he told me that he could do no
more for me, and that the sciatica would have to
take its course. I then determined to go to the
Bathurst Hospital, but my medical adviser said
he didn’t think I would get any benefit there.
However, I said I would try it. After being
completely bedridden for four months, and on the
day before I was going to the hospital, I managed
to get into town, and I met a friend and told him
my intentions. This gentleman strongly advised
me to try Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People
before I went to the hospital. As my friend spoke
with such confidence about the pilis, I took his
advice, and went there and then to Mr. C. Fer-
guson’s store, and purchased the last three boxes
he had. After the first box I could get about
again, and I went to work again drawing in
wheat for Mr. Robert Marsden. When I had
finished the second box I felt no pain whatever,
and considered I was cured. I then took the
third box at leisure, although there was no occa-
sion for it. I commenced taking the pills last
October 12 months. Mr. Marsden and Mr. Fer-
guson, and a host of other people who knew my
previous condition, could hardly believe the rapid
change which came over me. Some people said
that the sciatica would again return to me next
winter, but I am glad to say I went through the
winter without feeling the slightest effects of this
terrible complaint. I have enjoyed excellent
health ever since I took the pills, and my cure has
been the means of a large number of people taking
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills about here.
“I am certain that the Pills, and no other
remedy, cured me; in fact, the other remedies
did not give me the slightest benefit.”
Mr. Hynds’ wife was present during the inter-
view, and endorsed all her husband’s statements,
both appearing very happy when testifying the
marvellous curative properties of Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People.

A LADY’S GRATEFUL TESTIMONY.

acute for words ; she got no sleep either by dav or
by night, and her agony was at times so great that

cheeks would swell up as big as saucers,” says Mrs.

was no getting rid of the pains in the head. I
also suffered from a sluggish liver and a weak
heart, and at times my eyesight was so bad that
objects before me seemed but dim shadows, and
there were none of my friends who looked for my
restoration to health.

disappeared as if by magic, and the continued use

INDIGESTION.
DR. WILLIAM’S PINK PILLS SUCCEED
WHERE ALL OTHERS FAIL.
SHE SUFFERED FOR 15 YEARS.
“For 15 years I suffered from indigestion,”
said Mrs. Boyes, of 162 Dowling-street, Sydney,
to a reporter from the Leichhardt Standard,
“ and,” continued she, “a long, weary time to be
ill, and I might have been suffering still if I had
not the good fortune to drop on a paragraph in
the papers describing a cure by Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for
Pale People in a
case very similar
to mine. Though
I always had in-
digestion with
me it came on
much more vio-
lently at times
than was usual.
The attack s
were at first
comparatively
light, but as
years went on
they became
more and more
severe. They
commenced
with a feeling of sickness and a disinclination to
eat, and when I did manage to eat some food it
would lie on my chest in a heavy immovable lump.
The sensation was most oppressive and dis-
tressing, and sometimes I was in such pain that
I could hardly sleep, and often I would wake
up in the night with a start and not get any more
sleep till the morning, and when I did get out of
bed, I would feel depressed in spirits, and quite
overcome by a feeling of lassitude and weariness.
I had voilent headaches, and a most awkward
giddiness when I got up suddenly from a sitting
posture ; I also suffered from a continual bad
breath and an unpleasant taste in the mouth. I
also had bad bilious turns. I was not as you see
me now, but was much thinner, with a pale, un-
healthy pomplexion. During all these long years,
in addition to the attention of doctors, I tried a
large number of other remedies, but none of them
did me an atom of good. At length I tried Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills. The effect was astonishing;
after taking them for only a short time the attack
I was then suffering from was completely got rid
of. I had a sound, healthy appetite, which I
hadn’t had for years, and my sleep was no longer
disturbed. I took altogether about 12 boxes, and
the pills completely cured me of both my indiges-
tion and biliousness.”

Baker’s Lead.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Nobody knew much about Jim Baker, except that
he had a claim somewhere out in the ranges. No
one had ever seen it or knew where it was, and, as
he only sold about 2dwts. of gold every Saturday,
when he came in for the week’s supplies, it was
concluded that it was worth nothing more than,
as Jim; himself put it, “ Tucker—and not very
flash at that! ”
When he had a shilling left after laying in the
week’s grub, Jim would drop into the local gin-
mill for a long beer and a yarn, which generally
meant boasting about the rich lead he was after.
But as Jim had the reputation of being able to lie
like a gas-meter, and of not having enough brains
to get a headache, very little notice was taken of
Baker’s lead.
Occasionally he used to bring in a bundle of
opossum and wallaby skins and sell them at the
local store, consequently he was looked upon as a
hard-working fellow' to fossick all day and go
shooting all night, but he reckoned he would
never be able to keep after that lead if he didn’t
go shooting.
It was a sheep country, and, about eighteen
months after Jim’s arrival, a regular dingo plague
broke out. There wasn’t a night that one or
more of the squatters didn’t lose sheep.
And the manner of the killing left no doubt
about it being the work of -dingoes. Hunting
parties and drives were got up by the sheep-
owners, but still nearly every morning word came
of more slaughter the previous night. Then par-
ties took to sitting up all night to wait for the
brutes, but it was no go ; and as sure as morning
came so did news that the dingoes had been at it
in some part of the district where no watch had
been kept.
At last the sheep-owners held a council of war,
and decided to give .£5 for every full-grown dingo-
skin stripped in the district, which offer was duly
advertised. For the first week, however, nothing
came of it, and the sheep-killing went on just the
same. The squatters were getting very down in
the mouth over the matter, when one day Jim
Baker produced two full-grown skins and claimed
the £lO, which was willingly paid, though Jim
reckoned it was hard-earned, as he had to sit up
all night in the ranges to get them. Every week
after that for a month Jim used to bring in his
two or three skins, and it was noticed that the
sheep-killing had ceased. But when Jim brought
in his tenth skin he had to answer a few ques-
tions about how he got them. He reckoned he
had come on the haunt of a pack out in the
ranges, but that he had now pretty well got
through the lot. The squatters reckoned so, too,
so the skin-money was declared off, and Jim
again settled down to digging and shooting.
But about eight months later the dingo-plague
broke out again, and sheep were killed and
mangled right and left. Hunting-parties sallied
out as before, with no success ; but as soon as the
squatters clapped on the .£5 reward again Jim
once more came to the rescue, and in three weeks
he brought in twelve skins. People looked upon
Jim with admiration, though some waxed sus-
picious and wanted to know if he was running a
dingo-factory.
When he brought in the last two of the twelve,
the receivers wanted to know where his camp was,
as they intended paving him a visit in a couple of
days. Jim gave the necessary directions, and de-
parted richer by yet another tenner.
That night, at the railway-station about 15 miles
from the township, a tall, clean-shaved man, lead-
ing by a chain a three-quarter-bred dingo, took
train for Sydney. It was Jim Baker, minus his
long, scraggy beard, and that dog knew all about
the nights Jim and he had had with the sheep of
the district. He it was who had helped Jim work
the “lead,” and Jim called him “ Partner.”
Two days later three of the receiving committee
rode up to Jim’s hut, and as no one answered to
their calls, they dismounted and went inside.
The place was empty, but what struck them as
peculiar was an ordinary shaft sunk in the corner
of the hut with a ladder down it. They descended,
struck a light and fancied they had dropped on an
underground menagerie, for a large room, tun-
nelled and about 20ft. trom the surface, contained
three large cages, built of stringy-bark poles,
fitted into floor and roof, and in each cage was a
dead dingo slut with, in two cases, dead dingo-
pups strewn around.
None of the men spoke. The game was plain
enough. Jim had struck his rich “ lead,” and it
had been panning-out very well till their inquisi-
tiveness burst up the show and compelled Jim to
kill his stud-sluts and growing stock, and do a
timely “ get.” They supposed, and rightly, that
Jim had came upon a litter of pups in some cave
in the mountains, and had reared them and bred
from them. The only thing that puzzled them
was as to how the sheep-killing was done, but they
knew nothing of “ Partner,” or the nights the
pair used to have “ booming the market,” as Jim
called it.
HANS SHOOK.
Transvaal civil service corruption must be
heroic. A Commission of Inquiry has reported
that during the last 15 years .£3,000,000 has been
expended for which not a single voucher is forth-
coming.
Melb. Age approvingly quotes the fol-
lowing observation made by Lieut. Churchill
in his “ Story of the Malakand Field
Force
“ The spectacle of a doctor in action among soldiers in
equal danger and with equal courage, saving life when all
others are taking it, allaying pain when all others are
causing it, is one which must always seem glorious,
whether to God or man.”
The cheerfully-blasphemous way in which the
writer sets down his Creator as an omnipotent
Dullard, who can’t see the huge Gilbertian absur-
dity of such a spectacle, is only paralleled by the
placid impiety of the paper which quotes him.
CHINA, 1898.
She lies, a grave disdain all her defence,
Too imperturbable for scorn. She hears
Only the murmur of the thronging years
That thunder slowly on her shores immense,
And ebb away in moaning impotence !
Giants enduring, she and Time are peers—
Her dream-hazed eyes knowing nor hopes nor tears,
Her glance a langour-lidded insolence !
And though the rabble of the restless West
In her deserted courts set their rash sway,
She heeds them not; as when the Sun, withdrawn
From his untarnished sky, knows it distressed
By storm of weakling stars, that he at dawn
Will wither with one ruthless glance away 1
Maoriland. Arthur H. Adams.
An Old Hand.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The New-comer was evidently nervous, as he
eyed the recumbent figures stretched out on the
low, broad divans. In fact, so nervous was he
that a Chinese light-o’-love, hair-bedraggled and
blear-eyed, poured out a cupful of hot green-tea,
and handed it to him with a reassuring leer. The
Old Hand observing her, advanced hastily, left
the yellow-faced dispenser, with whom he was
arranging some business transaction, and, grasping
her by the arm, spilled the tea.
“ Leave him alone, can’t yer; he’s a gent and
my chum, so none of yer games ’ere, damn yer—
git! ”
So speaking, he secured a small divan, and
taught his companion how to recline on one hip
with a pillow—dirty—supporting the neck; which
done, he resumed his interrupted business.
The New-comer now surveyed at ease the room
and its occupants, and was much oppressed by a
strange silence —a silence only increased by softly
whispered dialogues in Eastern jargon com-
mingling strangely with suppressed Western oaths,
the mixture forming but a vague suggestion
of speech itself. The small oil-lamps placed
between the sleepers showed grotesque and weird-
like forms. A mountain of shadow formed by a
sleeper’s hips was one, and below and beyond it —
a face, of which the flickering oil-flame merely
revealed the nose and the lips. Beyond this lay
more shadow-mountains and more suggested faces,
all veiled in a moving haze of thin blue smoke. A
pipe began to bubble somewhere, but he could not
distinguish the smoker ; the queer blue smoke had
made him lazy, and besides, the Old Hand was
returning.
“ We’re all right now, chappy,” he whispered,
and, placing a pipe and a very small tin tray,
containing a dark, viscous substance, beside the
oil-lamp, he stretched himself on the divan, face-
to-face with the New-comer.
“Now,” he continued, “this being yer first
pipe, I’ll cook a pea and smoke it. It’ll only take
a minute, so watch me well—see! ”
The New-comer assented silently.
The Old Hand now dipped a long pin into the
opium and twisted some around it, and, after
several cookings in the flame, with alternate roll-
ings on the outer edge of the pipe rosette, he
placed the pellet so formed in the pipe and held it
over the oil-flame. The pipe gurgled as he in-
haled, and in a few seconds the pea was smoked.
“Hell! —that did me good,” he whispered to
the now wide-eyed New-comer. “I’ll do all the
cooking, and you’ll only have to suck, and suck
strong. Swaller it down from the ’oiler of the
’and what grips the pipe. Don’t lip the mouth-
piece—the Chows and others won’t ’ave it —but
put yer 'and over it tight, then yer lips into yer
’and, and suck like ’ell—see ! ”
“Yes,” whispered the New-comer. “But I
don’t want to go to sleep.’’
The Old Hand grew contemptuous and slightly
blasphemous.
“ Go ter sleep ! Now, did yer put yerself in my
’andsornot? Yer wanted to see some Weedin’
life, and got me ter show it yer. Am I a-showin’
yer or not, hey ? ”
“ Yes. But is the place safe ? ”
“Safe! Why, I’m with yer! ’Ere’s yer pipe
ready; don’t worry, but put yer ’and ’ere—so ;
now yer lips, and—suck I —suck! —suok I You—
Ah!”
“ A-a-a-a-a-ah ! ” drawled the New-comer, as
the Old Hand took the pipe to prepare it for a
second pea. How comfortable he felt, and how
his heart went out to his new, but dear, dear
friend—the Old Hand !
The light-o’-love sat unrebuked at his feet, and
he pondered awhile on her dream-tinged beauty,
vaguely wondering why he had not noticed it
before. Being a cigarette-inhaler, his first pipe
was wonderfully well smoked for a beginner. He
now lazily noted the fact that a large flea was on
his pillow, and, more lazily still, he touched it to
make it jump—it would be so supremely funny to
see it jump. He touched it —it rolled over and
kicked feebly ; it also was very tired and—drunk.
The second pipe was now ready, and it proved
a great success—so did many others.
** * *
Surely that was sunrise—where was he? Yes!
that must be the back of Sydney Hospital,
and this—the Domain. He feebly sat up, and saw
next him an old, grey-bearded man sitting on a
paper bed, and around him dozens of listless
dossers.
Grey-beard smiled sleepily, but good humoredly.
“ Morning, boss ! Been out on the bust last night,
I see. Two chaps brought you here and laid you
down —said the police might get you. How do
you feel?”
“ Damned queer, my head is hell! ”he sud-
denly yelled as he caught sight of his boots.
Large rents were in each and heels there were
none.
“ Oh, my God! he yelled again, for his
trousers would obtrude themselves —they were
moleskins, very old and very dirty. Half-way
down his back, and revealing a wide expense of
unclean shirt, hung an old “ duck ” coat.
“ How the —who the—what! ”he gasped.
Grey-beard smiled again as he lit his pipe,
puffed awhile, listened, and then proffered advice.
“ Get home, sonny; get home, it’s the best place
for you. What’s the matter ?—well, the way I
foot it is you’ve been bilked, and damn-well
bilked, too. He’s a clever cove is the old ’un —I
thought last night you were a pal of his by the
way he spoke. Your clothes were new, were
they! Poor chap!—well, you’ll be all right to-
morrow if you take a packet of salts and get a good
sleep. But steer clear of that chap in future,
sonny—for he’s qji Old Hand.”

according to a Moree (N.S.W.) paper

The Dons of Spain.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Eagle screams at the beck of trade, so Spam,
as the world goes round,
VTust wrestle the right to live or die from the sons
of the land she found ;
h or, as in the days when the buccaneer was
abroad on the Spanish Main,
The national honor is one thing dear to the hearts
of the Dons of Spain.
*>he has slaughtered thousands with fire and sword,
as the Christian world might know ;
We murder millions, but, thank the Lord ! we
only starve ’em slow.
The times have changed since the days of old, but
the same old facts remain -
We fight for Freedom, and God, and gold, and the
Spaniards fight for Spain.
We fought with the strength of the moral right,
and they, as their ships went down,
They only fought with the grit to fight and their
armour to help ’em drown.
It mattered little what chance or hope, for ever
their path was plain,
The Church was the Church, and the Pope the
_ Popie but the Spaniards fought for Spain.
If Providence struck for the honest thief at times
in the battle’s din—
If ever it struck at the hypocrite—well, that’s
where the Turks came in ;
But this remains ere we leave the wise to argue it
through in vain—
There’s something great in the wrong that dies as
the Spaniards die for Spain.
The foes of Spain may be kin to us who are Eng-
lish heart and soul,
And proud of our national righteousness and
proud of the lands we stole ;
But we yet might pause while those brave men
die, and the death-drink pledge again—
For the sake of the past, if you’re doomed, say I,
May your death be a grand one, Spain !
Then here’s to the bravest of Freedom’s foes who
ever with death have stood —
For the sake of the courage to die on steel as their
fathers died on wood ;
And here’s a cheer for the flag unfurled in a hope-
less cause again,
For the sake of the days when the Christian world
was saved by the Dons of Spain.
HENRY LAWSON.
Hiram: “In Melbourne hotel I saw a
wharf-lounger, whose face was strangely familiar.
A beer (with free lunch) at Antony’s brought out
the fact that he was an old acquaintance—a Glas-
fow chemist, who passed exams, for incompetents.
t was an easy matter in those days—an Edin-
burgh candidate would have a Glasgow proxy to
do his papers, and vice versd, not being personally
known to examiners. Pee, .£lO to .£lOO. Result,
wharf-rat.”
Lucretia’s Party.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Lucretia was three years old, and the party had
been gcit up by the neighbors in honor of her birth-
day. Rather an ill-timed compliment, perhaps,
with Mick Doolan, Lucretia’s father, away on the
tramp looking for work, and Kitty, his blue-eyed,
dark-haired wife, getting help from the Ladies’
Benevolent Society in his absence, and, even with
this aid, finding it hard enough to live.
But the neighbors meant .well, and, even if their
intentions had been less kind, Mrs. Doolan was
the last woman in the world to decline a dance ;
so she let her cronies please themselves, and on
the appointed evening made her small preparations
with a light heart. When she had taken her
fringe out of curl-papers and rubbed up her high-
heeled shoes, bought with one of Mick’s rare re-
mittances home, she sat down to await the arrival
of the company. First came Mrs. Bates, -the fat
washerwoman from over the road, bringing her
four youngest children and a concertina to supply
the dance-music. Next to appear was Harry
Brown, a great friend of Mrs. Doolan’s.
Unknown to Kitty, her intimacy with this
young man had been hotly discussed at the last
“ Benevolent meeting.” The President, old Mrs.
Boyce, had set the ball rolling by demanding of
the severe “Relieving Secretary” her reasons for
shutting the door in Mrs. Doolan’s face last time
that plausible pauper called for a grocery-order.
( Miss Snip promptly rejoined, in a pained voice,
“ But, Mrs. Boyce! are you aware that the girl
has a friend —a young man?”
“ Yes, yes, I know all about him ! She’s often
told me how good he is to the children. What’s
he got to do with the matter ? ”
“He’s a—boarder!” faltered the’ secretary,
bashfully.
“Rubbish ! What boarder would be content in
a house where there’s only one bed and one chair
between the poor woman and her three children ?”
“Well—er—he shares whatever—in fact, he
keeps her,” persisted the accuser, forgetting her
blushes.
This statement almost took Mrs. Boyce’s breath
away, but she professed to be unable to believe it
without unmistakable proof, and there the matter
had been allowed to rest until next meeting.
Meanwhile, Kitty, ignorant,of the gathering
storm, welcomed her friends without a misgiving.
There was no furniture to move out of the way ;
the visitors brought their own refr shments with
them, and, beyond boiling the kettle in time for
supper, no responsibility whatever rested on the
hostess.
As soon as enough couples had arrived for the
“ first set ” the initial stiffness began to wear off,
and by the time the following waltz was in full
swing all were enjoying themselves. Mrs. Doolan,
flushed and bright-eyed, forgot her out-at-beel
stockings as she “corkscrewed ”in the middle of
the floor with Harry Brown, whose step, from
much ,pr .cl-.ce,- went beautifully with her own.
The musician, enthroned on the bed, got a wonder-
ful deal of sound out of theconeertina, whileperched
at her side Lucretia, queen of the evening, chimed
in with a penny trumpet, Brown’s birthday gift.
Kitty’s only daughter did not inherit her mother’s
food looks. Her small face, like homely Mick
)oolan’s, seemed mostly mouth, and even the joy
of the party brought no color to her pasty cheeks.
To-night, with her scant lint-white locks over-
shadowed by a huge red top-knot, she looked
even quainter than usual; but as her tiny legs
waved gleefully in time to the music, she was “ a
vision of delight ”to her mother. The latter,
while she danced, gazed so fondly at the little
thing that her partner, following her glance, re-
marked, diplomatically, that “ the nipper was
growing a fine lump of a girl! ”
Suddenly a loud knock was heard. The street-
door opened into the dancing-room, and Mrs.
Doolan, still hanging on Harry’s arm, threw it
wide to admit, as she thought, some tardy guest.
Poor woman ! she turned pale with surprise and
horror when a familiar voice remarked, ironically,
“I hope I don't intrude!” It was Miss Snip !
Pursuing her researches into the Doolan scandal,
she had dropped in on the chance of picking up
evidence, and, as she told a friend afterwards,
“surprised the creature in the very act of giving
a ball.”
Strong in the consciousness of paying the rent,
the newcomer immediately ordered the guests out
of the house, and was promptly obeyed, for most
of those present had their own reasons for wishing
to keep friendly with the “ Relieving Secretary.”
Harry Brown slunk off with the rest. He was
out of work at the time, and if the Doolans
quarrelled with the Society he had no roof to take
them to.
When the house was cleared of strangers, Miss
Snip dealt severely with poor Kitty, and wound
up by striking her name off the relief-list. The
culprit turned a bold face to the enemy and had a
saucy reply ready for every reproach ; but when
at last the secretary left in high wrath, she leant
trembling against the wall, repentant and crest-
fallen enough to have softened the sternest judge.
For a week her children had talked all day and
dreamed all night of “ the party,’ and now when
the cup of bliss was dashed from their very lips,
they threw themselves on their mother howling
dismally. She, poor thing ! had no comfort to
offer them. ihe guests, in spite of their hurried
flight, had not forgotten to take their supper-con-
tributions away with them, and now Mrs. Doolan
remembered with a pang that there was not even a
bit of bread in the house. She had spent her last
sixpence on a few candles for the festival, and
where next day’s food was to come from was a
question she could not answer.
It would be long enough before she dared apply
to the Society again, and after their base desertion
she told herself fiercely she would ask nothing of
her neighbors. Angry with herself and everyone
else, she smacked poor Mick, junior, and his
brother Aloysius into comparative silence, but
could not quieten Lucretia, who only yelled the
louder for the punishment.
Finally the mother burst out crying, too, and
threw herself on the bed, hugging the screaming
baby tightly in her arms, while the boys tucked
themselves in at her feet to sob and snuffle in
chorus till they fell asleep.
That night’s disappointment was only the begin-
ning of Mrs. Doolan s troubles. Before the end
of the week, her three < hildren were down with
the prevalent scarlatina, and, ten days after the
party, Lucretia was dead.
When motherly Mrs. Boyce heard of the baby’s
death, she went to the Doolans’ cottage for the
first time since their disgrace. Kitty received her
cold y, but she kindly invited herself in, and stood
gazing pitifully at the tiny corpse, as it lay on an
empty fruit-case neatly covered with clean news-
papers, for the! bed was occupied by the other chil-
1 F u n ' ,-Heatb sometimes comes as a beautifier, but
baby Dooian, dead, was even less lovely than she
had been in life. There was so little of her that
the small figuredooked as if it had been forcibly
flattened down against the box, and the expression
of the drawn mouth was painfully like the fixed
grin of a dead kitten.
While the visitor bent over the little sleeper,
Kitty stood by, pale and tearless. She was worn-
out with watching and worry, but seemed resent-
ful rather than sad.
“ Meningitis—the doctor calls it—must have
been coming on long before the fever,” she said in
answer to a question. “But she was all right
till her party ; she cried herself sick that night.”
“Has a clergyman been to see you?” asked
Mrs. Boyce, soothingly.
“The Sisters came when they heard she was dead
—see, they put this on her! ” And the mother
showed a rosary of tiny pearl beads round the
little neck. “ She doesn’t look a bit like herself! ”
she went on presently, her heavy eyes fixed wist-
fully on Lucretia’s pinched face. “The Sisters
smoothed her hair down like that. I’d tied it up
with her ribbon same as she always liked it her-
self, but they said it wasn’t suitable—she never
did get anything she wanted.”
The mourner twisted the rejected red bow ner-
vously between her fingers as she talked, her
restless glance wandering from the gay ribbon to
her own high-heeled shoes, lying in the middle of
the floor, just where she had kicked them off when
her feet grew' too tired and swollen to wear them
any longer—then back again to the baby’s face.
Suddenly her mood changed, and she cried as if
her heart were breaking, gasping out between her
sobs _ “I wouldn’t have cared so much, if only
she’d had her party ! ”
ALICE MONTGOMERY.
